What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
The Two Prefaces: Science vs. Book
Author distinguishes between two types of preface and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muqaddimat al-'ilm and muqaddimat al-kitab and evaluate al-Sharif's objection regarding the latter.

Core Idea
The science's preface is a prerequisite; the book's preface is a literary convention—they are not identical.

In this passage, the author clarifies an important terminological distinction that often confuses students: the difference between the preface that belongs to the science itself and the preface that belongs to the book. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting the structure of logic textbooks. The author also defends the legitimacy of calling a section 'preface' against a critique by al-Sharif al-Jurjani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of preface
Locate where the author defines 'muqaddimat al-'ilm' and 'muqaddimat al-kitab'. Notice that the first depends on the science's prerequisites (definition, goal, subject), while the second is a portion of speech placed before the intended content.
2
Extract their definitions
Write down the exact definitions: muqaddimat al-'ilm is that upon which the commencement of insight depends; muqaddimat al-kitab is a section of speech placed before the main content for connection and benefit.
3
Understand the container metaphor
The author says muqaddimat al-'ilm is the container (zarf) for muqaddimat al-kitab. This means the science's preface encompasses the book's preface, not that they are identical.
This avoids the problem of container and contained being the same.
4
Analyze al-Sharif's objection
Al-Sharif claimed 'muqaddimat al-kitab' is a new convention not found in earlier usage. Identify where this objection appears in the passage.
5
Examine the author's refutation
The author counters by noting that authors have always coined terms for sections (e.g., 'namat' in al-Isharat) and that the word 'muqaddima' originally meant 'vanguard', later metaphorically applied. This shows the term is valid.
The author also cites the Kashshaf to support the etymology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله يتبين فيها امور ثلاثة اعلم ان توجيه الظرفية هيهنا كما مر فى توجيه قوله : «القسم الأول فى المنطق و للمصنف هيهنا طريقة أخرى كما يشهد به عبارته في شرحه على التلخيص وهي : ان المقدمة مقدمة العلم وهى التى يتوقف عليها الشروع فى البصيرة كمعرفة حد العلم و غايته و موضوعه و مقدمة الكتاب وهى طائفة من الكلام قدمت امام المقصود لارتباط لها به ونفعها فيه وعلى هذا فيكون مقدمة العلم عنده ظرفاً لمقدمة الكتاب فلا يلزم اتحاد الظرف و المظروف و انما يلزم لو انحصر المقدمة في مقدمة العلم كما هو المشهور لكن افاد الشريف فى حاشيته عليه ان مقدمة الكتاب اصطلاح جديد لا نقل عليه في كلامهم ولا هو مفهوم من اطلاقاتهم. و يرد عليه ان المصنفين اصطلحوا على ان يسموا ما قدموه مقدمة كما يسمون طائفة من كلامهم فناً أو باباً او فصلاً فاطلاق المقدمة على تلك الطائفة المقدمة كاطلاق فن الكتاب و بابه وفصله على ما جعل اجزاء له فكما ان للمصنف ان يغير هذه الاسامى الى اسامى آخر كالنمط و التنبيه والاشارة كما هو دأب الشيخ فى الاشاراته فكذلك يجوز ان يسمى ما قدم امام المقصود بالغرّة كما فعله بعض مشايخنا رضوان الله عليهم فلا ينبغي لاحد ان يقول : ان هذه اصطلاح جديد اذا لم يسم احد الباب بالنمط والمقدمة والغرة لان هذه الامور ليست مضبوطة تحت قاعدة حتى يلزم ذلك ولذلك جرى على ذلك جميع المصنفين مع ان صاحب الكشاف قال فى الفائق: المقدمة الجماعة التي يتقدم الجيش من قدم بمعنى تقدم ثم استعير لاول كل شيء. فقيل مقدمة الكتاب و مقدمة العلم وفتح الدال خلف (عبد الرحيم)
His statement: 'in which three things are clarified': know that the explanation of the circumstantial relation here is as mentioned in explaining his statement: 'the first section in logic'. And the author has another method here, as indicated by his statement in his commentary on al-Talkhis: that the preface is the preface of the science, which is that upon which the commencement of insight depends, like knowing the definition of the science, its goal, and its subject; and the preface of the book, which is a portion of speech placed before the intended subject due to its connection and benefit in it. On this basis, the preface of the science according to him becomes the container for the preface of the book, so the identity of container and contained does not necessarily follow. It would only follow if the preface were restricted to the preface of the science as is well-known. But al-Sharif stated in his hashya that the preface of the book is a new convention not transmitted in their speech nor understood from their usages. And it is objected to him that the authors have conventionally agreed to call what they present a preface, just as they call a portion of their speech a 'fan', a 'bab', or a 'fasl'. So applying 'preface' to that presented portion is like applying 'fan of the book', 'bab', 'fasl' to what they have made parts of it. For just as the author may change these names to other names like 'al-namat', 'al-tanbih', 'al-ishara' as was the practice of the Shaykh in al-Isharat, so too it is permissible to call what is placed before the intended subject 'al-ghurra' as some of our teachers, may God be pleased with them, did. So no one should say that this is a new convention just because no one has called the chapter 'namat', 'muqaddima', or 'ghurra', because these matters are not fixed under a rule that would necessitate that. Therefore all authors have proceeded upon that, especially since the author of al-Kashshaf said in al-Faiq: 'al-muqaddima is the group that precedes the army, from qadima meaning to precede; then it was borrowed for the first of everything.' So it is said 'preface of the book' and 'preface of the science', and opening the dal is a variant (from 'Abd al-Rahim).
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage addresses the meaning of 'Mukaddimah' in the context of the book's opening. The author explains that the word 'in which' (فيها) refers to the preface, but there is a distinction: the 'preface of the science' (muqaddimat al-'ilm) contains essential prerequisites for understanding the science, like its definition, goal, and subject. The 'preface of the book' (muqaddimat al-kitab) is just a section of text placed before the main content for some connection or benefit. The author argues that the preface of the science serves as the container (zarf) for the preface of the book, so they are not identical. However, al-Sharif objected that 'preface of the book' is a new term not found in earlier usage. The author refutes this by noting that authors have always coined terms for sections of their works, just like 'namat' in al-Isharat. Furthermore, the word 'muqaddima' originally meant the vanguard of an army, then metaphorically applied to the first part of anything. Thus, calling a section 'preface' is well-established. This is useful for understanding the nature of the introduction: it is both a structural part of the book and a conceptual prerequisite.

Key Terms
مقدمة العلم
مقدمة الكتاب
ظرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is al-Sharif's objection that 'muqaddimat al-kitab' is a new convention invalid?
A) Because authors have always coined terms for sections and the word has etymological roots.
B) Because muqaddimat al-kitab is identical to muqaddimat al-'ilm.
C) Because al-Sharif misunderstood the container metaphor.
D) Because the preface is only for the science, not the book.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between the science's preface and the book's preface is essential to avoid conflating content and structure in Islamic logical texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a logic textbook and identify one sentence that belongs to muqaddimat al-'ilm and one that belongs to muqaddimat al-kitab. Explain your reasoning based on the definitions in this passage.
?
Write a short paragraph refuting someone who claims that 'muqaddimat al-kitab' is a modern innovation, using the author's arguments from this passage.

Three Excuses for Omitting a Definition Before Division
Commentator gives three excuses for omitting definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three excuses the commentator gives for why the author did not define knowledge before dividing it.

Core Idea
A minimal conception of knowledge suffices for division, much like grammarians dividing the excluded without defining it.

In logical and legal texts, it is common to define a term before dividing it into types. However, the author of this text on logic skipped the definition of knowledge and went straight to division. The commentator steps in to defend him with three justifications. Understanding these excuses will help you appreciate when a definition can be omitted and why a minimal understanding of a term is sometimes enough for scholarly discussion.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three excuses
Find the passage where the commentator says 'the commentator apologized for him with three aspects'. These are the three excuses. Read each one carefully.
The excuses are explicitly numbered in the Arabic: 'al-awwal', 'al-thānī', 'al-thālith'.
2
Paraphrase each excuse
For each excuse, restate it in your own words. The first: a certain conception suffices, like grammarians. The second: the definition is so famous it can be omitted for brevity. The third: knowledge is self-evident like internal sensations.
3
Evaluate the strength of each excuse
The commentator later critiques the arguments for the third excuse, but does he reject the excuse itself? Notice that he upholds the third excuse's conclusion but refines the reasoning. The first two excuses stand without direct refutation. Assess which excuse is most compelling from a logical standpoint.
Think about whether 'famous definition' is a valid reason in rigorous scholarship.
4
Apply the concept to a parallel case
Consider the example of the grammarians dividing 'al-mustathnā' (the excluded) without defining it. Why was that acceptable? Because the term is familiar from everyday language. Similarly, 'knowledge' is a common term. This shows that in some contexts, a technical definition is not necessary before analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله والمصنف لم يتعرض بتعريفه : لما ترك المصنف ما هو المناسب من تعريف العلم قبل التقسيم، ضرورة ان التقسيم حكم من أحكامه وهى لا يتحقق الا بعد تحقق الشيء كما هو ظاهر، اعتذر المحشى (ره) عنه بثلاثة وجوه الاول : انه يكفى التصور بوجه ما فى مقام التقسيم، يعنى انه يتبادر منه عند الاطلاق انه ما يطلق عليه في اصطلاحهم العلم و هذا القدر من التصور يكفى فى مقام التقسيم كما هو ظاهر و ذلك، كما ان التحويين قسموا المستثنى الى المتصل و الى المنفصل من غير ان يعرفوه اولاً اعتماداً على ذلك. الثاني: انه لما كان تعريف العلم مشهوراً مستفيضاً اكتفى به عن ذكر تعريفه روماً للاختصار. الثالث ان العلم بديهى التصور كما نقل عن الامام الرازى وذلك ، لانه من الكيفيات الوجدانية التي يجدها كل من رجع الى نفسه كالجوع والعطش والرى والشبع. وربما يستدل ايضاً بان كل احد من العقلاء الذين لم يتمارسوا اكتساب التصورات من الحدود والرسوم اصلاً ولا عرفوا كيفيته، إذا استفهم عنهم هل تعلمون الشيء الفلانى ام لا؟ اختاروا في الجواب احد الامرين قطعاً وما ذلك الا لعلمهم بمفهوم السؤال الذى من جملته العلم، ولا يحقى ان واحداً منهما لا يثبت المدعى. اما الأول، فظاهر فان غاية ما يستلزمه انه لا يحتاج الى التعريف المعنوى و اما احتياجه الى التعريف اللفظي و تعيين مسماه من بين المعافي المخزونة في الذهن فياق على حاله وكذلك حال جميع الوجدانيات لظهور ان البداهة انما هى بالنسبة الى ادراك مصداق العلم و مفهومه لا الى ادراك ان هذا المعنى موضوع له للفظ العلم كما هو غير خفى على من له ذوق سليم. و اما الثانى فلان ما ذكر من اختيار الجواب عند السؤال ان كان بالنسبة الى من هو عالم بالوضع، فمسلم لكن المعتبر في الحدود حال غير العالمين بالوضع، ولو سلم فجميع المحدودات بالنسبة الى العالم بالوضع معلوم لا يحتاج الى التعريف فلا وجه لتخصيص ذلك بالعلم، وان كان بالنسبة الى من هو غير عالم بالوضع، فلا نسلم انه يختار في الجواب احد الامرين فان الجواب فرع الفهم وغير العالم بالوضع لا يفهم من السؤال شيئاً حتى يصح منه الجواب و كانه لهذا نسب المحشى هذا الوجه الى القيل تمريضاً له، هذا ما خطر ببالى اولاً، والحق ان الاستدلال بهذين الوجهين لاثبات بداهة مفهوم العلم و عدم احتياجه الى التعريف الحقيقى لا الى التعريف اللفظى وح لا يرد شيء مما ذكر عليه. اما الاول، فظاهر، كما اشير اليه هناك. و اما الثانى، فبانا نختار الشق الأول من شقى الترديد. ولا يخفى انه ليس كلها علم ما وضع له هو يكون مفهومه الحقيقى معلوماً بالنسبة الى العالم بالوضع مطلقاً و ان كان من العوام الغير الممارسين لطرق الاكتساب والا لما احتيج إلى معرفة احكام المعرف والقول الشارح كما هو ظاهر. وفيه أن ذلك مسلم لكن اختيار احد الامرين فى الجواب لايدل على معلومية مفهومه الحقيق لصحة الجواب بمعرفة ما وضع له اللفظ خاصة كما هو ظاهر فتأمل.
His statement: 'And the author did not address its definition' because the author left what is appropriate of defining knowledge before division, since division is one of its rulings and rulings are only realized after the thing is realized, as is apparent. The commentator apologized for him with three aspects: First: that a certain conception suffices in the context of division, meaning that the term 'knowledge' immediately suggests what is called knowledge in their terminology, and this amount of conception suffices in the context of division, as is apparent. This is just as the grammarians divided the excluded (al-mustathnā) into continuous and discontinuous without first defining it, relying on that. Second: because the definition of knowledge was well-known and widespread, he sufficed with that instead of mentioning its definition, seeking brevity. Third: that knowledge is self-evident in conception, as transmitted from Imam al-Razi, because it is among the found qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-wijdāniyya) that everyone finds in themselves, like hunger, thirst, satiety, and fullness. And perhaps it is also argued that every one of the rational people who have never practiced acquiring conceptions from definitions and descriptions at all, and do not know the manner of that, when asked 'Do you know such-and-such?', they choose one of the two answers decisively, and that is only because of their knowledge of the meaning of the question, which includes 'knowledge'. And it is not hidden that neither of these two [arguments] proves the claim. As for the first, it is apparent, for its utmost entailment is that it does not need a semantic definition, but its need for a verbal definition and specifying its referent among the meanings stored in the mind remains as it is. Similarly for all found qualities, because it is apparent that self-evidence is only relative to perceiving the instance of knowledge and its concept, not to perceiving that this meaning is the referent of the word 'knowledge', as is not hidden from one of sound taste. As for the second, because what was mentioned of choosing the answer when asked: if it is relative to one who knows the linguistic usage, it is granted, but what is considered in definitions is the state of those who do not know the usage. Even if granted, all defined things are known to the one who knows the usage and do not need definition, so there is no reason to specify that with knowledge. If it is relative to one who does not know the usage, then we do not grant that he chooses one of the two answers, for the answer depends on understanding, and one who does not know the usage does not understand anything from the question such that a correct answer could be given from him. It is as if for this reason the commentator attributed this aspect to 'qīl' (it is said) to indicate its weakness. This occurred to me first. The truth is that the inference using these two aspects is to prove the self-evidence of the concept of knowledge and its not needing a real definition, not a verbal definition; thus nothing of what was mentioned applies to it. As for the first, it is apparent, as indicated there. As for the second, we choose the first of the two alternatives. And it is not hidden that not everyone who knows what the word is used for has the true concept known relative to the knower of the usage absolutely, even if he is from the common folk not practiced in the methods of acquisition; otherwise there would be no need to know the rulings of the definer and the explanatory statement, as is apparent. But in it [it can be said] that this is granted, but the choice of one of the two answers in the response does not indicate the knowledge of its true concept, because a correct answer is possible by merely knowing what the word is used for, as is apparent. So reflect.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage discusses why the author (al-muṣannif) did not define knowledge before dividing it. The commentator (al-muḥashshī) offers three excuses. First, a minimal conception of knowledge is sufficient for division, just as grammarians divide ‘excluded’ without defining it. Second, the definition is so well-known that brevity is achieved by omitting it. Third, knowledge is self-evident (badīhī al-taṣawwur), as it is a found quality (kayfiyya wijdāniyya) like hunger. Then two arguments are presented to prove self-evidence: (1) ordinary people can answer yes/no to 'do you know?' without training; (2) the same. The commentator critiques these arguments: the first only shows no need for semantic definition but still needs verbal definition; the second fails because it assumes the question is understood – if the person knows the linguistic usage, all defined things are known, not just knowledge; if they don't, they cannot even understand the question. The commentator then clarifies that the true intention of these arguments is to prove self-evidence of the concept, not to dispense with verbal definition. Finally, a subtle point: knowing the word usage does not necessarily imply knowledge of the true concept. The passage ends with 'so reflect'.

Key Terms
بديهي التصور
الوجدانيات

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three excuses the commentator gives for not defining knowledge before division?
A) A minimal conception of knowledge suffices for division.
B) The definition of knowledge is so famous that mentioning it would be redundant.
C) Knowledge is a complex concept that requires extensive definition.
D) Knowledge is self-evident, like internal sensations.

Key Takeaway
The three excuses show that a definition can be omitted when a minimal conception suffices, the definition is well-known, or the concept is self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Self-Evidence of Knowledge: Arguments and Critiques
Commentator critiques and refines arguments for self-evidence of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the arguments for the self-evidence of the concept of knowledge and distinguish between needing a real definition vs. a verbal definition.

Core Idea
Knowledge is self-evident in conception, but still requires a verbal definition to fix its referent.

The commentator presents a deeper discussion on whether the concept of knowledge is self-evident (badīhī al-taṣawwur). He gives two arguments to support this claim and then carefully refutes them as originally presented, before offering a refined interpretation. This lesson will guide you through his logical moves, which are essential for understanding how definition works in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the two arguments
Find where the commentator says 'knowledge is self-evident in conception' and then 'it is also argued' (wa-rubbamā yustadallu). The two arguments are: (1) knowledge is a found quality like hunger, (2) people can answer yes/no to 'Do you know?' without training.
The found qualities argument is part of the third excuse; the second argument is additional.
2
Analyze the commentator's initial critique
The commentator says 'neither of these two proves the claim'. For the first argument (found qualities), he says it only shows no need for semantic definition (taʿrīf maʿnawī), but still needs verbal definition (taʿrīf lafẓī). For the second argument, he raises a dilemma: if the respondent knows the linguistic usage, then all defined things are known, so it's not special to knowledge; if they don't know the usage, they cannot even understand the question.
The key distinction is between real definition (what is the thing) and verbal definition (what does the word mean).
3
Understand the refined resolution
The commentator then says 'the truth is that the inference using these two aspects is to prove the self-evidence of the concept of knowledge and its not needing a real definition, not a verbal definition'. So the arguments are valid for their intended purpose, but they were misinterpreted. He then chooses the first horn of the dilemma for the second argument and defends it: even common people who know the usage have a concept sufficient for answering, though they may not have a true definition.
This is a subtle point: knowing the word's referent does not mean having a scientific definition.
4
Apply the distinction to a new case
Consider the concept of 'time' (zamān). Many people can say whether an event happened before or after another, but they cannot define time philosophically. Does this prove that the concept of time is self-evident? Justify your answer using the commentator's framework.
Think about what kind of definition (real vs. verbal) is being argued about.

Source Text

الاول : انه يكفى التصور بوجه ما فى مقام التقسيم، يعنى انه يتبادر منه عند الاطلاق انه ما يطلق عليه في اصطلاحهم العلم و هذا القدر من التصور يكفى فى مقام التقسيم كما هو ظاهر و ذلك، كما ان التحويين قسموا المستثنى الى المتصل و الى المنفصل من غير ان يعرفوه اولاً اعتماداً على ذلك. الثاني: انه لما كان تعريف العلم مشهوراً مستفيضاً اكتفى به عن ذكر تعريفه روماً للاختصار. الثالث ان العلم بديهى التصور كما نقل عن الامام الرازى وذلك ، لانه من الكيفيات الوجدانية التي يجدها كل من رجع الى نفسه كالجوع والعطش والرى والشبع. وربما يستدل ايضاً بان كل احد من العقلاء الذين لم يتمارسوا اكتساب التصورات من الحدود والرسوم اصلاً ولا عرفوا كيفيته، إذا استفهم عنهم هل تعلمون الشيء الفلانى ام لا؟ اختاروا في الجواب احد الامرين قطعاً وما ذلك الا لعلمهم بمفهوم السؤال الذى من جملته العلم، ولا يحقى ان واحداً منهما لا يثبت المدعى. اما الأول، فظاهر فان غاية ما يستلزمه انه لا يحتاج الى التعريف المعنوى و اما احتياجه الى التعريف اللفظي و تعيين مسماه من بين المعافي المخزونة في الذهن فياق على حاله وكذلك حال جميع الوجدانيات لظهور ان البداهة انما هى بالنسبة الى ادراك مصداق العلم و مفهومه لا الى ادراك ان هذا المعنى موضوع له للفظ العلم كما هو غير خفى على من له ذوق سليم. و اما الثانى فلان ما ذكر من اختيار الجواب عند السؤال ان كان بالنسبة الى من هو عالم بالوضع، فمسلم لكن المعتبر في الحدود حال غير العالمين بالوضع، ولو سلم فجميع المحدودات بالنسبة الى العالم بالوضع معلوم لا يحتاج الى التعريف فلا وجه لتخصيص ذلك بالعلم، وان كان بالنسبة الى من هو غير عالم بالوضع، فلا نسلم انه يختار في الجواب احد الامرين فان الجواب فرع الفهم وغير العالم بالوضع لا يفهم من السؤال شيئاً حتى يصح منه الجواب و كانه لهذا نسب المحشى هذا الوجه الى القيل تمريضاً له، هذا ما خطر ببالى اولاً، والحق ان الاستدلال بهذين الوجهين لاثبات بداهة مفهوم العلم و عدم احتياجه الى التعريف الحقيقى لا الى التعريف اللفظى وح لا يرد شيء مما ذكر عليه. اما الاول، فظاهر، كما اشير اليه هناك. و اما الثانى، فبانا نختار الشق الأول من شقى الترديد. ولا يخفى انه ليس كلها علم ما وضع له هو يكون مفهومه الحقيقى معلوماً بالنسبة الى العالم بالوضع مطلقاً و ان كان من العوام الغير الممارسين لطرق الاكتساب والا لما احتيج إلى معرفة احكام المعرف والقول الشارح كما هو ظاهر. وفيه أن ذلك مسلم لكن اختيار احد الامرين فى الجواب لايدل على معلومية مفهومه الحقيق لصحة الجواب بمعرفة ما وضع له اللفظ خاصة كما هو ظاهر فتأمل.
His statement: 'And the author did not address its definition' because the author left what is appropriate of defining knowledge before division, since division is one of its rulings and rulings are only realized after the thing is realized, as is apparent. The commentator apologized for him with three aspects: First: that a certain conception suffices in the context of division, meaning that the term 'knowledge' immediately suggests what is called knowledge in their terminology, and this amount of conception suffices in the context of division, as is apparent. This is just as the grammarians divided the excluded (al-mustathnā) into continuous and discontinuous without first defining it, relying on that. Second: because the definition of knowledge was well-known and widespread, he sufficed with that instead of mentioning its definition, seeking brevity. Third: that knowledge is self-evident in conception, as transmitted from Imam al-Razi, because it is among the found qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-wijdāniyya) that everyone finds in themselves, like hunger, thirst, satiety, and fullness. And perhaps it is also argued that every one of the rational people who have never practiced acquiring conceptions from definitions and descriptions at all, and do not know the manner of that, when asked 'Do you know such-and-such?', they choose one of the two answers decisively, and that is only because of their knowledge of the meaning of the question, which includes 'knowledge'. And it is not hidden that neither of these two [arguments] proves the claim. As for the first, it is apparent, for its utmost entailment is that it does not need a semantic definition, but its need for a verbal definition and specifying its referent among the meanings stored in the mind remains as it is. Similarly for all found qualities, because it is apparent that self-evidence is only relative to perceiving the instance of knowledge and its concept, not to perceiving that this meaning is the referent of the word 'knowledge', as is not hidden from one of sound taste. As for the second, because what was mentioned of choosing the answer when asked: if it is relative to one who knows the linguistic usage, it is granted, but what is considered in definitions is the state of those who do not know the usage. Even if granted, all defined things are known to the one who knows the usage and do not need definition, so there is no reason to specify that with knowledge. If it is relative to one who does not know the usage, then we do not grant that he chooses one of the two answers, for the answer depends on understanding, and one who does not know the usage does not understand anything from the question such that a correct answer could be given from him. It is as if for this reason the commentator attributed this aspect to 'qīl' (it is said) to indicate its weakness. This occurred to me first. The truth is that the inference using these two aspects is to prove the self-evidence of the concept of knowledge and its not needing a real definition, not a verbal definition; thus nothing of what was mentioned applies to it. As for the first, it is apparent, as indicated there. As for the second, we choose the first of the two alternatives. And it is not hidden that not everyone who knows what the word is used for has the true concept known relative to the knower of the usage absolutely, even if he is from the common folk not practiced in the methods of acquisition; otherwise there would be no need to know the rulings of the definer and the explanatory statement, as is apparent. But in it [it can be said] that this is granted, but the choice of one of the two answers in the response does not indicate the knowledge of its true concept, because a correct answer is possible by merely knowing what the word is used for, as is apparent. So reflect.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage discusses why the author (al-muṣannif) did not define knowledge before dividing it. The commentator (al-muḥashshī) offers three excuses. First, a minimal conception of knowledge is sufficient for division, just as grammarians divide ‘excluded’ without defining it. Second, the definition is so well-known that brevity is achieved by omitting it. Third, knowledge is self-evident (badīhī al-taṣawwur), as it is a found quality (kayfiyya wijdāniyya) like hunger. Then two arguments are presented to prove self-evidence: (1) ordinary people can answer yes/no to 'do you know?' without training; (2) the same. The commentator critiques these arguments: the first only shows no need for semantic definition but still needs verbal definition; the second fails because it assumes the question is understood – if the person knows the linguistic usage, all defined things are known, not just knowledge; if they don't, they cannot even understand the question. The commentator then clarifies that the true intention of these arguments is to prove self-evidence of the concept, not to dispense with verbal definition. Finally, a subtle point: knowing the word usage does not necessarily imply knowledge of the true concept. The passage ends with 'so reflect'.

Key Terms

الوجدانيات

A) Both arguments are completely false and should be rejected.
B) The arguments prove that knowledge needs neither real nor verbal definition.
C) The arguments prove self-evidence of the concept, but only dispense with real definition, not verbal definition.
D) Only the first argument is valid; the second is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The self-evidence of a concept does not eliminate the need for a verbal definition to fix its referent; this distinction is crucial for understanding definition in Islamic logic.

Distinguishing Tasdiq and Tasawwur with Correction of Errors
Author defines tasdiq and tasawwur and critiques two errors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish tasdiq from tasawwur based on the presence of idh'ān, and identify two common errors in classifying propositions as tasawwur.

Core Idea
Tasdiq requires the soul's submission; without it, it is not knowledge.

The author defines tasdiq and tasawwur as two modes of disclosing reality. He then corrects two mistakes made by a previous commentator: counting performative sentences and non-assentive declarative propositions as tasawwur. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying these categories in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modes of disclosure
Reality is disclosed either in a judgmental proposition (nisba ḥukmiyya) between subject and predicate, or in individual parts, dispersed particulars, or incomplete relations. The former can lead to tasdiq if the soul submits; the latter leads to tasawwur.
The judgmental proposition can be negative or affirmative.
2
Distinguish between submission and non-submission
When the soul submits (idh'ān) to a judgmental proposition, it is tasdiq. If it apprehends the proposition without submission (e.g., imagining, doubting, or being deluded), it is not tasdiq—it is defiance or mere agitation.
The author calls non-assentive apprehension 'tamarrud wa ṭughyān' (defiance and transgression).
3
Identify what does NOT count as tasawwur
The commentator erroneously included performative propositions (inshā'iyyāt) and complete declarative propositions (nisba tāmma khabariyya) apprehended without assent. Performative propositions are creations, not disclosures, so they cannot be tasawwur. Non-assentive declarative propositions are agitations of the soul hidden from reality.
The author insists that every tasawwur is a disclosure of reality (inkishāf al-wāqi').
4
Understand the nature of tasdiq
Tasdiq is a simple state of the soul—submission and acknowledgement—not a composite of subject, predicate, and relation. The author dismisses the composite view as utterly fallacious.
The composite view would make tasdiq a 'totality' of parts, which the author rejects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲۰) قوله العلم ان كان اذعاناً بالنسبة فتصديق والاقتصور يعنى ان الواقع تارة ينكشف عن نسبة حكمية سالبة او موجبة بين محمول و موضوع فذلك حق لانه واقعى واذا حصل للنفس خضوع و اعتراف بتلك النسبة الحكمية فذلك تصديق. واذالم يحصل اعتراف و خضوع مع حصول الانكشاف فتمرد وطغيان، واخرى ينكشف عن جزئى منفرد او جزئيات منشتتة او نسب ناقصة مثل زيد او عمرو و خالد و بكر او غلام زيد فذلك يقال له: تصور اى استحضار لصور هذه الاشياء. وعد الشارح النسب الانشائية من التصورات غلط لان الانشاء ايجاد وخلق فلا واقع له حتى ينكشف. وكذلك عده النسب التامة الخبرية المدركة بادراك غير اذعانى كما في صور التخييل والشك والوهم من التصورات التي هي من انكشافات الواقع باعتباره ان كل تصور فهو علم و كل علم فهو انكشاف حتماً، غلط ايضاً. نعم هذه الامور من اختلاجات النفس المحجوبة عن الواقع كما لا يخفى. وبناء على ما عرفت، فخضوع النفس و تصديقها بما تذعن و تصدق به من انكشافات الواقع، من حالات النفس وليس بامر مركب من موضوع ومحصول و نسبة سالبة أو موجبة و مدعي ان التصديق هو مجموع هذه الاشياء لم يعرف مقال نفسه ولم يحط خبراً بهوية قوله فانه ساقط للغاية (التقريب ص (۱۵)
(20) His statement: 'Knowledge, if it is an act of assent regarding a relation, then it is tasdiq; and if it is a mere conception,' meaning that reality is sometimes disclosed regarding a judgmental proposition, negative or affirmative, between a predicate and a subject; that is true because it is real. When the soul obtains submission and acknowledgement of that judgmental proposition, then that is tasdiq. If no acknowledgement and submission occur despite the disclosure, then it is defiance and transgression. And sometimes reality is disclosed regarding an individual part or dispersed particulars or incomplete relations, like Zayd, ʿAmr, Khālid, Bakr, or the slave of Zayd; that is called taṣawwur, i.e., the summoning of the images of these things. The commentator's counting of the construct propositions among taṣawwurāt is a mistake, because inshāʾ is creation and invention and has no reality to be disclosed. Likewise, his counting of complete assertive propositions apprehended by non-assentive apprehension, as in cases of imagining, doubt, and illusion, among taṣawwurāt that are among disclosures of reality, considering that every taṣawwur is knowledge and every knowledge is necessarily disclosure, is also a mistake. Indeed, these things are among the agitations of the soul hidden from reality, as is not hidden. And based on what you have known, the soul's submission and its assenting to what it yields and believes from disclosures of reality are among the states of the soul, not something composed of a subject, predicate, and negative or affirmative relation. And he who claims that tasdiq is the totality of these things does not know his own discourse and has not encompassed the identity of his statement, for it is utterly fallacious. (al-Taqrib, p. 15)
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the definition of 'ilm from the main text. It first distinguishes between tasdiq and tasawwur: tasdiq occurs when the soul submits to a judgmental proposition (e.g., 'Zayd is sitting'), while tasawwur is mere conception without judgment (e.g., the concept of Zayd). The author then critiques two errors made by the previous commentator. First, including performative (inshā'ī) sentences like commands among tasawwurāt is a mistake because performatives have no truth value—they are creations, not disclosures of reality. Second, including complete assertive propositions apprehended without assent (e.g., imagining or doubting 'Zayd is sitting' without believing it) as tasawwurāt is also wrong because such apprehensions are not genuine disclosures of reality; they are agitations of the soul. The correct view is that tasdiq is a simple state of the soul (submission and acknowledgement), not a composite of subject, predicate, and relation. The passage ends by dismissing the composite view as utterly fallacious.

C) They are composed of parts.
D) They are not propositions.

Key Takeaway
Tasdiq is the soul's submission to a judgmental disclosure; tasawwur is mere conception without submission, and not every proposition qualifies as tasawwur.

The Reality of Tasdiq: Three Views
Author summarizes three schools on the reality of tasdiq and states his choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three main views on the reality of tasdiq (assent) and identify the author's chosen position.

Core Idea
Tasdiq is either identical to judgment, a composite including it, or a composite where judgment is an accident.

In Islamic logic, understanding the nature of tasdiq (assent) is crucial for grasping how propositions are affirmed. The scholars differed on its reality, leading to three distinct views. This lesson will unpack those views and show which one the author of the matn adopted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three schools
Find the names of the three groups: the philosophers (al-Ḥukamā'), Imam al-Rāzī and his followers, and Ṣāḥib al-Kashf and his followers.
2
Extract the components of tasdiq for each view
For each school, note what tasdiq is composed of. The first view says it is simple (just the act of assent/judgment). The second says it is a composite of four things: conception of subject, conception of predicate, judgmental relation, and judgment. The third says it is a composite of the first three, with judgment being an accident.
3
Clarify the role of judgment (ḥukm)
For each view, determine whether judgment is identical to tasdiq, a part of it, or external to it. The text explicitly says: 'judgment according to the first is itself tasdiq; according to the second it is a part; according to the third it is external to it and accidental to it.'
4
Identify the author's choice
The passage states: 'The author chose the first doctrine.' Look for the phrase 'wa-l-muṣannif ikhtāra al-madhhab al-awwal' and the supporting quote from the matn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲۲) اعلم أنهم اختلفوا في حقيقة التصديق فقال الحكماء: إنه نفس الاذعان والحكم. والامام الرازي و من تبعه انه المركب من التصورات الثلاث والحكم، بمعنى انه عبارة عن المجموع المركب من الامور الاربعة تصور المحكوم عليه و به والنسبة الحكمية بينهما والاذعان. هي وصاحب الكشف و متابعوه انه المركب من الامور الثلاث الاول من حيث ان الحكم عارض لها وهو المشهور بالمذهب المستحدث. والحكم على الاول نفس التصديق وعلى الثاني جزئه وعلى الثالث خارج عنه عارض له. والمصنف اختار المذهب الأول حيث قال: «العلم ان كان اذعاناً للنسبة فتصديق». وهيهنا زيادة كلام لا يليق بذلك المختصر فليطلب من المطولات. (محمد علی)
(22) Know that they differed regarding the reality of taṣdīq. The philosophers said: It is the very act of assent and judgment. Imam al-Rāzī and those who followed him: It is the composite of the three conceptions and the judgment, meaning it is the total composite of four things: the conception of the subject of judgment, the conception of the thing judged by, the judgmental relation between them, and the judgment. And Ṣāḥib al-Kashf and those who followed him: It is the composite of the first three things in that the judgment is an accident thereof. This is known as the renewed doctrine. The judgment according to the first is itself taṣdīq; according to the second it is a part; according to the third it is external to it and accidental to it. The author chose the first doctrine, saying: 'Knowledge, if it is an act of assent regarding a relation, then it is taṣdīq.' There is further discussion here not suitable for this abridgment, so let it be sought from the detailed works.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents the three main views on the reality of tasdiq (assent). The first view, from the philosophers (al-Hukamā'), is that tasdiq is identical to the act of assent and judgment—it is simple. The second view, from Imam al-Rāzī and his followers, is that tasdiq is a composite of four elements: the conception of the subject, the conception of the predicate, the judgmental relation between them, and the judgment. The third view, from Ṣāḥib al-Kashf (the author of al-Kashshāf? but likely a logician) and his followers, is that tasdiq is a composite of the first three elements only, with judgment being an accidental attribute. The passage then clarifies the role of judgment in each view: in the first, judgment is tasdiq itself; in the second, it is a part; in the third, it is an accident. The author of the main text chose the first view. The passage ends by noting that further discussion is beyond the scope of this abridgment.

Key Takeaway

Two Views on Tasdiq: Simple vs. Composite
Explaining the two conflicting definitions of tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Hukamā' and Rāzian definitions of tasdiq and identify which is simple and which composite.

Core Idea
Tasdiq is either simple perception with assent or a composite of four elements — the difference matters for how we acquire knowledge.

In this passage, the author contrasts two major definitions of tasdiq: the philosophers' (Hukamā') view and that of Imam al-Rāzī. Understanding this distinction is crucial because it affects how we classify knowledge and its acquisition methods. Let's break down both definitions and see why one is considered simple and the other composite.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Locate the descriptions of the Hukamā' view and al-Rāzī's view in the Arabic text. Note that the Hukamā' view is introduced first, then al-Rāzī's.
2
Extract the definition elements
For the Hukamā': tasdiq is perception (idrāk) in the mode of assent (idh'ān) related to a complete declarative proposition. For al-Rāzī: it is a composite of four things: conception of subject, conception of predicate, the relation between them, and the judgment.
The commentator notes that the relation in al-Rāzī's view is omitted here for brevity.
3
Analyze simplicity vs. compositeness
The Hukamā' view is described as 'simple' (basīṭ) but conditioned by three things (subject, predicate, relation). Al-Rāzī's view is explicitly 'composite' (murakkab). Understand that simplicity here means it is one type of perception, not an aggregate of multiple cognitive acts.
4
Determine the type of difference
The Hukamā' assert that tasdiq differs from tasawwur in essence (bi-al-dhāt), not merely in the object (bi-al-muta'alliq). This means they are two fundamentally different kinds of perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲۳) قوله فقد اختار المصنف مذهب الحكماء : اعلم ان هيهنا مقامان : الأول في التصديق وقد اختلف في حقيقته ما هي ؟ فذهب الحكماء الى انه عبارة عن الادراك على وجه الاذعان المتعلق بالنسبة التامة الخبرية فهو نوع آخر من الادراك مغاير للتصور بالذات لا بالمتعلق. و ذهب الامام الرازي و متابعوه الى انه عبارة عن مجموع امور اربعة هی تصور محکوم علیه، و به والنسبة بينهما، والحكم. وعدم تعرض المحشى للنسبة اما لان مقصوده هيهنا ليس تحقيق مذهب الامام وتفصيله بل بيان ما يميزه عن مذهب الحكماء او لما سيجيء. والفرق بين المذهبين انه على الاول بسيط مشروط في تحققه امور ثلاث وعلى الثانى مركب و ما اصطلاح عليه الحكماء راجح لانه موافق لما هو غرضهم من تقسيم العلم الى هذين القسمين، لأنهم انما قسموا العلم الى هذين القسمين يمتاز كل منهما بطريق من طرق الاكتساب اذكان بيانها على الوجه الجزئي متعذراً لكثرتها وعدم انضباطها لكن لما كانت مع تلك الكثرة راجعة الى نوعين فاراد وابيانها على الوجه
(23) His statement: 'For the author chose the doctrine of the philosophers.' Know that there are two stations. The first is concerning taṣdīq: they differed as to what is its reality. The philosophers held that it is perception in the mode of assent related to the complete declarative proposition; it is another type of perception, different from taṣawwur by essence not by what it is related to. Imam al-Rāzī and his followers held that it is a composite of four things: the conception of the subject of judgment, the conception of the predicate, the relation between them, and the judgment. The commentator's not addressing the relation is either because his aim here is not to investigate the doctrine of the Imam in detail but rather to clarify what distinguishes it from the doctrine of the philosophers, or because it will come later. The difference between the two doctrines is that according to the first, it is simple whose actualization is conditioned by three things; according to the second, it is composite. And what the philosophers have coined is preferable because it agrees with their purpose in dividing knowledge into these two divisions. For they divided knowledge into these two divisions, each distinguished by a method of acquisition, since their explanation in particular detail was difficult due to their abundance and lack of regulation, but when despite that abundance they reduce to two types, they intended to explain them in a general way.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the discussion on the reality of tasdiq, specifically explaining why the author preferred the Hukamā' view. It distinguishes two stations (maqāmāt): the first is about tasdiq itself. The Hukamā' view is that tasdiq is a kind of perception (idrāk) characterized by assent (idh'ān) and directed to a complete declarative proposition. This perception is different in essence (dhāt) from tasawwur, not just in the object. Al-Rāzī's view, on the other hand, makes tasdiq a composite of four elements. The commentator (al-muḥashshī) omitted the relation in al-Rāzī's view either for brevity or because it will be discussed later. The passage then argues for the preferability of the Hukamā' view: it is simple (basīṭ) although conditioned by three things (the subject, predicate, and relation), while al-Rāzī's is composite. The Hukamā' view aligns with their overall project of dividing knowledge into two types (tasawwur and tasdiq) that correspond to two distinct methods of acquisition. The passage is cut off, but the thrust is that the simple view better serves this division.

Why the Philosophers' View on Tasdiq is Preferable
Arguing for the superiority of the philosophers' definition of tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical reason why the philosophers' definition of tasdiq is preferable to al-Rāzī's, based on the division of knowledge.

Core Idea
The simple view of tasdiq supports the division of knowledge into two acquisition methods.

The author not only presents two definitions of tasdiq but also argues for the superiority of the Hukamā' view. The key reason is that it aligns with the overarching goal of dividing knowledge into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq), each with a distinct method of acquisition. Let's unpack this argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of the division of knowledge
The passage states that the philosophers divided knowledge into two types (tasawwur and tasdiq) based on their methods of acquisition. This division is fundamental because it simplifies the multitude of individual acquisition methods into two general categories.
2
Locate the preference argument
Find the line: 'ما اصطلاح عليه الحكماء راجح لانه موافق لما هو غرضهم من تقسيم العلم الى هذين القسمين' (what the philosophers coined is preferable because it agrees with their purpose in dividing knowledge into these two divisions).
3
Understand the reasoning
The Hukamā' definition of tasdiq as a simple perception with assent aligns with the binary division. Since tasdiq is a single type of perception, it naturally corresponds to one of the two methods. In contrast, al-Rāzī's composite definition would complicate this neat division, potentially mixing elements from both categories.
4
Contrast with the alternative
If tasdiq were composite (as per Rāzī), it would be less clear which acquisition method applies. The simple view maintains a clean epistemological boundary between conception and assent.

The Proposition: Three or Four Parts? A Dialectical Analysis
Commentators debate whether the proposition has three or four components and justify the author's choice of the ancients' view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three-part and four-part definitions of a proposition (qadiyya) and explain the ancient philosophers' position that assent (tasdiq) is directed at the relation itself, not at its occurrence.

Core Idea
The proposition has three parts; the fourth 'occurrence' is a redundant phantom.

The commentators debate a subtle but crucial point: does a proposition (qadiyya) consist of three parts (subject, predicate, relation) or four (adding the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation)? This debate directly affects how we understand the act of assent (tasdiq). In this lesson, you will follow the scholastic arguments as commentators defend the author's choice of the ancient view, and you will see how a later objection tries to undermine that choice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
In the passage, locate the statements defining the ancients (al-qudama') and later scholars (al-muta'akhirūn). Note that the ancients say the proposition has three parts (subject, predicate, relation), while the later say it has four (adding occurrence/non-occurrence).
This distinction is the foundation of the entire debate.
2
Extract the definition of assent (tasdiq)
The passage clarifies that both sides agree tasdiq is simple judgment/conviction, not composite. How does the choice between three and four parts relate to the object of tasdiq? According to the author (and ancients), tasdiq targets the relation itself, not its occurrence.
Note the shift: the object of assent is the relation, not an extra component.
3
Analyze the critique from al-Taqrib
Comment (30) from al-Taqrib argues that 'occurrence' cannot be a separate part: whether it means verbal affirmation/negation or real existence, it is not additional to the relation. Summarize this objection in your own words.
4
Examine the defense in comment (31)
Muhammad Ali refutes the critique by pointing to the author's later explicit statement on tripartite division. How does this seal the argument? What is the role of the Quranic examples (e.g., 'garments that protect you from the heat')?
The defense uses the consistency of the matn to reject the objection.
5
Synthesize: why this debate matters
Connect to a real example from Islamic theology: the proposition 'The world is temporally originated.' In the three-part view, assent is to the relation between the world and temporality. In the four-part view, one would add an extra step of assenting to the 'occurrence' of that relation. Which view is more parsimonious?
This demonstrates how logical debates affect actual theological knowledge.

ومحط اذعان النفس بالضرورة هو النسبة التي انكشف عنها الواقع بايجاب او بسلب. و اما نفس النسبة السالبة او الموجبة غير ملحوظ بها انكشاف الواقع عنها فاذعان النفس بها قد يكون كاذعانها للتمويهات والاضاليل الجواز ان يقدر مضاف و معطوف في الكلام اى العلم ان كان اذعاناً لوقوع النسبة أولا وقوعها، وكلاهما جايز واقع في الفصيح، اما الاول فظاهر واما الثانى فقد ذكره جماعة من النحويين ومثلواله بامثلة منها قوله القضية الذى هو مذهب القدماء فحينئذ لا جواز لما ذكر لكونه تفسيراً بما لا يرضى صاحبه و احتمال تغيير مذهبه او مراعاته لمذهب الغير بعيد جداً مع ان ظواهر الالفاظ حجة و احتمال التقدير تأويل. (محمد علی) (۳۲) قوله وسيشير المصنف إلى تثليث اجزاء القضية فى مباحث القضايا اى حيث يقول: فان كان الحكم فيها بثبوت شيء لشيء أو نفيه عنه فحملية موجبة أو سالبة ويسمى المحكوم عليه موضوعاً و المحكوم به محمولاً والدال على النسبة رابطة، ولم يتعرض لوقوع النسبة او لا وقوعها بشيء لا بالصراحة ولا بالضمن التقريب ص۱۶) (۳۳) اى تقييدية اضافية كالمثال المذكور او توصيفية كـ «رجل «قائم» او غير هما که «الذى ضرب ابوه». (محمد علی)
In the introduction to the book of logic, the author will indicate in the topics of propositions regarding the tripartite division of the parts of the proposition. And what he mentioned in the topics of propositions does not contradict what he mentioned here of the simplicity of assent, for the proposition is not assent, so understand. And here there are lengthy topics whose detail is not suitable for this gloss. (Abd al-Rahim) (24) The first is that one should add to it the conception of the judgmental relation, for the Imam's statement that it is composed of four things rather than three, as you have known, but since his intent was not to investigate his own doctrine but rather to explain the difference between it and what the philosophers held, it is not directed at it, so reflect. (Muhammad Ali) (25) His statement: 'And he chose the doctrine of the ancients': Know that the philosophers, after agreeing that assent is itself the judgment and conviction, not the composite of it and the three conceptions, differed regarding the proposition: is it composed of three things, namely the conception of the subject, the predicate, and the predicative relation, affirmative or negative? Or of four things: the three mentioned and the conception of the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation? The ancients went to the first and the later scholars to the second. And the author chose the first, since he made the object of conviction and assent the judgmental relation itself, not its occurrence or non-occurrence, as did he who went to the second. (Muhammad Ali) (26) With the opening of the lam, i.e., what conviction is attached to, and it may be distorted with its kasra, and its distance is not hidden. (Muhammad Ali) (27) An adjective for the object, not for conviction, necessarily, because conviction and assent are not a part of the proposition, but rather attached to its last part. (Muhammad Ali) (28) His statement: 'And the judgment which is the last part of the proposition' i.e., and he made the object of the judgment, which is the last part of the proposition, the predicative relation, meaning that the judgment regarding the predicative relation is necessarily attached to the mentioned relation. (al-Taqrib p.16) (29) His statement: 'Not the occurrence of the affirmative restrictive relation': It is not hidden that adding this restriction is unnecessary, and it would have been better to replace it with 'predicative' as is apparent, and similarly there is no meaning to the restriction by his saying 'restrictive', necessarily, for the predicative relation is only restrictive, so understand. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) And the professor Sheikh Muhammad al-Kurdi, may his beneficence continue, said in this place: Restricting the complete relations with 'restrictive' is superfluous, because the complete relations are inevitably restrictive, which is the restriction of its subject by its predicate, and generally the term 'restrictive relations' is only applied to incomplete relations such as adjunctive and descriptive relations and what is on their pattern. (al-Taqrib p.16) (30) His statement: 'The occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation' If what is meant by occurrence and non-occurrence is the affirmation and negation apparent in the uttered proposition or the thoughts in the implicit proposition in the soul, then that is another expression for their saying 'the affirmative relation' and 'the negative relation'. And if what is meant by occurrence and non-occurrence is the real existence and stability of the matter of the proposition in reality, and its non-stability and existence as such, then this is something other than the proposition of negation and affirmation apparent in the uttered proposition and other than the thoughts in the implicit proposition, for the uttered and thought negation and affirmation may contradict reality intuitively. And the object of the soul's conviction necessarily is the relation that reveals reality by affirmation or negation. But the negative or affirmative relation itself, without consideration of the revelation of reality from it, the soul's conviction of it may be like its conviction of deceptions and delusions. In any case, the occurrence of the relation, if it is by virtue of its verbal or thought affirmation, and its non-occurrence by virtue of its verbal or thought negation, then it is not something additional to it, but rather the relation itself. And the occurrence of the relation, if it is by virtue of the revelation of reality from it, and its non-occurrence by virtue of the non-revelation of it, then similarly it is not something additional to the relation. And the external occurrence is the same as the real mind-independent occurrence. So what is the meaning of the commentator's saying 'And he chose the doctrine of the ancients since he made the object of conviction the affirmative or negative predicative relation, not the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation'? And occurrence and non-occurrence do not make the proposition have four parts: subject, predicate, relation, and occurrence or non-occurrence, after you have known that occurrence and non-occurrence are not something additional to the relation itself. (al-Taqrib pp.15-16) (31) His statement: 'And the author will indicate': A refutation of what might be imagined, that his making the object of conviction and judgment the relation itself, not its occurrence or non-occurrence, does not apparently indicate his choosing the doctrine of the ancients, because it is permissible to estimate a genitive and a conjunct in the utterance, i.e., knowledge, if it is conviction of the occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence, and both are permissible and occur in eloquent speech. As for the first, it is apparent; as for the second, a group of grammarians have mentioned it and given examples of it, including His statement: '... and He made for you garments that protect you from the heat' (Quran 16:81), i.e., and from the cold, and including His statement: 'So remind, if the reminder benefits' (Quran 87:9), i.e., and if it does not benefit. And the summary of the refutation is that he will indicate the tripartite division of the parts of the proposition, which is the doctrine of the ancients; then there is no possibility for what was mentioned because it is an interpretation that its author would not be satisfied with, and the possibility of his changing his doctrine or his considering the doctrine of another is very remote, especially since the apparent meanings of words are evidence, and the possibility of estimation is reinterpretation. (Muhammad Ali) (32) His statement: 'And the author will indicate the tripartite division of the parts of the proposition in the topics of propositions', i.e., where he says: For if the judgment in them is of the affirmation of something of something or its negation from it, then it is a categorical affirmative or negative, and the judged-upon is called subject, and the judged-by is called predicate, and what indicates the relation is the copula, and he did not discuss the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation at all, neither explicitly nor implicitly. (al-Taqrib p.16) (33) i.e., a restrictive adjunctive relation, like the mentioned example, or a descriptive relation like 'a standing man', or other than them, like 'the one whose father struck'. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a series of glosses on the main text's discussion of the definition of assent (tasdiq) and the components of the proposition (qadiyya). The central issue is whether a proposition consists of three elements (subject, predicate, and the relation between them) or four (adding the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation). The author of the main text, according to the commentators, chose the view of the ancient philosophers (al-mutqaddimun) that the proposition has only three parts, and that assent (tasdiq) is the judgment (hukm) or conviction (idh'an) regarding that relation itself, not regarding its occurrence. The first comment (by Abd al-Rahim) notes that the author will later indicate the tripartite division. Then a series of comments by Muhammad Ali and others unpack the debate. In comment (25), the different views are explained: all philosophers agreed that tasdiq is the judgment itself, not a composite, but they differed on the proposition's composition. The ancients said three parts, the later scholars (muta'akhirun) said four. The author chose the ancients'. Then grammatical clarifications are given. A critical remark (29) objects to the phrase 'affirmative restrictive relation' as redundant. A longer objection (30) from al-Taqrib argues that 'occurrence' is not actually an additional component: whether 'occurrence' refers to the verbal affirmation/negation or to the real state of affairs, it is not separate from the relation itself. The commentator's statement that the author chose the ancients' view because he made the object of conviction the relation rather than its occurrence is criticized. Then a defense (31-32) by Muhammad Ali refutes this objection by pointing out that the author will explicitly adopt the tripartite division later, so there is no room for the skeptical interpretation. The final comment (33) clarifies the types of restrictive relations. Throughout, the passage demonstrates the scholastic method of pinpointing precise definitions and defending the matn from misinterpretation.

نسبة خبرية
وقوع النسبة
مذهب القدماء
مذهب المتأخرين

A) The ancients said the proposition has three parts; the later scholars said it has four.
B) The ancients said tasdiq is composite; the later scholars said it is simple.

Key Takeaway
The proposition's parts are three; the fourth 'occurrence' is not a separate component but either identical to the relation or extraneous.

Refuting Infinite Regress and Circularity in Knowledge
Author refutes infinite regress and circularity in foundation of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument against infinite regress and circularity in the foundation of knowledge.

Core Idea
Infinite regress and circularity collapse because they force a thing to be prior to itself.

This passage tackles a classic epistemological problem: if every act of knowledge requires a prior act, how does knowledge begin? The author demonstrates that both infinite regress (tasalsul) and circularity (dawr) are impossible. We will trace the logical moves carefully and see how the argument depends on deeper assumptions about concepts and assents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two problematic scenarios
Recognize that the author presents two possible ways a chain of dependencies could go: either it extends infinitely (tasalsul) or it loops back to the beginning (dawr). Both are declared impossible.
2
Understand the argument against infinite regress
The author shows that infinite regress entails a thing being prior to itself. Using variables A, B, C: if A depends on B, B on C, and C on A, then A would be prior to B, B prior to C, and C prior to A, so A would be prior to itself — which is impossible.
The argument uses a simple chain to illustrate the contradiction.
3
Understand the argument against circularity
Circularity requires summoning an infinite series of perceptions, which is impossible. The author states this is clear without extensive explanation.
The impossibility here is about actualizing an infinite set in a finite context.
4
Examine the objection about infinite times
An objector suggests that infinite regress might be possible if the soul is pre-eternal and perceptions are spread over infinite time. The author counters that this only works under the philosophers' false doctrine of the pre-eternity of the world and souls.
The author refutes the objection by rejecting its metaphysical basis.
5
Recognize the dependency of the inference
The entire argument rests on the impossibility of acquiring concepts through assents and vice versa. If that is not established, the foundation could end in a necessary concept or assent, avoiding the regress.
This connects to the broader theory of knowledge in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في مقدمة علم المنطق على حصول علم آخر وهكذا فاما ان يذهب ذلك الى ما لانهاية له فهو التسلسل او يعود الى ما بده به اولاً فهو الدور وكلاهما باطل. اما الاول فلاستلزامه حصول الشيء قبل حصوله و هو محال والمستلزم للمحال محال . بيان الملازمة انه اذا توقف حصول الف على ب و هو على ج. و هو على الف كان حصول الف سابقاً على حصول ب و هو على حصول ج وهو على حصول الف والسابق على السابق على الشيء سابق فيكون الف سابقاً على نفسه و كذا ب وج و اما الثاني فلاستلزامه استحضار مالانهاية له و هو محال باطل و كذا المستلزم له. وبيان الملازمة واضح. لا يقال ان المحال هو استحضار امور غير متناهية فى زمان واحد أو في ازمنة متناهية واما في أزمنة غير متناهية فلا لجواز ان يكون النفس قديمة موجودة فى از منة غير متناهية ماضية و يحصل لها في تلك الازمنة اذا كانت غير متناهية فيحصل لها الان الادراك الموقوف على تلك الادراكات الغير المتناهية، فان اردتم انه يستلزم استحضار ما لانهاية له فى زمان واحد او في ازمنة متناهية منعنا الملازمة، لأن الأمور الغير المتناهية من قبيل المعدات الحصول العلم مطلقا وهى غير لازمة الاجتماع في زمان واحد او ازمنة متناهية بل يجوز حصولها في أزمنة غير متناهية بحيث يكون السابق منها معد اللاحق وان اردتم انه يستلزمه في ازمنة غير متناهية سلمنا الملازمة ومنعنا الاستحضار لما ذكر. مرکز تحقیقات کا موتر معلوم اسلامی لانا نقول: هذا انما يصح على مذهب الحكماء القائلين بقدم العالم والنفوس الناطقة وقد تقرر في موضعه بطلان مذهبهم و فساد اعتقادهم و نحن نتكلم على هذا التقدير. ثم لا يخفى ان الاستدلال موقوف على عدم جواز اكتساب التصورات بالتصديقات وبالعكس فان تم والا فلا الجواز ان يكون التصورات نظرياً وينتهي الى تصديق ضروری او جميع التصديقات جميع نظرياً وينتهى الى تصور ضروری فافهم (میرزا محمد علی)
In the introduction to logic, [knowledge] depends on the acquisition of another knowledge, and so on. Then either it goes to infinity, which is tasalsul, or it returns to what it began with, which is dawr, and both are invalid. As for the first, because it necessitates the occurrence of a thing before its occurrence, which is impossible, and that which necessitates the impossible is impossible. Explanation of the entailment: if the occurrence of A depends on B, and B on C, and C on A, then the occurrence of A is prior to the occurrence of B, and B prior to C, and C prior to A, and what is prior to what is prior to a thing is prior to that thing, so A would be prior to itself, and likewise B and C. As for the second, because it necessitates the summoning of that which is infinite, which is impossible and false, and likewise that which necessitates it. The explanation of the entailment is clear. It is not said that the impossible is the summoning of infinite things in one time or in finite times, but in infinite times it is not, because it is possible that the soul is pre-eternal, existing in infinite past times, and it acquires in those times, if they are infinite, so now it would have the perception dependent on those infinite perceptions. So if you mean that it necessitates the summoning of that which is infinite in one time or in finite times, we deny the entailment, because infinite things are like instruments for the acquisition of knowledge absolutely, and they do not require coming together in one time or finite times; rather, it is permissible for them to occur in infinite times such that the preceding one is an instrument for the succeeding one. And if you mean that it necessitates that in infinite times, we grant the entailment but deny the impossibility due to what was mentioned. We say: This is valid only according to the doctrine of the philosophers who affirm the pre-eternity of the world and rational souls, and it has been established in its place the falsehood of their doctrine and the corruption of their belief. And we speak on this assumption. Moreover, it is not hidden that the inference depends on the impossibility of acquiring concepts through assents and vice versa. If it is established, then fine; otherwise, it is possible that all concepts are theoretical and end in a necessary assent, or all assents are theoretical and end in a necessary concept. So understand. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage discusses the problem of infinite regress (tasalsul) and circularity (dawr) in the context of the foundation of knowledge. It argues that both are impossible. The author presents two cases: first, infinite regress leads to a thing preceding itself, which is absurd; second, circularity requires summoning an infinite series, which is impossible. An objection is raised about infinite times, but the author dismisses it based on the philosophers' doctrine, which is rejected. The passage then notes that the whole inference depends on the impossibility of cross-acquisition between concepts and assents. This is a critical refinement typical of a hashiyah, analyzing foundational assumptions of logic.

D) The objection is based on a misinterpretation of 'summoning infinite things.'

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of infinite regress and circularity is fundamental to any sound foundation of knowledge, and depends on the distinction between concepts and assents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Evaluating Wording in Definitions: The Case of 'Nazar'
Author critiques the definition of nazar and prefers 'al-ma'lum' over 'al-ma'qul'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the appropriateness of wording in a logical definition based on clarity and the nature of the definiendum.

Core Idea
The best definition word is the one that immediately conveys the intended logical operation.

In defining 'nazar' (intellectual consideration), the choice between 'ma'qul' (intelligible) and 'ma'lum' (known) is not trivial. This gloss reveals how logical precision requires considering both the immediate import of a term and the scope of the definiendum. Let us analyze the reasoning behind the preference for 'al-ma'lum'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the competing definitions
Find the two wordings: 'observation of the intelligible' (original) vs 'observation of the known' (preferred). Note the terms 'ma'qul' and 'ma'lum'.
The definition is in the opening line of the passage.
2
Understand the author's preference for 'al-ma'lum'
The author argues that 'known' is clearer because it immediately suggests something already disclosed, from which we discover the hidden. 'Intelligible' does not carry this connotation.
He says it would be 'awḍaḥ wa alṣaq bi al-nafs'.
3
Analyze the commentator's defence of 'al-ma'qul'
The commentator offers three reasons: (1) avoiding ambiguity of 'ilm, (2) indicating that nazar deals only with universals, (3) rhyme. Identify these in the passage.
The author later rebuts the first two.
4
Evaluate the author's rebuttal
The author rejects the first reason by defining 'ilm as simple disclosure, not ambiguous. He rejects the second by explaining that the known means to an unknown cannot be a particular, but this is a necessary exclusion, not a reason to prefer 'ma'qul'. He accepts only the rhyme motive.
He says 'kull hādhā takthīr 'ibārāt'.
5
Extract the general principle
The key principle: definitions should use words whose immediate meaning matches the intended logical operation, and the definiens must be appropriate to the nature of the definiendum (e.g., particulars cannot be means to unknown).
This is a critical refinement in the hashiya tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله و هو اى) النظر) ملاحظة المعقول لتحصيل المجهول » ولاريب انه لو قال ملاحظة المعلوم كان أوضح والصق بالنفس فان مفاده الحاضر للذهن بسرعة هو ملاحظة الامر المكشوف ليكتشف بوسيلته امر محجوب و كلمة المعقول لا تعطى هذا المعنى اصلا وانما تعطى ملاحظة الامر الذى ادركه العقل ليستفاد من وراء هذه الملاحظة امرقد جهله العقل قبل سيره في الملاحظة المذكورة و اين يكون المفاد من ذاك ؟ و لكن داعي السجع اهاب به إلى التعبير بالمعقول ليوازن قوله لتحصيل المجهول وقد ادعى الشارح لعدوله عن المعلوم الى المعقول فوائد: التحرز عن استعمال اللفظ المشترك فى التعريف مدعياً ان العلم يطلق تارة على الصورة الحاصلة من الشيء في العقل وتارة على التصديق واخرى على اليقين الى غير ذلك وهذا كله تكثير عبارات فان العلم كما عرفناك به هو انكشاف الواقع وعن اى شيء انكشف فهو علم كما ان العقل هو القوة المميزة للكليات وعن اى شيء انكشفت فهي عقل. + ان ٢- التنبيه بلفظ المعقول على ان الفكر انما يجرى في المعقولات اى في الامور الكلية دون الامور الجزئية والعلم وان كان هو انكشاف الواقع واشعة الواقع تتناول الكليات والجزئيات، الا ان المعلوم الذى يتخذ وسيلة لتحصيل مجهول لا يمكن ان يكون جزئيا لان الجزئى بمحدوديته الثامة قاصر عن يعرف غيره فهو خارج قهراً عن رعيل المعلومات الموصلة الى المجهولات. (۳) قال ومنها رعاية السجع اقول : و هذه هي التي اهابت به الى العدول كما بينا انفاً قوله : «فان الجزئى لا يكون كاسباً ولا مكتسباً اى لا تنتج معرفته معرفة غيره ولا معرفة غيره من الجزئيات معرفته فالجزئى لا يعرف جزئيا آخر ولا يتعرف به كل ذلك للتعينات القائمة بين الفرد والفرد الآخر قياماً حاجباً. (التقريب ص ١٧)
His saying: 'and it (i.e., consideration) is the observation of the intelligible to obtain the unknown.' There is no doubt that if he had said 'observation of the known', it would be clearer and more immediately understood, for its meaning that quickly comes to mind is the observation of the disclosed matter in order to discover through it a hidden matter. The word 'intelligible' does not give this meaning at all; rather, it gives the observation of that which the intellect has perceived in order to benefit beyond this observation something that the intellect was ignorant of before its proceeding in the mentioned observation. How far apart are the meanings! But the motive of rhyme called him to express 'intelligible' to balance his saying 'to obtain the unknown'. The commentator claimed for his turning from 'known' to 'intelligible' benefits: avoidance of using ambiguous words in the definition, claiming that 'ilm is used sometimes for the form obtained from the thing in the intellect, sometimes for assent, sometimes for certainty, etc. All this is verbosity, for knowledge as we have defined it for you is the disclosure of reality; whatever discloses, it is knowledge, just as the intellect is the discriminating power for universals; whatever discloses, it is intellect. Second: alerting by the word 'intelligible' that thought runs only in intelligibles, i.e., universal matters, not particular matters. And knowledge, though it is the disclosure of reality and the rays of reality encompass universals and particulars, the known that is taken as a means to obtain the unknown cannot be particular, because the particular, due to its complete limitation, is incapable of making known something else. So it is necessarily excluded from the troop of knowns that lead to unknowns. Third: he said, including the observance of rhyme. I say: This is what impelled him to turn, as we have shown earlier. His saying: 'For the particular is neither acquisitive nor acquired' — meaning its knowledge does not produce knowledge of another, nor does knowledge of another of the particulars produce knowledge of it. So the particular does not know another particular, nor is it known by it. All that is due to the determinations standing between one individual and another, serving as a veil. (Taqrib p. 17)
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This extensive gloss critiques the definition of 'nazar' (intellectual consideration) as 'observation of the intelligible (al-ma'qul) to obtain the unknown'. The author argues that using 'al-ma'lum' (the known) instead would be clearer and more appropriate, because the purpose is to start from what is known to discover what is unknown. However, the commentator defends the original wording for several reasons: to avoid ambiguity of the term 'ilm, to indicate that nazar deals only with universals (not particulars), and for rhetorical rhyme. The author then rebuts these justifications, asserting that 'ilm is simply disclosure and that the particular cannot serve as a means to knowledge due to its individuality. This is a typical hashiyah critical refinement.

Key Terms
النظر
المعقول
المعلوم

B) Because a particular is too limited (bi mahdudiyatihi al-tammah) to make known something else.
C) Because the term 'known' is ambiguous.
D) Because the author prefers rhyme.

Key Takeaway
When defining a term in logic, choose wording that immediately and unambiguously conveys the intended epistemological operation, and ensure the definiens respects the nature of the definiendum.

?

What Is Mantiq?

Scope of 'Intelligible Matter' in the Definition of Mantiq
Author clarifies that 'toward the intelligible' covers both singular and composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain what 'al-amr al-ma'qul' includes and why it must cover both singular and composite intelligibles.

Core Idea
The definition's scope includes all intelligibles, ensuring no logical object is excluded.

In the definition of mantiq, the phrase 'toward the intelligible matter' (naḥwa al-amr al-ma'qūl) is deliberately broad. This gloss from Muhammad Ali, as recorded in Yazdi's Hashiya, expands on that phrase to confirm it includes both concepts (tasawwurāt) and assents (tasdiqāt), whether certain, probable, or ignorant. Moreover, it covers both single and multiple instances so that the definition applies to simple terms and composite arguments alike, serving as the genus for the object of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being expanded
Locate the phrase 'al-amr al-ma'qul' in the definition of mantiq. It is the object toward which reasoning moves. Note that the gloss explicitly says 'whether it is a concept or an assent'.
This shows the term is not limited to propositions.
2
Recognize the categories included
The gloss lists three epistemic categories: certain (yaqini), probable (zanni), and ignorant (jahli). This means even false or uncertain beliefs are included as intelligible matter.
The definition of mantiq does not require truth, only intelligibility.
3
Understand the 'one or more' expansion
The author states 'it is general, whether it is one or more'. This ensures that the definition covers a single concept (mufrad) and a composite expression (murakkab), such as a proposition or syllogism.
Without this, the definition might be thought to apply only to single terms.
4
Connect to the genus role
The phrase 'al-amr al-ma'qul' serves as the genus (jins) in the definition. By being inclusive, it provides the proper category for nazar (systematic reasoning).
This is why the commentator warns 'do not neglect' – the breadth is essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله نحو الامر المعقول سواء كان تصوراً او تصديقاً يقينياً أوظنياً أو جهلياً، ولا يخفى ان المراد منه اعم من ان يكون واحداً أو أكثر ليتناول التعريف بالمفرد والمركب قائلام فيه للجنس فلا تغفل. (محمد علی)
His saying: 'toward the intelligible matter' – whether it is a concept or an assent, certain or probable or ignorant. And it is not hidden that what is meant by it is general, whether it is one or more, so that the definition includes the simple and the composite, serving as genus therein, so do not neglect. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This gloss explains that the phrase 'toward the intelligible matter' (naḥwa al-amr al-ma'qūl) includes both concepts and assents, and also covers both single and multiple intelligibles. This ensures the definition applies to simple and composite premises, which serve as the genus for nazar.

B) Only singular assents
C) Both concepts and assents, singular and plural
D) Only multiple premises

Key Takeaway
The term 'al-amr al-ma'qul' in the definition of mantiq is intentionally broad to encompass all objects of thought, ensuring the definition is comprehensive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
