Scope of the Unknown in Mantiq's Definition
Author clarifies the object of acquisition is also general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether the unknown (ghayr ma'lūm) targeted by mantiq is conceptual or assertoric, and explain why the definition must cover both.

Core Idea
Mantiq aims to obtain both conceptual and assertoric unknowns.

This brief gloss expands the definition of mantiq by making explicit that the 'unknown' to be obtained is general. It is not limited to unknown judgments (tasdīqāt) but also includes unknown concepts (tasawwurāt). This clarification is essential because logic deals with both forming definitions (concepts) and constructing arguments (judgments). Understanding this prevents a common reduction of logic to mere propositional reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Locate the term being expanded
Identify the phrase 'amr ghayr ma'lūm' (an unknown thing) in the gloss. This is the object of the verb 'taḥṣīl' (to obtain) in the definition of mantiq.
The gloss explicitly says 'this is also general'.
2
Identify the two categories
Notice the gloss divides the unknown into two types: taṣawwurī (conceptual) and taṣdīqī (assertoric). These correspond to the two main branches of logic: definitions and arguments.
The words are clear despite the cut-off text.
3
Connect to the definition of mantiq
The definition of mantiq is 'a tool for obtaining an unknown thing from known things' (al-āla li-taḥṣīl al-amr al-ghayr al-ma'lūm). This gloss ensures that 'thing' includes both concepts and judgments.
Without this, one might think mantiq only produces judgments.
4
Reason why both are necessary
Consider that without having a clear concept of a term (e.g., the definition of 'gharar' in contracts), you cannot form a sound judgment about its ruling. Mantiq must cover both stages.
Example: obtaining the concept of 'riba' is a prerequisite for the judgment that 'interest is haram'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This brief gloss extends the same point as the previous: the unknown to be obtained (tahṣīl) can be either a concept (tasawwurī) or an assent (tasdīqī). The text cuts off, but the idea is clear.

Why 'Ma'qul' Not 'Ma'lum'? The Refinement of Nazar's Definition
Author comments on the definition of nazar and reasons for using 'ma'qul'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three reasons given in the sharh for preferring 'ma'qul' over 'ma'lum' in the definition of nazar, and explain how each reason refines the definition.

Core Idea
The definition of nazar is refined to use 'ma'qul' to ensure it deals only with universals and avoids ambiguity.

In the definition of reflection (nazar), the original author chose the word 'ma'qul' (intelligible) over 'ma'lum' (known). The commentator (sharh) and the hashya author provide detailed justifications for this choice. In this lesson, we unpack their reasoning and see how it refines the concept of nazar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original definition
Find the phrase: 'ملاحظة المعقول لتحصيل المجهول'. This is the definition being refined.
2
Understand the proposed alternative
The author notes that if he had said 'ملاحظة المعلوم' it would be clearer ('أوضح وألصق بالنفس'). Reflect on why 'ma'lum' might seem more immediate.
3
Extract the three benefits from the sharh
List the three benefits: (1) avoiding ambiguity of 'ilm, (2) indicating thought deals only with universals, (3) rhyme (saj').
4
Analyze the reasoning for each benefit
For benefit 2, explain why particulars cannot be means: they are limited and cannot make known other things. For benefit 1, note that 'ilm has multiple meanings.
5
Evaluate the refinement
Consider how using 'ma'qul' makes the definition more precise: it restricts nazar to universal objects and avoids confusion with other meanings of 'ilm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله و هو اى) النظر) ملاحظة المعقول لتحصيل المجهول » ولاريب انه لو قال ملاحظة المعلوم كان أوضح والصق بالنفس فان مفاده الحاضر للذهن بسرعة هو ملاحظة الامر المكشوف ليكتشف بوسيلته امر محجوب و كلمة المعقول لا تعطى هذا المعنى اصلا وانما تعطى ملاحظة الامر الذى ادركه العقل ليستفاد من وراء هذه الملاحظة امرقد جهله العقل قبل سيره في الملاحظة المذكورة و اين يكون المفاد من ذاك ؟ و لكن داعي السجع اهاب به إلى التعبير بالمعقول ليوازن قوله لتحصيل المجهول وقد ادعى الشارح لعدوله عن المعلوم الى المعقول فوائد: التحرز عن استعمال اللفظ المشترك فى التعريف مدعياً ان العلم يطلق تارة على الصورة الحاصلة من الشيء في العقل وتارة على التصديق واخرى على اليقين الى غير ذلك وهذا كله تكثير عبارات فان العلم كما عرفناك به هو انكشاف الواقع وعن اى شيء انكشف فهو علم كما ان العقل هو القوة المميزة للكليات وعن اى شيء انكشفت فهي عقل. + ان ٢- التنبيه بلفظ المعقول على ان الفكر انما يجرى في المعقولات اى في الامور الكلية دون الامور الجزئية والعلم وان كان هو انكشاف الواقع واشعة الواقع تتناول الكليات والجزئيات، الا ان المعلوم الذى يتخذ وسيلة لتحصيل مجهول لا يمكن ان يكون جزئيا لان الجزئى بمحدوديته الثامة قاصر عن يعرف غيره فهو خارج قهراً عن رعيل المعلومات الموصلة الى المجهولات. (۳) قال ومنها رعاية السجع اقول : و هذه هي التي اهابت به الى العدول كما بينا انفاً قوله : «فان الجزئى لا يكون كاسباً ولا مكتسباً اى لا تنتج معرفته معرفة غيره ولا معرفة غيره من الجزئيات معرفته فالجزئى لا يعرف جزئيا آخر ولا يتعرف به كل ذلك للتعينات القائمة بين الفرد والفرد الآخر قياماً حاجباً. (التقريب ص ١٧) (۴۸) اى بطريق القصد كما هو المتبادر سيما وقد قيل بالغاية فلا ينتقض بتعقل المبادى المرتبة دفعة في الحدس لأنه ليس بقصد النفس واختياره بل يسنح لها بغير اختيارها. (عبد الرحيم) (۴۹) قوله نحو الامر المعقول سواء كان تصوراً او تصديقاً يقينياً أوظنياً أو جهلياً، ولا يخفى ان المراد منه اعم من ان يكون واحداً أو أكثر ليتناول التعريف بالمفرد والمركب قائلام فيه للجنس فلا تغفل. (محمد علی)
His statement: 'and it (i.e., reflection) is the observation of the intelligible to obtain the unknown.' There is no doubt that if he had said 'observation of the known' it would be clearer and more immediate to the soul, because its meaning, present to the mind quickly, is the observation of the uncovered thing to discover through it a hidden thing. The word 'intelligible' does not give this meaning at all; rather, it gives the observation of the thing that the intellect has apprehended, so that from beyond this observation something that the intellect knew before its movement in the aforementioned observation is obtained. And where is that meaning from that? But the motive of rhyme called him to express by 'intelligible' to balance his saying 'to obtain the unknown'. The commentator claimed for his turning away from 'known' to 'intelligible' benefits: (1) Caution against using an ambiguous word in the definition, claiming that 'ilm is sometimes applied to the form obtained from a thing in the intellect, sometimes to assent, sometimes to certainty, and so on. All this is multiplication of expressions, for 'ilm, as we have defined it for you, is the uncovering of reality, and whatever it is uncovered from is knowledge, just as the intellect is the power that distinguishes universals, and whatever it is uncovered from is intellect. (2) Alerting by the word 'intelligible' that thinking only proceeds in intelligibles, i.e., universal things, not particular things, and although 'ilm is uncovering of reality and the rays of reality encompass universals and particulars, the known that is taken as a means to obtain an unknown cannot be particular, because the particular, due to its complete limitation, is incapable of making known something else, so it is necessarily outside the ranks of leading to unknowns. (3) He said: Among them is the observance of rhyme. I say: This is what called him to turn away, as we explained earlier. His statement: 'For the particular does not acquire nor is acquired', i.e., knowledge of it does not produce knowledge of something else, and knowledge of other particulars does not produce knowledge of it. So the particular does not know another particular nor is it known by it, all due to the determinations existing between one individual and another as a hindering barrier. (al-Taqrib p. 17) (48) That is, intentionally, as is the apparent meaning, especially since the purpose has been stated, so it is not contradicted by the comprehension of premises arranged at once in intuition, because it is not by the soul's intention and choice, but rather it occurs to it without its choice. (Abd al-Rahim) (49) His statement: 'toward the intelligible matter' whether it be a concept or an assent, certain or probable or ignorant. It is not hidden that what is meant is general, whether it is one or more, to include the definition by a single term or composite, and it is a genus for the definition, so do not be heedless. (Muhammad Ali).
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a hashiya commentary on the definition of reflection (nazar) given by the original author. The original definition said nazar is 'the observation of the intelligible (al-ma'qul) to obtain the unknown.' The commentator (sharh) had already argued for using 'ma'qul' instead of 'ma'lum' (the known), and the hashya author here expands on those reasons. First, he notes that 'ma'lum' would be clearer, but the author of the original text (al-musannif) chose 'ma'qul' for rhyme. Then he lists three benefits from the sharh: (1) avoiding ambiguity because 'ilm is a common term with many meanings; (2) alerting that thought only deals with universals (ma'qulat), since particulars cannot serve as means to knowledge; (3) rhyme. The hashya author adds his own remarks, including a short point from (48) that nazar must be intentional, not like intuition. Then in (49) he explains that 'nahw al-amr al-ma'qul' covers both concepts and assents and includes singular and composite definitions. The commentary flows from the main definition to each of its parts, providing justifications and clarifications typical of a critical refinement.

نظر
معقول
معلوم

B) Because 'ma'qul' indicates that only universals are the means to obtain unknown knowledge, and avoids ambiguity.
C) Because 'ma'lum' is only used for certain knowledge, not probable.
D) Because 'ma'qul' is easier to rhyme with 'majhul'.

Key Takeaway
The choice of 'ma'qul' over 'ma'lum' in defining nazar refines the definition to ensure it applies only to universal intelligibles and avoids terminological ambiguity.

The Scope and Intentionality of Nazar
Author comments on intentionality and scope of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between intentional reflection (nazar) and intuitive comprehension, and identify the scope of nazar as covering both concepts and assents.

Core Idea
Nazar is an intentional act of the mind aimed at universals, not a passive intuition.

The hashya author adds two important clarifications: first, that nazar must be intentional (bi-qasd), not like sudden comprehension or intuition; second, that the object of nazar ('nahw al-amr al-ma'qul') includes both concepts (tasawwur) and assents (tasdiq), and can be singular or composite. These refinements ensure the definition covers all cases of deliberate reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Find the condition of intentionality
Look at the note (48) about 'بطريق القصد' and the exception for intuition. Nazar must be an intentional act.
2
Understand why intuition is excluded
Intuition (hads) occurs without choice, so it is not nazar. The passage says 'ليس بقصد النفس واختياره'.
3
Identify the scope of 'nahw al-amr al-ma'qul'
From note (49): it includes concepts and assents, whether certain, probable, or even ignorant. It also includes singular and composite objects.
4
Consider why the definition uses 'nahw' (toward) rather than a direct object
The use of 'nahw' allows inclusion of composite definitions, as noted in the commentary.
5
Apply the scope to an example
For instance, the definition of 'hadd' (definition) as a composite concept falls under nazar. Give a concrete example from Islamic logic.

Why 'Ma'qul' and Not 'Ma'lum'? Avoiding Ambiguity and Excluding Particulars
Author justifies shift from 'ma'lum' to 'ma'qul' in defining nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why ambiguous words are avoided in logical definitions and why particulars cannot serve as premises or conclusions in nazar.

Core Idea
Precision in definition requires univocal terms; particulars lack the generality needed for reflection.

In defining nazar, the author chose 'ma'qul' over 'ma'lum'. Why? Because 'ilm is an ambiguous term, and particulars cannot be means or ends of reflective thought. This lesson unpacks these two critical refinements, showing the care required in logical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The passage begins by addressing a potential objection: if 'ma'lum' (the known) is used, it pairs naturally with 'majhul' (the unknown) as opposites, since 'ilm and jahl are opposites. The author shifts from 'ma'lum' to 'ma'qul' to avoid this.
2
Recognise the ambiguity of 'ilm
The second comment lists the many meanings of 'ilm: the form in the mind, tasdiq (assent) which includes certainty, probability, compound ignorance, taqlid, etc. This polysemy makes 'ilm unsuitable for a precise definition.
Ambiguous words (lafz mushtarak) are to be avoided in definitions.
3
Understand why particulars are excluded
The third comment argues: particulars are perceived by external or internal senses; arranging them yields only another particular sensation or nothing universal. Hence particulars cannot be kasib (producing another knowledge) nor muktasab (acquired from universals).
Thus thought (nazar) involves only universals.
4
See how 'ma'qul' signals universality
The word 'ma'qul' is applied only to universals, unlike 'ma'lum' which applies to both universals and particulars. Using 'ma'qul' ensures that nazar is understood as reflection on universals only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(محمد علی) (۵۳) اما الاول فلان الجزئى اما ان يكون محسوساً بالحواس الظاهري التي هي: الباصرة و الشامة والذائقة واللامسة والسامعة، او بالخواص الباطنى التى هى الحس المشترك والخيال والوهم والمتصرفة والحافظة، فلا يحصل من ترتيب المحسوسات المتعددة احساس جزئى آخر ولا ادراك كلى. و اما الثاني فلانه لوكان مكتسباً لا يخلو اما ان يكون مكتسباً بالجزئى او بالكلى، الأول باطل، لمامر ان الجزئى لا يكون كاسباً، وكذلك الثانى، لأن ضم كلى الى كلى آخر لا يفيد الجزئية واذا لم يكن الجزئيات كاسبة ولا مكتسبة فلا يجرى فيها الفكر و فى استعمال لفظ المعقول اشارة الى ذلك لانه لا يطلق الأعلى الكلى بخلاف لفظ المعلوم فانه يستعمل في الكلى والجزئى (عبدالرحيم (ره)
His statement: 'And in turning away from the word ma'lum (known)' – this is an indication of the answer to what is raised against the author, that he did not define reflection as 'observation of the known to obtain the unknown', even though 'ilm and jahl are opposites. (Sheikh Abd al-Rahim) (52) His statement: 'Among them is the caution against using the ambiguous word (mushtarak)': For 'ilm is applied to the form obtained in the intellect, which they divided into concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) with its divisions: probability, fixed certainty corresponding to reality which they call certainty (yaqin) in terminology, and non-corresponding which is expressed as compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), and fixed certainty which does not correspond to reality and which they call taqlid in some terminologies and itiqad in others. Likewise, it is applied to fixed certainty corresponding to reality that admits no doubt, and to belief in the general sense that includes it and others like probability, ignorance, and taqlid. So it is not permissible to use it in the definition like other ambiguous words, because sometimes one meaning is intended and the addressee understands another. So understand. (Muhammad Ali) (53) As for the first [reason], the particular is either perceived by the external senses: sight, smell, taste, touch, hearing, or by the internal faculties: common sense, imagination, estimation, cogitative, memory. Arranging multiple perceived objects does not produce another particular sensation nor a universal perception. As for the second [reason], if it (the particular) were acquired, it would be either by a particular or by a universal. The first is invalid because, as previously stated, a particular does not acquire. Similarly, the second, because combining a universal with another universal does not result in particularity. Since particulars neither acquire nor are acquired, thought does not involve them. In the use of the word 'ma'qul' there is an indication of this, because it is only applied to the universal, unlike 'ma'lum' which is used for both universal and particular. (Abd al-Rahim (ra))
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage consists of three related comments that further justify the choice of 'ma'qul' over 'ma'lum' in the definition of nazar. The first comment (51) notes that the shift answers a potential objection: if 'ma'lum' is used, it would seem to pair with 'majhul' as opposites, but the author avoided that. The second comment (52) elaborates on the problem of ambiguity (ishtirak): the word 'ilm has many meanings, including the cognitive form in the mind, assent (tasdiq) with various subdivisions (certainty, probability, compound ignorance, taqlid), and even belief in a general sense. Using such an ambiguous word in a definition is not allowed because it may lead to misunderstanding. The third comment (53) explains why particulars are excluded from the means of reflection (they cannot be kasib or muktasab), and how using 'ma'qul' signals that only universals are involved. The passage demonstrates meticulous care in terminology, typical of hashya literature.

Key Terms

جزئي
كاسب

D) Because it is not used in the classical texts of logic.

Key Takeaway
Definitions must use univocal terms, and only universals can function as means or ends of logical reflection.

The Necessity of a Universal Rule in Logic
Author argues for the indispensability of a universal rule (qa'ida kulliyya) despite the goal being particular knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logicians rely on a universal rule (qa'ida kulliyya) even though the goal is to know particular thoughts, and identify how this principle applies in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Universal rules are practical shortcuts, not the ultimate goal of logic.

This passage from a commentary on logic addresses a fundamental question: if the purpose of logic is to evaluate particular thoughts, why do we need universal rules? The author clarifies that universal rules are indispensable because teaching every possible particular thought is impossible. Instead, we learn a universal rule that, when applied, allows us to judge any particular instance. This lesson will walk through the logical reasoning behind this methodological choice and show its relevance to Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text claim
Locate the statement 'la budda min qā'idati kulliyya' (a universal rule is necessary). This is the claim from the original matn being commented on.
2
Understand the lexical clarification
The commentator explains that 'budda' can mean 'separation' or 'substitute'. Here 'la budda min' means 'no substitute for' or 'must have'. This emphasizes the indispensability of the rule.
3
Recognize the apparent problem
The commentator raises an objection: if the logician's aim is to explain the details of particular thoughts, why do we need a universal rule? The goal seems to be particular, not universal.
4
Extract the answer: practical necessity
The answer is that it is practically impossible to teach every particular thought directly. So logicians use a universal rule that, when applied, allows the student to distinguish correct from corrupt thoughts in any specific case.
5
Apply to an Islamic science
Consider the universal rule in usul al-fiqh: 'Al-umuru bi maqasidiha' (acts are judged by intentions). This rule is applied to countless particular actions to determine their ruling. Without the rule, each action would need separate study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۶۴) قوله قلابد من قاعدة كلية اى لافراق منها، من قولهم بده يبده بدأ اي فرقه، والتبديد التفرقة وتبدد اى تفرق ولا عوض عنها فان البد يجيء بمعنى العوض أيضاً فتكون «من» ح بمعنى عن وسيجيء زيادة كلام انشاء الله تعالى. و انما احتجنا الى القاعدة الكلية القاعدة الكلية مع ان المقصود معرفة تفاصيل احوال الافكار الجزئية فان غرض المنطقى بيان احوال تلك الافكار على الوجه الجزئى التفصيلى اذ المتعلم ما لم يعلم حال الفكر الذي ورد عليه على الوجه الجزئى التفصيلي لم يتميز عنده هذا الفكر الجزئى عن فاسده لأنه لما لم يتيسر لهم صحيح الاتيان بهذا المقصود اكتفوا بما اليه يؤل عند الاحتياج و هو القاعدة الكلية التي لولوحظت في معرفة احول
(64) His saying: 'la budda min qā'idati kulliyya' (a universal rule is necessary): i.e., for separating from it [error?]. From their saying: 'bada yabdu bad' i.e., he separated. And 'al-tabdi' is separation. And 'tabadda' i.e., became separated. And there is no substitute for it, for 'al-badd' also comes in the meaning of substitute, so 'min' here is in the meaning of 'an'. And further speech will come, God willing. And why are we in need of the universal rule, even though the object is to know the details of particular thoughts? For the purpose of the logician is to explain the states of those thoughts in a particular detailed manner, because the student, unless he knows the state of the thought that occurs to him in a particular detailed manner, cannot distinguish this particular thought from its corrupt counterpart. Since it was not feasible for them to correctly achieve this purpose, they sufficed with what it leads to when in need, namely the universal rule, which if observed in knowing the states...
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage explains the necessity of a universal rule (qa'ida kulliyya) in logic. The author begins with a lexical analysis of the phrase 'la budda min' meaning 'no substitute for' or 'must have', where 'badd' can mean separation or substitute. He then poses a problem: the logician's goal is to explain particular thoughts in detail, so why do we need a universal rule? The answer: because it is impractical to teach every particular thought individually; instead, we use a universal rule that, when observed, allows the student to distinguish a correct particular thought from its corrupt counterpart by applying the rule. The text cuts off but the argument is clear: universal rules are a necessary shortcut for the practical purpose of training the mind.

Definitions of Qadim and Alam: Lexical and Conventional Meanings
Gloss explaining the lexical and conventional meanings of qadim and alam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the lexical and conventional definitions of 'alam and identify its derivations.

Core Idea
The term 'alam has no singular form and its meaning shifts from rational beings to all creatures.

In Islamic logic, precise definitions are crucial. This gloss clarifies two foundational terms: qadim (ancient) and 'alam (world). The commentator explores lexical, conventional, and derived meanings, showing how a single term can carry multiple layers of definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms defined
The gloss defines two terms: 'al-qadim' (the ancient) and 'al-alam' (the world). Note that 'qadim' is the opposite of 'hadith' (temporally originated).
2
Analyze the lexical meaning of 'alam
Linguistically, 'alam means a group of rational beings. Evidence: Arabs say 'ja'ani alamun min al-nas' (a group of people came to me) but not 'min al-baqar' (of cows).
This shows 'alam is reserved for rational beings in its original usage.
3
Contrast the conventional meaning
In common custom ('urf), 'alam means all creatures (jami' al-makhluqat). This is a broader extension.
Be aware of the shift from lexical (rational) to conventional (all creatures).
4
Explore other interpretations
Some say 'alam refers to possessors of knowledge (angels, humans, jinn). Others say it is a name for what indicates the Creator (substances and accidents).
These interpretations tie to derivation from 'alama (sign) or 'ilm (knowledge).
5
Examine derivations and the Ibn Abbas report
Derivation is either from 'alama (sign) or 'ilm (knowledge). Ibn Abbas interprets 'worlds' in the Quran as angels, humans, and jinn, because each group is a 'alam of knowledge.
This shows how definition impacts tafsir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(محمد على) (۶۱) «القدم» كعنب ضد الحدوث و «العالم» جمع لا واحد له من لفظه كالرهط والجيش و غير ذلك وهو فى عرف اللغة عبارة عن جماعة من العقلاء لأنهم يقولون: جائني عالم من الناس ولا يقولون: «عالم من البقر» و فى عرف الناس عبارة عن جميع المخلوقات وقيل انه اسم لاولى العلم من الملائكة و الثقلين وقيل: هو اسم لما يعلم به الصانع من الجواهر والاعراض، واشتقاقه من العلامة للصانع تعالى :وقيل انه مشتق من العلم على ماروى عن ابن عباس في قوله تعالى: «الحمد لله رب العالمين حيث قال هم صنف من الملائكة و الانس والجن لانه لا يصلح ان يكون كل صنف منهم عالماً. (عبد الرحيم)
(Muhammad Ali) (61) 'Al-Qadim' is like 'grape' meaning opposite of temporality, and 'al-Alam' is a plural with no singular from its own word, like 'al-raht' and 'al-jaysh' and others. In linguistic custom it means a group of rational beings, because they say 'a group of people came to me' and do not say 'a group of cows'. In common custom it means all creatures. And it is said it is a name for possessors of knowledge from the angels and the two weighty ones. And it is said it is a name for what the Creator is known by, from substances and accidents, and its derivation is from 'al-alamah' (the sign) for the Creator. And it is said it is derived from 'al-ilm' (knowledge) according to what is narrated from Ibn Abbas regarding His saying 'Praise be to God, Lord of the worlds', where he said: they are a group from the angels, humans, and jinn, because it is not suitable for every group among them to be a world. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This gloss defines two key terms. 'Al-Qadim' is the opposite of hadith (temporally originated), like the word for grape. 'Al-Alam' is a broken plural with no singular from its root, similar to 'raht' (group) or 'jaysh' (army). Its linguistic meaning is a group of rational beings, as Arabs use 'alam' only for people, not animals. Its common meaning is all created beings. Other interpretations: it refers to knowledgeable beings (angels, humans, jinn), or it is a name for what indicates the Creator (via substances and accidents). Derivation is either from 'alama (sign) or 'ilm (knowledge), with a report from Ibn Abbas that 'worlds' in the Quran means angels, humans, and jinn.

Key Terms
القدم
العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All created beings
B) A group of rational beings
C) The universe
D) Knowledge

Key Takeaway
Alam is a plural without a singular, and its meaning shifts from a group of rational beings (lexical) to all creatures (conventional), with additional interpretations linking it to knowledge or a sign of the Creator.

Lexical Analysis: Tracing the Roots of 'Nas'
Lexical gloss on the word 'nas'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform a basic lexical analysis of a key term by tracing its original form, derivations, and contextual usage in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Precise definition begins with lexical roots: 'nas' originally 'unas'.

Before you can define a term logically, you must know what its words actually mean. This lesson walks through a classical lexical gloss on the word 'nas' (people) as found in logic commentaries. The gloss unpacks its original form, derivations, and whether it includes jinn or only humans. Such lexical care is essential for accurate definitions in Islamic logic, fiqh, and tafsir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word and its context
Find the word under analysis – here it is 'الناس' (al-nas). Notice the passage explains it as part of a broader definition of 'mantiq' (logic). Ask: Why would the author pause to explain this word?
Lexical glosses often indicate a term is ambiguous or critical for the definition.
2
Trace the original form and changes
The gloss says the original form is 'أناس' (unas) with a hamza, which was dropped for lightness (takhfif). Understand that this is a common linguistic process. Note that after the hamza is dropped, the word becomes 'الناس' (al-nas) as a fixed form.
The omission is described as 'almost necessary' – a linguistic rule.
3
Examine the derivation roots
Two derivations are mentioned: from 'إنسان' (insan) or 'أنس' (uns) – both related to rational beings or sociability. Also from 'نوس' (naws) meaning movement. Compare these to see how the word might connect to its meaning.
Multiple derivations suggest a rich semantic field.
4
Check the lexical scope (who is included?)
The gloss cites al-Qamus that 'nas' can include jinn, but then clarifies that in this context it means humans specifically. This teaches that words can have a general and a specific usage depending on context.
This is critical: a word's scope in a definition must match the author's intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۶۵) الناس في الاصل اناس حذفت همزته تخفيفاً وحذفها مع لام التعريف كاللازم لا يكاد يقال الاناس ويشهد لاصله انسان و انس عن العقلاء الطالبين للصواب وقيل انه جمع لا واحد له من لفظه، واشتقاقه من النوس و هو الحركة يقال: ناس ينوس نوصاً اذا تحرك و النوس تذبذب الشيء في الهواء. قال فى القاموس : الناس يكون من الجن والانس و المراد به هينا الانس (عبدالرحيم)
(65) 'Al-Nas' originally is 'unas', its hamza was omitted for lightness, and its omission with the definite article is almost necessary; it is hardly said 'al-unas'. Its origin is attested by 'insan' and 'uns'. It is from the rational beings seeking correctness. And it is said it is a plural with no singular from its own word, and its derivation is from 'al-naws' which is movement; it is said 'nas yanus nawsan' when he moves, and 'al-naws' is the oscillation of a thing in the air. He said in al-Qamus: 'al-nas' includes jinn and humans, but the intended here is humans. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This gloss provides a lexical analysis of the word 'nas' (people). It states that the original form was 'unas', but the hamza was dropped for ease. The word is derived from 'insan' (human) or 'uns' (sociability), and it refers to rational beings who seek correctness. Another derivation is from 'naws' meaning movement. The author cites al-Qamus that 'nas' can include jinn, but here it means humans specifically.

Defining Logic by Its End and Identifying Its Subject Matter
Commentator explains definition by end (rasm) and states subject matter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how logic is defined by its end (ghaya) as a description (rasm) and identify its subject matter (mawdu') in the Islamic logical tradition.

Core Idea
Logic's definition by its end ensures it is a tool, not a guarantor of truth.

In this passage, the commentator shows that logic is defined not by its essence but by its purpose: preserving the mind from error. He clarifies that this is a definition by description (rasm) using the coextensive property of its end. He also states the subject matter of logic: known conceptual and assent-based things as means to the unknown. This lesson unpacks these key ideas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of definition
The passage states that logic is defined by its end, which is a rasm (definition by description), not a hadd (essential definition). Note that rasm uses accidents or properties, not genus and differentia.
Rasm is common in defining practical sciences where essential definitions are difficult.
2
Recognize the ghaya as a coextensive property
The end (ghaya) of logic—preserving the mind from error—must be specific to logic and equal (musawiya) to it. The commentator defends this against the objection that ends can be broader.
Coextensiveness (musawiya) means the property applies to all and only instances of logic.
3
Understand why 'its observance' is used
The author says 'its observance preserves' not 'it preserves'. This is because logic itself does not guarantee correctness; only careful application of its rules does. The tool may be neglected.
This highlights logic as a tool (ala) requiring active use.
4
State the subject matter of logic
The subject (mawdu') of logic is known conceptual (tasawwuri) and assent-based (tasdiqi) things, considered from the aspect of their leading to unknown conceptual or assent-based things.
This means logic studies known information to derive new knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله و علم من هذا تعريف المنطق اى بالرسم لان اثبات الاحتياج اليه هو ان يبين ان الناس فى اى شيء يحتاجون اليه فذلك الشيء يكون غايته وعرضه ويحصل بذلك معرفة العلم بغايته وهي تصوره برسمه لا يقال ان تعريف الشيء بخاصته البيئة الشاملة وتلك الخاصة لا تكون الامساوية وغاية الشيء يجوز ان يكون اعم منه لجواز ان يكون الأمر الواحد غاية لأمور متعددة. لانا نقول : المراد بالغاية الغاية المساوية، فلا محذور (عبدالرحيم) و انما قال تعصم مراعاتها و لم يقل نفسها بدل مراعاتها) لان المنطق ليس نفسه تعصم الذهن عن الخطاء والالم يعرض للمنطق حظاً وليس كذلك فانه ربا يخطأ لاهمال الآلة. (شمسية) قوله فهيهنا اى فى هذه الحاشية علم امران من الامور الثلاثة التي انعقدت المقدمة تقريباً لبيانها و هما بيان الحاجة الى المنطق وتعريفه وبقى الامر الثالث و هو بيان موضوعه فقال و موضوعه المعلوم التصوري و التصديق من حيث ايصالها الى مجهول تصوری او تصديق بمعنى انه يلزم ان تكون فيهما
His saying: 'And from this is known the definition of logic' — i.e., by description (rasm), because proving the need for it is to show in what thing people need it. That thing becomes its end (ghaya) and its property (arad), and by that the knowledge of the science is obtained through its end, which is its depiction (tasawwur) by its description. It cannot be said that defining a thing by its comprehensive specific property, and that property is not equal (musawiya), and the end of a thing may be more general than it, because it is possible that one thing is the end for multiple things. For we say: What is meant by 'end' is the equal (musawiya) end, so there is no problem. (Abd al-Rahim) And he only said 'its observance preserves' and did not say 'itself' instead of 'its observance', because logic itself does not preserve the mind from error, and it is not that harm befalls logic as a share, and it is not like that, for it may err due to neglect of the tool. (Shamsiyya) His saying: 'So here' — i.e., in this Hashiya, two matters from the three matters for which the introduction was roughly composed for their explanation are known: the explanation of the need for logic and its definition. The third matter remains, which is the explanation of its subject. So he said: 'And its subject: conceptual and assent-based known things, from the aspect of their leading to an unknown conceptual or assent-based' — meaning that it is necessary that they be in them.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining how logic is defined and what its subject is. The first gloss (Abd al-Rahim) clarifies that defining logic by its need (ghaya) is a definition by description (rasm), not by essential definition (hadd). The ghaya is a property (arad) that is coextensive (musawiya) with the thing defined, not a broader goal. The second gloss (Shamsiyya) explains why the author of the matn said 'its observance' (mura'atuha) preserves the mind rather than 'it itself' (nafsuha): logic itself is not the preserver; rather, observing the rules of logic preserves the mind. The third gloss states that in this Hashiya, the author has clarified two of three matters: the need for logic and its definition. The third matter, the subject of logic, is then given: it is known conceptual and assent-based matters (al-ma'lum al-tasawwuri wa al-tasdiqi) from the aspect of their leading to an unknown conceptual or assent-based matter.

Key Terms
رسم
غاية
موضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Determining the Subject of Logic: A Categorical Syllogism
Author uses a categorical syllogism to derive the subject of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism that establishes the subject of logic and explain how a universal rule about the subject of a science is applied.

Core Idea
The subject of logic is determined by applying a universal rule through a categorical syllogism.

In this passage, the commentator first establishes a universal principle: the subject of any science is that which is investigated for its essential accidents. He then applies this principle to logic via a categorical syllogism, concluding that conceptual and assented knowledge are the subject of logic. This method demonstrates how logical reasoning grounds the definition of a science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal rule
Find the statement that defines what counts as the subject of a science. The commentator says: 'كل ما يبحث في العلم عن العوارض الذاتية له فهو موضوعه' (Everything that is investigated in a science regarding its essential accidents is its subject).
This rule is presented as a given definition.
2
Locate the minor premise
Identify what is investigated in logic: 'المعلوم التصوري او التصديقي يبحث في المنطق عن العوارض الذاتية لهما' (Conceptual or assented knowledge is investigated in logic regarding its essential accidents). This is the specific claim about logic.
3
Form the categorical syllogism
Combine the universal rule (major premise) with the specific claim (minor premise) to form a syllogism: Major: Everything investigated in a science regarding its essential accidents is its subject. Minor: Conceptual and assented knowledge are investigated in logic regarding their essential accidents. Conclusion: Therefore, conceptual and assented knowledge are the subject of logic.
The middle term is 'investigated in a science regarding its essential accidents'.
4
Verify validity and figure
Check that the conclusion follows necessarily. This is a first-figure syllogism with two universal affirmative premises (AAA), which is valid (Barbara). The middle term is the subject of the major premise and the predicate of the minor premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۷۲) قوله موضوع العلم انما تصدى المحشى اولاً لتعريف موضوع مطلق العلم دون علم المنطق خاصة كما هو المناسب ليحصل بذلك قاعدة كلية منطبقة على جزئياتها حتى يحصل الأمر في تعيين موضوع العلم فيكون امراً محققاً ثابتاً بالدليل وترتيب القياس، كان يقال مثلاً: المعلوم التصوري او التصديقي يبحث في المنطق عن العوارض الذاتية لهما وكل ما يبح فيه عن العوارض الذاتية له فهو موضوعه لان كل ما يبحث في العلم عن العوارض الذاتية له فهو موضوعه فينتج انهما موضوع المنطق، فافهم.
(72) His statement: The subject of a science. The commentator (al-muhashshi) first undertook to define the subject of absolute science, not the science of logic specifically, as is appropriate, so that thereby a universal rule applicable to its particulars is obtained, so that the matter of determining the subject of a science becomes a verified matter established by evidence and the arrangement of a syllogism. For example, it is said: Conceptual or assented knowledge is investigated in logic regarding its essential accidents, and everything that is investigated in [a science] regarding its essential accidents is its subject, because everything that is investigated in a science regarding its essential accidents is its subject. So it follows that they (conceptual and assented knowledge) are the subject of logic. So understand.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the definition of the subject of a science (mawdu' al-'ilm). The commentator (al-muhashshi) first aims to define the subject of absolute science (mawdu' mutlaq al-'ilm) rather than the specific subject of logic. The reason is to establish a universal rule (qa'ida kulliyya) that can then be applied to particular sciences. This rule is: 'The subject of any science is that which is investigated in that science concerning its essential accidents.' The author then demonstrates how to determine the subject of logic using a categorical syllogism. The syllogism is: (1) Conceptual and assented knowledge are investigated in logic regarding their essential accidents. (2) Everything that is investigated in a science regarding its essential accidents is its subject. (3) Therefore, conceptual and assented knowledge are the subject of logic. This syllogism uses the universal premise (2), which itself is derived from the definition of a science's subject. The author concludes with 'so understand' (fafham), emphasizing that this is a key methodological point.

Key Terms
موضوع العلم (mawḍū' al-'ilm): The subject matter of a science; that which is investigated in that science with respect to its essential accidents. Here defined as a universal category applicable to all sciences. [Note: Standard term; stable across Islamic logic and philosophy.]
عوارض ذاتية (a'rāḍ dhātiyya): Essential accidents; properties that belong to the subject as such and are investigated in the science. They are the focus of scientific inquiry. [Note: Stable; sometimes contrasted with 'arad gharib.]
معلوم تصوري (ma'lūm taṣawwurī): Conceptual knowledge; a simple concept (e.g., 'man', 'animal'). Here considered part of the subject of logic along with assented knowledge. [Note: Standard binary division of knowledge in Islamic logic.]
معلوم تصديقي (ma'lūm taṣdīqī): Assented knowledge; a proposition or judgment (e.g., 'the world is created'). Together with conceptual knowledge, forms the subject of logic. [Note: Standard term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the syllogism presented in this passage?
A) Logic investigates only assented knowledge.
B) Conceptual and assented knowledge are the subject of logic.
C) The subject of a science must be self-evident.
D) Logic is not a science because its subject is too broad.

Key Takeaway
The subject of logic is established as conceptual and assented knowledge through a valid categorical syllogism grounded in the universal definition of a science's subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a categorical syllogism to determine the subject of 'ilm al-kalam (theology) using the same universal rule: 'The subject of a science is that which is investigated regarding its essential accidents.' Hint: What does kalam investigate? (e.g., Allah's attributes, prophethood, etc.)

Understanding the Subject of a Science
Author clarifies the pronoun reference and gives examples of subjects of sciences (medicine, fiqh, nahw).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the subject (mawḍū') of a science and identify it in examples from medicine, fiqh, and nahw.

Core Idea
Every science has a distinct subject investigated regarding its essential accidents.

This passage clarifies what is meant by 'subject of a science' and provides concrete examples from three Islamic sciences. Understanding this definition is crucial for grasping how sciences are demarcated—each science has a specific subject and studies its essential properties, not peripheral matters. Let's break down the definition and see how it applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition given
The author states: 'the subject of every science is that which is investigated in that science regarding its essential accidents.' Find this formulation in the Arabic and understand its structure: subject (mawḍū'), investigation (baḥth), and essential accidents (a'rāḍ dhātiyya).
2
Analyze the components
What is meant by 'essential accidents'? These are the properties that are intrinsic to the subject within that discipline. For example, in medicine, health and disease are essential accidents of the human body. Not all properties of the body are studied—only those relevant to health and disease.
3
Examine the medicine example
The subject is the human body. The essential accidents are states of health and disease. The science of medicine investigates these states regarding causes, symptoms, treatments, etc.
4
Examine the fiqh example
The subject is the actions of legally responsible persons (mukallafin). The essential accidents are legal rulings: permissibility (ḥalāl), prohibition (ḥarām), validity (ṣaḥīḥ), and invalidity (fāsid). Fiqh investigates these rulings.
5
Examine the nahw example
The subject is the word (kalima) and speech (kalam). The essential accidents are inflection (i'rāb) and construction (binā'). Grammar studies these aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۷۴) الضمير المجرور فى قوله : «فيه» يعود الى العلم و فى قوله عن عوارضه الى الموصول، :یعنی ان موضوع كل علم ما يبحث فى ذلك العلم عن عوارضه الذاتية و ذلك كبدن الانسان لعلم الطب فانه باحث عن احواله من جهة الصحة والمرض وكافعال المكلفين لعلم الفقه فانه باحث عن احواله من حيث الحل والحرمة والصحة و الفساد و كالكلمة والكلام لعلم النحو فانه ناظر فيهما من حيث الأعراب والبناء. (محمد علی)
(74) The genitive pronoun in his statement 'fihi' (in it) refers to the science, and in his statement 'an a'radihi' (about its accidents) refers to the relative pronoun (al-mawsul). That is: the subject of every science is that which is investigated in that science regarding its essential accidents. That is like the human body for the science of medicine, for it investigates its states from the aspect of health and disease; and like the actions of legally responsible persons for the science of jurisprudence (fiqh), for it investigates their states from the aspect of permissibility and prohibition, validity and invalidity; and like the word and speech for the science of grammar (nahw), for it examines them from the aspect of inflection (i'rab) and construction (bina'). (Muhammad 'Ali)
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory note on the phrase 'fihi' (in it) and 'an a'radihi' (about its accidents) from the preceding discussion. The author clarifies that 'fihi' refers to the science, and 'an a'radihi' refers to the subject mentioned. He then reiterates the definition: the subject of every science is that which is investigated in that science regarding its essential accidents. To illustrate, he provides three concrete examples from Islamic sciences: (1) In medicine, the subject is the human body, and the science investigates its states concerning health and disease. (2) In fiqh, the subject is the actions of mukallafin (legally responsible persons), and it examines their states regarding legal rulings (halal/haram, sahih/fasid). (3) In grammar (nahw), the subject is the word (kalima) and speech (kalam), and it studies them from the perspective of i'rab (case endings) and bina' (invariability). These examples make the abstract definition tangible, showing that each science has a distinct subject and focuses on its essential accidental properties.

Key Terms
موضوع كل علم
عوارض ذاتية

Logic's Subject: Essence vs Accident
Refutes objection by distinguishing act from modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an object's essence and its accidental manner of operation in defining a science's subject.

Core Idea
Logic studies the how of conveying, not conveying itself.

A careful objection is raised: if logic investigates definition and proof insofar as they convey to the unknown, then it seems to study conveying itself, which would violate the principle that sciences study accidents of their subjects. The author refines the claim: the investigation is about the 'how' of conveying (kayfiyya al-īṣāl), which is an accident of definition and proof. This distinction preserves the integrity of logic's subject.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that logic's investigation of definition and proof from the aspect of conveying makes conveying part of the subject, yet sciences study accidents of subjects, not the subject itself. This would be a contradiction.
The objection relies on the principle that subjects are not studied per se in a science; only their accidents are.
2
Extract the author's response
The author denies the contradiction. He says that what is part of the subject is the absolute conveying (al-īṣāl al-muṭlaq), but the investigation is about the how of conveying (kayfiyya al-īṣāl), which is an accident of definition and proof.
The key move: distinguishing between the act (conveying) and the manner of the act (how it is done).
3
Analyze the logical distinction
The how (kayfiyya) is an accident (ʿaraḍ) that qualifies the act of conveying. So logic studies an accidental property of definition and proof, consistent with the principle that sciences investigate accidents of their subjects.
This distinction is a common tool in defining sciences: the subject is the thing, but the investigation is about its properties or modalities.
4
Apply to another science
Consider the science of usul al-fiqh: its subject is the evidences (adilla) of fiqh, but it investigates how these evidences lead to rulings (istidlāl). The 'how' is an accident of the evidences.
This exercise reinforces the conceptual tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال: أن البحث عن المعرف والحجة من هذه الحيثية هو البحث عن الإيصال بعينه، وهو ينا في ما تقدم آنفاً من أن موضوع المنطق هو المعرف والحجة من حيث إيصالهما إلى المجهول، فإن ذلك نص في كون الإيصال من تتمة الموضوع، ولا ريب أن الموضوع وأجزائه لا يبحث في العلم عن أنفسهما بل عن الأحوال العارضة لهما كما تقدم إليه الإشارة. لأنا نقول: ما وقع قيداً للموضوع وتتمة له هو نفس الإيصال، وهذا يدل على أن البحث في هذا العلم عن كيفية الإيصال لا عن الإيصال نفسه، وهي من الأحوال العارضة له كما هو ظاهر، فلا يحتاج إلى ما قبل من أن ما جعل من تتمة الموضوع هو الإيصال المطلق، والمراد هنا أنه يبحث في العلم عن الإيصالات.
It should not be said: 'The investigation about definition and proof from this aspect is the investigation about conveying (īṣāl) itself, and it contradicts what preceded that the subject of logic is definition and proof insofar as they convey to the unknown, for that is text in making conveying part of the subject, and there is no doubt that the subject and its parts are not investigated in the science about themselves, but rather about the accidental states that occur to them, as previously indicated.' For we say: 'What occurred as a condition of the subject and a completion of it is the conveying itself, and this indicates that the investigation in this science is about the how of conveying (kayfiyya al-īṣāl), not about the conveying itself, and it is among the accidental states occurring to it, as is apparent. So there is no need for what preceded that what was made part of the subject is absolute conveying, and what is meant here is that the science investigates conveyances.'
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange. An objection is raised: if logic investigates definition and proof insofar as they convey to the unknown, then it seems logic investigates conveying itself, which would be part of the subject, not an accident. But the author refutes this: the qualification 'insofar as they convey' is indeed part of the subject, but the investigation is not about conveying itself; rather it is about the 'how' of conveying (kayfiyya al-īṣāl). The how of conveying is an accident of definition and proof, not the act of conveying. Thus the investigation is about accidental states, consistent with the principle that sciences investigate accidents of their subjects.

Key Terms
الإيصال (al-īṣāl): conveying; the act of bringing the unknown to knowledge.
الكيفية (al-kayfiyya): modality or how; the manner of conveying, considered an accident.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Logic investigates the how of conveying, an accident of definition and proof, not conveying itself.

Does Logic Study Definition and Proof Themselves or Their Accidents?
Commentator refutes an objection that logic's subject includes definition and proof themselves rather than their accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the position that logic investigates the accidental states of definition and proof, specifically the modality of their delivery.

Core Idea
Logic studies the how of intellectual delivery, not the what of definition and proof.

In this passage, the commentator responds to a subtle objection: if logic's subject includes definition and proof 'from the perspective of their delivering' to the unknown, then delivery itself becomes part of the subject. But no science investigates its own subject's components; it only investigates their accidental states. How does the commentator resolve this? Follow his reasoning to understand the precise scope of logical inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the objection in the Arabic: it claims that investigating definition and proof from the perspective of their delivering is investigating delivery itself, which would make delivery part of the subject, contradicting the rule that a science does not investigate its subject's parts.
The objection is introduced by 'la yuqal' (it should not be said) – a typical dialectical device.
2
Analyze the commentator's response
The commentator says that what is a condition/completion of the subject is delivery itself, but the investigation is about the modality (kayfiyya) of delivery, not delivery itself. Modality is an accident of delivery.
Notice the phrase 'al-bahth fi hadha al-ilm an kayfiyyat al-isal la an al-isal nafsihi'.
3
Distinguish between absolute delivery and modality
The earlier statement mentioned 'absolute delivery' (al-isal al-mutlaq) as a completion, but here the focus is on the modality. Reflect on the difference between the thing itself and its how.
This distinction is crucial: absolute delivery is the essence; modality is an accident.
4
Apply the distinction to the definition of logic
Recognize that logic's subject is definition and proof, but logic investigates their accidental states, specifically the how of their delivery. This resolves the objection without contradiction.
The commentator ends with 'fa-ta'ammal' (so reflect), indicating careful thought is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۸۰) لايقال : أن البحث عن المعرف والحجة من هذه الحيثية هو البحث عن الايصال بعينه وهو ينا في ما تقدم آنفاً من ان موضوع المنطق هو المعرف والحجة من حيث ايصالهما إلى المجهول فان ذلك نص فى كون الايصال من تتمة الموضوع ولاريب ان الموضوع واجزائه لا يبحث في العلم عن انفسهما بل عن الاحوال العارضة لهما كما تقدم اليه الاشارة. لانا نقول: ما وقع قيداً للموضوع وتتمة له هو نفس الايصال وهذا يدل على ان البحث في هذا العلم عن كيفية الايصال لاعن الايصال نفسه وهى من الاحوال العارضة له كما هو ظاهر فلا يحتاج الى ما قبل من ان ما جعل من تتمة الموضوع هو الايصال المطلق و المراد هنا انه يبحث في العلم عن الايصالات في مقدمة علم المنطق المخصوصة فتأمل. (محمد علی)
(80) It should not be said: 'The investigation of the definition and proof from this perspective is the investigation of the delivery itself, which contradicts what was mentioned earlier that the subject of logic is the definition and proof from the perspective of their delivering to the unknown; for that is clear in that delivery is part of the subject, and there is no doubt that the subject and its parts are not investigated in a science regarding themselves, but rather regarding their accidental states, as indicated previously.' For we say: What became a condition for the subject and a completion of it is the delivery itself, and this indicates that the investigation in this science is about the modality of delivery, not about delivery itself, and that is among its accidental states, as is evident. So it does not need what came before that what is made a completion of the subject is absolute delivery, and what is meant here is that it investigates in the science about deliveries in the special introduction of the science of logic, so reflect. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement regarding whether logic investigates definition and proof (al-mu'arraf wa al-hujja) themselves or their accidents. The commentator refutes a potential objection. The objection states: investigating definition and proof from the perspective of their delivering (al-isal) to the unknown is investigating the delivery itself, which contradicts the earlier statement that the subject of logic is definition and proof from the perspective of their delivering. That would mean delivery is part of the subject, and parts of the subject are not investigated in the science; rather, their accidental states are. The commentator answers by clarifying that what is a condition and completion of the subject is delivery itself, but the investigation in logic is about the modality (kayfiyya) of delivery, not delivery itself. The modality is an accidental state of delivery. Thus, the investigation is about the accidents of the subject's parts, not the parts themselves. He notes that the earlier formulation had 'absolute delivery' (al-isal al-mutlaq) as the completion, but here it is about deliveries in the special introduction of logic. He ends with 'fa-ta'ammal' (so reflect), indicating careful consideration.

Logic's True Subject: Meanings, Not Words
Commentator argues that logic's true subject is meanings, not words; words are merely means.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the subject of logic being meanings rather than words, and explain the incidental role of words using the commentator's argument.

Core Idea
Logic studies meanings; words are mere vehicles.

A common misconception is that logic studies words because logicians talk about 'definitions' and 'syllogisms' using verbal expressions. This passage critically refines that view: the true subject of logic is the meanings behind those expressions. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim being refined
Find the claim that the subject of logic is words. The commentator calls this a mistaken view (zalim). Mark the phrases where he states 'the truth is that the subject of logic is the meanings'.
The term 'al-haqq' (the truth) signals the correct position.
2
Examine the first example: definition
The passage says 'definition' (al-mu'arraf) is applied to 'rational animal', which is a meaning. The word 'animal' is the genus and 'rational' is the differentia, both parts of the meaning. Distinguish the meaning from the word itself.
This shows that when logicians say 'definition', they refer to the meaning, not the utterance.
3
Examine the second example: syllogism
The passage says 'syllogism' is applied to the form 'every J is B and every B is A'. This is a pattern of meanings, not a specific set of words. The variables J, B, A stand for terms that could be replaced by any content.
The logical notation confirms that the syllogism is an abstract meaning structure.
4
Analyze the reasoning: naming by way of the signified
The commentator explains that words like 'definition' are names of meanings, but because words indicate meanings, we sometimes call the words by the names of the meanings. This is a kind of metonymy (tasmīyat al-dāll bi-ism al-madlūl).
This is why the opponents were confused: they mistook the vehicle for the subject.
5
Conclude: the subject is meanings, words are incidental
The commentator asserts that attention to words is 'only incidental and by way of being a means' (bi al-'araḍ wa al-ṭarīqiyya). The true subject (mawḍū') of logic is the meanings (ma'ānī).
This distinction protects logic from being reduced to grammar or lexicography.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۸۱) فيكون من باب تسمية الدال باسم المدلول وفيه وفى قوله لانها تصير سببا الخ، اشارة الى ان المعرف والحجة اللذين هما موضوع العلم عبارتان في الحقيقة عن معنييهها لظهور ان ما يبين و يعرف حال المجهول التصورى مثلاً هو المعانى لا الالفاظ لكنه لما كانت الالفاظ طريقة الى المعانى سميت باسمها وفي هذا ردّ على من زعم ان موضوع المنطق هو الالفاظ من حيث أنها تدل على المعانى وذلك لما رأوا لانهم يطلقون المعرف على الحيوان الناطق» مثلاً والجنس على الجزء الأول منه (اى الحيوان والفصل على الجزء الاخير منه اى) الناطق) وكذا يطلقون القياس على قولنا كل ج، «ب» و «كل بالف» مثلاً والصغرى على القضية الأولى والكبرى على القضية الأخرى زعماً منهم ان تلك الاسماء كلها بازاء تلك الالفاظ من حيث الدلالة على المعانى بل الحق ان موضوع المنطق هو المعانى و تلك الاسماء في الحقيقة بازائها ورعاية جانب الالفاظ انما هي بالعرض و الطريقية كما سيصرح به المحشي. (میرزا محمد علی)
(81) Thus it is from the category of naming the signifier by the name of the signified. And in it and in his statement 'because they become a cause, etc.' there is an indication that the definition and proof, which are the subject of the science, are expressions in reality for their meanings, because it is apparent that what explains and makes known the state of the unknown conceptual, for example, is the meanings, not the words. However, since words are a path to the meanings, they are named by their names. And in this is a refutation of those who claim that the subject of logic is words from the perspective that they indicate meanings. That is because they see that they apply the term 'definition' to 'rational animal', for example, and 'genus' to its first part (i.e., 'animal'), and 'differentia' to its last part (i.e., 'rational'), and likewise they apply 'syllogism' to our statement 'every J is B' and 'every B is A', for example, and 'minor premise' to the first proposition and 'major premise' to the other, claiming that all those names correspond to those words from the perspective of indicating meanings. Rather, the truth is that the subject of logic is the meanings, and those names in reality correspond to them, and attention to the side of words is only incidental and by way of being a means, as the commentator (al-muhashi) will explicitly state. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage critically refines the view that the subject of logic is words. The commentator argues that when we say 'definition' we mean the meaning like 'rational animal', not the word. He explains that terms like 'genus' and 'differentia' are applied to the parts of the meaning (animal and rational), not to the words themselves. Similarly, 'syllogism' refers to the argument form, not the verbal expression. Those who claim logic is about words mistakenly think that because we use words like 'definition', 'genus', 'syllogism' to refer to verbal expressions that indicate meanings, the subject is words. But the truth is that these names stand for the meanings, and the linguistic aspect is merely incidental (bi al-'arad) and instrumental (bi al-tariqiyya). The commentator ends by noting that this will be clarified later by the commentator (al-muhashi). This is a defense of the standard position that logic deals with mental concepts, not linguistic expressions.

Logic's Subject is Meanings, Not Words
Commentators refute the view that logic's subject is words and establish it as meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the claim that logic's subject is words from the correct position that it is meanings, and identify the logical error of confusing signifier with signified.

Core Idea
Logic is about meanings, not words; words are just vehicles.

In this passage, the commentator refutes a common misconception: that logic deals with words because logicians speak of 'genus', 'differentia', and 'syllogism'. He shows that these terms actually refer to the underlying meanings. The mistake arises from confusing the signifier (daal) with the signified (madlool). This distinction is crucial for understanding how definitions and proofs function in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistaken claim
Find the view being refuted: some claim that logic's subject is words because logicians use terms like 'genus' for 'animal', 'differentia' for 'rational', and 'syllogism' for verbal formulas. The commentator rejects this.
2
Understand signifier vs. signified
The passage explains that naming the signifier by the signified is a common figure of speech. In logic, terms like 'definition' and 'proof' refer to meanings, not words. Words are merely a path (tariqa) to meanings.
This is key to the refutation.
3
Analyze the cause-effect naming
The commentator illustrates with 'hujja' (proof) meaning 'overcoming the opponent'. The effect (overcoming) gives its name to the cause (proof). This shows that the real subject is the meaning, not the word.
Similar to calling rain 'vegetation' because it causes vegetation.
4
Apply to a real kalam argument
The passage gives the example of proving the world's temporal origination: 'the world is changing, everything changing is temporally originated'. The proof is the meaning of these premises, not their wording. If words were the subject, the argument would only work for those who know the language.
This reinforces that logic deals with universal meanings.

It is from the category of naming the signifier by the signified, and in it and in his statement 'because they become a cause etc.' there is an indication that the definition and the proof, which are the subject of the science, are in reality expressions of their meanings, because it is clear that what explains and makes known the state of the conceptual unknown, for example, is the meanings, not the words; but because words are a path to meanings, they are named by their names. And in this is a refutation of those who claim that the subject of logic is words insofar as they signify meanings, because they see that they apply the term 'definition' to 'rational animal' for example, and 'genus' to its first part (i.e., animal) and 'differentia' to its last part (i.e., rational), and likewise they apply 'syllogism' to our saying 'every J is B and every A is J' for example, and 'minor premise' to the first proposition and 'major premise' to the other, claiming that all those names correspond to those words in terms of signifying meanings. Rather the truth is that the subject of logic is the meanings, and those names are in reality in correspondence to them, and consideration of the aspect of words is only accidental and instrumental, as the commentator will clarify. His statement: 'from the category of naming the cause' means that the proof is: overcoming the opponent; and since this known assent (tasdiq) is a cause for that overcoming, it is named by its name, naming the cause by the name of the effect, just as they call rain 'vegetation' in their saying 'the sky rained vegetation' because rain is a cause for it. And that is because what explains and makes known the quiddity of man, for example, in the statement 'man is a rational animal' is the meaning of 'rational animal', not its wording; otherwise that definition would have to be obtained for someone who is also not aware of the convention. And similarly, what is a proof and a cause for overcoming the opponent in proving the temporal origination of the world, for example, is the meaning of our statement 'the world is changing and everything changing is temporally originated', not its wording, and an indication has preceded.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary refuting the view that the subject matter of logic (mantiq) is words (alfaz). The commentators argue that logic is actually about meanings (ma'ani). They begin by explaining that the terms 'definition' (had) and 'proof' (hujja) mentioned in logic are in reality about the meanings, not the words. For example, when we say 'man is a rational animal', the definition is the meaning of 'rational animal', not the phrase itself. Words are merely vehicles (tariqa) to meanings, so they are called by the names of those meanings. This is a refutation of those who think logic deals with words insofar as they signify meanings. The text notes that some people, seeing that logicians apply terms like 'genus' to 'animal' and 'differentia' to 'rational', and 'syllogism' to a formula like 'every J is B, every A is J', mistakenly believe those terms refer to the words. The correct view is that the subject is the concepts themselves, and words are secondary and instrumental. The commentator then gives another example: naming the proof (hujja) as 'overcoming the opponent' is like naming the cause by the effect, such as calling rain 'vegetation' because it causes vegetation. Finally, the text reinforces that in proving the temporal origination of the world, the argument (e.g., 'the world is changing, everything changing is temporally originated') is about the meanings, not the words, as the words are only understood by those familiar with the convention (wad').

What Is Mantiq?

The Necessity of Mutabaqa in Tadammun and Iltizam
Author critiques objection that tadammun/iltizam exist without mutabaqa, defends majority view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the relationship between mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam according to the majority view, and defend the necessity of mutabaqa even when it is not intended.

Core Idea
Mutabaqa is always present when a word signifies, even if neither speaker nor hearer intends it.

In this lesson, we examine a subtle objection: can tadammun and iltizam exist without mutabaqa? The author of the hashiya defends the majority view that mutabaqa is always present, even when attention is diverted to a part or concomitant. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the three significations in Islamic legal theory and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the statement 'فان قلت' and note the claim: tadammun and iltizam do not imply mutabaqa because they can exist when a word is used for a part or concomitant with a contextual indicator.
The objector assumes that contextual indicators remove mutabaqa entirely.
2
Analyze the response
The author replies that the contextual indicator only prevents intention of mutabaqa, not its existence. The word still signifies its full meaning due to knowledge of the convention.
Key distinction: existence vs. intention of signification.
3
Distinguish the two schools
Compare the majority view (significations absolute) with the view of some (significations only when word used for proper meaning with primary intention). Note how they differ on when tadammun/iltizam occur.
The majority allows tadammun/iltizam even when the word is used metaphorically for a part.
4
Resolve the problem for the majority
Understand why the majority view initially seems to allow tadammun/iltizam without mutabaqa. Then see how the author's answer 'even estimatively' (تقديراً) preserves the necessity of mutabaqa.
Mutabaqa is present in potentiality or estimation, even if not actually intended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان قلت: التضمن والالتزام لا يستلزمان المطابقة لانها قد يوجدان كما اذا استعمل اللفظ في جزء الموضوع له او لازمه و نصب قرينة صارفة عن ارادة الموضوع له . قلت: القرينة انما تمنع عن ارادة الدلالة المطابقة لا عن وجودها فالدلالة المطابقة متحققة لوجود العلم بالوضع لكنها ليست بمرادة فافهم. اعلم انه اختلف كلماتهم في تفسير الدلالات فالمشهور عند الجمهور ما اشار اليه المحشى (ره) من ان المطابقة دلالة اللفظ على تمام معناه الحقيقى مطلقا سواء كان مرادا منه ام لا والتضمنية دلالته على جزئه مطلقا ايضاً والالتزامية دلالته على لازمه مطلقا ايضاً وذهب بعضهم الى ان المطابقة دلالته على تمام ما وضع له حقيقة أو حكما والتضمنية دلالته على جزئه والالتزامية دلالته على لازمه بشرط ان يكون استعماله فيما وضع له فى الثلاثة ويكون هو مرادا منه بالاصالة و ان قصد الجزء أو اللازم بالتبع وعلى هذا اذا استعمل اللفظ فى الجزء او الخارج اللازم و يراد هو من اللفظ بالاصالة فلا تسمى هذه بالتضمن او الالتزام بل بالمطابقة بخلافه على ما ذكره المحشى من مذهب الجمهور فان الاولى داخلة تحت التضمن والثانية تحت الالتزام كما هو ظاهر. اذا تمهد هذا فنقول: كون المطابقة لازمة للتضمن والالتزام على ما ذكره البعض من تفسير هما ظاهر، فانه لابد ان يستعمل اللفظ اولاً في الكل أو الملزوم فيفهم منه الجزء أو اللازم بالتبع و أما على مذهب الجمهور ففيه خفاء لانه اذا استعمل اللفظ في الجزء او اللازم بسبب الاشتهار او القرائن الصارفة يصدق عليه على مذهبهم تعريف التضمن او الالتزام و ليس هناك دلالة مطابقة اصلاً والمصنف لما اختار هذا المذهب تصدى الى الجواب بقوله: «ولو تقديراً» على ما فسره المحشى فلا تغفل و هيهنا كلام لا يسعها المقام.
If you say: Tadammun and iltizam do not necessarily imply mutabaqa because they can exist [without it], as when the word is used for a part of its subject matter or a necessary concomitant and a contextual indicator is set up diverting from intending the subject matter. I say: The contextual indicator only prevents intending the signification of mutabaqa, not its existence; for the signification of mutabaqa is realized due to the existence of knowledge of the convention, but it is not intended—so understand. Know that their statements differ in interpreting the significations. The well-known [view] among the majority is what the commentator referred to: that mutabaqa is the word's signification of its entire real meaning absolutely, whether it is intended or not; tadammun is its signification of a part of it absolutely; and iltizam is its signification of a necessary concomitant of it absolutely. And some held that mutabaqa is its signification of what it was posited for, really or virtually; tadammun is its signification of a part; and iltizam is its signification of a necessary concomitant, on condition that the word be used for what it was posited for in all three, and that [the whole] be intended primarily, and the part or concomitant be intended secondarily. Accordingly, if the word is used for a part or an external necessary concomitant and that is intended primarily from the word, then this is not called tadammun or iltizam but rather mutabaqa, contrary to what the commentator mentioned of the majority view, for the first falls under tadammun and the second under iltizam, as is apparent. Once this is established, we say: The fact that mutabaqa is necessary for tadammun and iltizam according to what some mentioned in their interpretation is evident, for the word must first be used for the whole or the melzum so that the part or the necessary concomitant is understood secondarily. As for the majority view, there is obscurity in it, because if the word is used for a part or a necessary concomitant due to fame or diverting contextual indicators, then according to their view the definition of tadammun or iltizam applies to it, and there is no signification of mutabaqa at all. And since the author chose this school, he set out to answer by saying: 'even estimatively', as the commentator interpreted it, so be not heedless. And there is here a discourse that the space does not accommodate.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the hashiya author (Yazdi) on the nature of the three significations: mutabaqa (correspondence), tadammun (inclusion), and iltizam (implication). The commentator first presents an objection: tadammun and iltizam can exist without mutabaqa, as when a word is used metaphorically for a part of its meaning or a concomitant, with a contextual indicator preventing the intended meaning. The response is that the contextual indicator only prevents the intention of mutabaqa, not its existence; the mutabaqa signification is still present because knowledge of the convention remains, even if not intended. The passage then explains two schools: the majority holds that the three significations occur absolutely, regardless of intention; another group holds that they occur only when the word is used for its proper meaning and the whole is intended primarily. According to the latter, using a word for a part or concomitant would be mutabaqa (if that part/concomitant is what the word is conventionally used for, either really or virtually), not tadammun/iltizam. The author notes that on the majority view, if a word is used for a part or concomitant due to fame or contextual indicators, the definitions of tadammun/iltizam apply, but there is no mutabaqa at all. This poses a problem, and the author (the original commentator) answered by saying 'even estimatively' (تقديراً), meaning that mutabaqa is present in potentiality or estimation. The passage concludes that there is more to say but space is limited.

Key Takeaway
The majority view holds that mutabaqa is always present as a signification, even when not intended, because the word still relates to its conventional meaning.

What Is Mantiq?
