The Author's Response on Hypothetical Indication (التقدير)
Author explains the majority view and his response that tadammun/iltizam imply mutabaqa hypothetically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the relationship between mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam, and explain how the author preserves mutabaqa hypothetically in cases of partial or concomitant usage.

Core Idea
Even when a word is used for a part or concomitant, mutabaqa indication remains hypothetically.

In this passage, the commentator delves into the nuances of the three types of indication. The majority view strictly separates them—when a word is used for a part or necessary concomitant due to common usage, only tadammun or iltizam apply, and mutabaqa is completely absent. However, the author of the matn refines this by asserting that mutabaqa is never truly absent; it is present hypothetically. Understanding this subtlety is crucial for correctly analyzing how words signify in definitions and logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of indication
List the three types: mutabaqa (whole meaning), tadammun (part of meaning), iltizam (necessary concomitant). Note that mutabaqa is primary.
These are standard categories in Islamic logic.
2
Understand the majority view
The majority (al-jumhūr) holds that when a word becomes commonly used for a part or concomitant (via ishtihār or qarā'in), the usage qualifies as tadammun or iltizam, and there is no mutabaqa indication at all.
This view treats the usage as a shift away from the original whole meaning.
3
Locate the author's response
The author of the matn chose the majority view but responded with 'wa law taqdīran' (even hypothetically). Find this phrase in the passage and note the glossator's explanation.
The response is a refinement, not a rejection.
4
Analyze the hypothetical preservation
The response means that even when a word is used for a part or concomitant, a hypothetical mutabaqa indication still exists. This ensures that the term's primary meaning is never fully abandoned.
This preserves the unity and coherence of the definition.
5
Apply to the examples given
Consider 'insān' used only for 'animal' (part, tadammun) or only for 'laughter' (concomitant, iltizam). Explain how mutabaqa is still hypothetically present in these usages.
The examples illustrate the practical application of the principle.

Regarding indications: the word is used primarily for the whole or the necessary concomitant, so the part or the consequent is understood secondarily. As for the opinion of the majority, there is subtlety in it, because if the word is used for the part or the consequent due to common usage or contextual indicators, then according to their school, the definition of tadammun or iltizam applies to it, and there is no mutabaqa indication at all. When the author chose this school, he undertook to answer by saying: 'even hypothetically (wa law taqdīran)', as explained by the glossator. Do not be heedless. Here there is discourse that cannot be accommodated in this context. (Muhammad Ali) (29) Example of the first: 'insān' if it becomes common only for 'animal' or only for 'rational'. Example of the second: also if it becomes common for 'laughter' for example. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on the indication types. It begins by explaining the norm: a word is originally used for the whole or the necessary concomitant, and the part or the logical consequent is understood secondarily. The 'majority' school (al-jumhūr) holds that when a word is used for a part or a necessary concomitant due to common usage (ishtihār) or contextual indicators (qarā'in), that usage qualifies as tadammun (implication of part) or iltizām (implication of concomitant) respectively, and there is no mutābaqa (primary indication) at all. The author of the main text (al-muṣannif) chose this majority view and responded with the phrase 'wa law taqdīran' (even hypothetically), as interpreted by the glossator (al-muḥashshī). This means that even in such cases, there is a hypothetical mutābaqa indication. The commentator warns not to overlook this. Then two examples are given: the word 'insān' (human) might become commonly used for only 'animal' or only 'rational' (its parts), illustrating tadammun; or for 'laughter' (a necessary concomitant), illustrating iltizām.

What Is Mantiq?

Tadammun and Iltizam Do Not Necessarily Imply Mutabaqa
Commentary explains that tadammun and iltizam do not necessarily imply mutabaqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when mutabaqa indication can exist without tadammun or iltizam, and explain why the reverse implication does not hold.

Core Idea
Mutabaqa does not guarantee tadammun or iltizam; simple meanings lack parts and concomitants.

In Islamic logic, the three types of indication—mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam—are intimately connected, but not in a one-way street. The commentator refines the relationship by explaining that tadammun and iltizam do not necessarily follow from mutabaqa. This is crucial for precise analysis of terms in Islamic texts, preventing over-interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three types of indication
Define mutabaqa (word for whole meaning), tadammun (word for a part), and iltizam (word for a necessary concomitant). The commentator's claim is that the second two do not automatically accompany the first.
These are standard definitions from the matn.
2
Understand the commentator's claim
The phrase 'wa la 'aks' means the reverse implication fails: from the existence of tadammun or iltizam, you cannot infer that mutabaqa is also present. But here the focus is the opposite direction: tadammun/iltizam do not necessarily imply mutabaqa.
The passage clarifies that the absence of parts or external concomitants can break the entailment.
3
Consider the case of simple meanings
If a meaning has no parts (e.g., 'unity') or no necessary concomitant, then mutabaqa exists alone. Tadammun and iltizam are impossible, even hypothetically.
Examples: 'unity' (al-wahda) and 'point' (al-nuqta) are given.
4
Examine the counterarguments
The commentator addresses objections: the mere possibility of absence does not prove non-entailment. He then provides a stronger argument: if entailment held, then whenever we conceive something, we would necessarily conceive its parts and concomitants—but we often conceive things without that.
Al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif accepts this argument conditionally.
5
Identify the practical implication
In analyzing Islamic texts, do not assume that a term's mutabaqa indication automatically brings its tadammun or iltizam. Each must be separately justified based on context and the nature of the meaning.
This prevents misinterpretation in fiqh and usul.

(30) His statement 'and not vice versa' means: tadammun and iltizam indications do not necessitate mutabaqa indication. For the meaning, even if it has parts or a distinguishing feature external to it, often the word posited for it is used and nothing is understood from it except the entire posited meaning without specifying its parts or its necessary concomitants. And the meaning for which the word is posited may be a simple thing having no part and no distinguishing feature external to it; here the two indications (tadammun and iltizam) are absent, even hypothetically. (al-Taqrib p. 20) (31) That is, neither rationally nor conventionally. (Muhammad Ali) (32) His statement: 'then mutabaqa is realized without tadammun' — as for its realization without tadammun, that is because if the meaning has no part, indication of the part is not realized so that tadammun could be realized. And as for its realization without iltizam, that is because if the meaning has no rational or conventional necessary concomitant such that if the word indicated it would be an iltizam indication, then iltizam also is not realized. But it is not correct that the utmost this proof yields is ignorance of the entailment, and that is not what is sought; rather what is sought is knowledge of non-entailment, and it does not yield that, for possibility does not entail actuality. Some took that as a doctrine regarding iltizam and argued for the non-entailment of tadammun by example of 'unity' and 'point', because mutabaqa exists in them without tadammun since the part is certainly absent.
And one may argue for non-entailment by way of certainty and knowledge: if entailment were realized, then whenever we conceive something we would conceive something else, but we know necessarily that we conceive many things while being heedless of their other accompaniments — we conceive with it.
Al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif said: if that claim is sound, then what he claimed of non-entailment is established; otherwise not. And perhaps one may argue for the non-entailment of iltizam specifically: if it entailed it, then the perception of infinite things would be necessary at one moment. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise. Explanation of the entailment: if for everything there is a necessary concomitant such that its conception is impossible without it—as is claimed—then it is necessary to conceive that concomitant when conceiving it; that concomitant is something, and it is supposed that it also has a necessary concomitant impossible to conceive without conceiving it; so that concomitant must also be conceived, and so on ad infinitum. The commentator of al-Mutāli' weakened this argument, and al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif followed him, because it is possible to end at a necessary concomitant whose necessary concomitant is some of its necessary conditions in one or more ranks, since there is no impossibility in mental mutual entailment, as in correlatives like fatherhood and sonship, because mutual entailment does not necessitate each one's dependence on the other such that it would be a vicious circle.
Al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif said: it cannot be said: if it does not end, the impossibility drops; and if it ends, the end is a concept and it is something, so it must have a necessary concomitant. For we say: the existence of the end does not necessitate its conception such that conception of its necessary concomitant would be necessitated. Some of the commentators of the text said: and the argument can be formulated in a way that removes that from it. Its explanation: if we conceive its quiddity, if it has no mental necessary concomitant, the object is achieved; if it has a mental necessary concomitant, we shift the discourse to the sum of the entailing and the entailed, and we say: that sum is also a quiddity; if it has another necessary concomitant, we shift the discourse to the sum of the two entailers, and so on. And if it has no other necessary concomitant, the object is achieved. End. And I say: what is in it is not hidden, for it is clear that conceiving the entailing and the entailed does not require conceiving their sum, such that from conceiving it, conception of another necessary concomitant would be necessitated, and so on. So understand.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the statement 'wa lā 'aks' (and not vice versa), which means that tadammun and iltizam indications do not necessarily imply mutabaqa indication. The commentator explains that even if a meaning has parts or external distinguishing features, often the word is used for the whole meaning without specifically indicating the parts or necessary concomitants. Moreover, if the meaning is simple (having no parts or external distinguishing features), then tadammun and iltizam are absent entirely, even hypothetically. The commentator then addresses the claim that mutabaqa can exist without tadammun or iltizam. For tadammun, if a meaning has no parts, no indication of a part can occur. For iltizam, if a meaning has no rational or conventional necessary concomitant, iltizam is not realized. However, a counterargument is considered: the mere possibility of absence does not prove actual non-entailment. The commentator then presents a stronger argument from necessity: if tadammun and iltizam necessarily accompanied mutabaqa, then whenever we conceive something we would necessarily conceive its parts and concomitants, but we often conceive things while being oblivious to them. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif accepts this argument conditionally. Then a specific argument for iltizam is given: if iltizam were entailed, it would lead to an infinite regress in conception, which is absurd. But this is criticized because mental entailment can be reciprocal (like father and son) and can terminate. The passage ends with a formulation by some commentators that avoids the regress, followed by a final critical note that even that formulation does not guarantee entailment.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
10 min
Tadammun and Iltizam: Why They Do Not Imply Each Other
Commentator explains non-implication of tadammun and iltizam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish tadammun and iltizam and explain why they are not mutually implicative, using examples from Islamic semantics.

Core Idea
A whole's part does not force a necessary concomitant, and a simple entity can have one.

In this passage, the commentator explains a crucial distinction between two types of indication: tadammun (implication of a part by the word for the whole) and iltizam (implication of a necessary concomitant). While they may seem related, they are independent. Some scholars argued that tadammun necessarily implies iltizam, but the commentator identifies a subtle fallacy in that argument. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate this distinction and recognize the fallacy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of indication
Review the definitions of tadammun and iltizam. Tadammun is when a word indicates a part of its meaning (e.g., 'house' indicates 'room'). Iltizam is when a word indicates something necessarily associated (e.g., 'sun' indicates 'light'). Both are types of dalalat al-alfadh.
These distinctions are foundational in Islamic logic and usul al-fiqh.
2
Understand the claim of non-implication
The passage states that tadammun does not imply iltizam and vice versa. The reason: a composite meaning may lack any necessary concomitant, so tadammun can occur without iltizam. Conversely, a simple meaning can have a mental necessary concomitant, so iltizam can occur without tadammun.
Example of composite without necessary concomitant: 'human' (composite of animal and rational) might have no necessary external concomitant. Example of simple with necessary concomitant: 'sun' (simple orb) necessarily implies 'light'.
3
Analyze the counterargument
Some scholars argued that tadammun does imply iltizam. Their reasoning: when a word is applied to a composite meaning, the whole as whole and the part as part are understood. From this, the composition is necessarily understood, and that composition is an external necessary concomitant of the meaning. Therefore, tadammun necessitates iltizam.
This argument is a categorical syllogism: if understanding whole and part entails understanding composition, and composition is a necessary concomitant, then tadammun entails iltizam.
4
Identify the refutation and the fallacy
The commentator refutes this as a sophism: what is understood is the actual whole and part, not the whole qua whole or part qua part. We often conceive a composite without reflecting on its composition. The fallacy is 'confusing the accident with the substance' (ishrabah al-'arid bil ma'rud): the composition is an accidental concomitant of the act of conception, not a necessary property of the meaning itself.
This distinction is critical: the part indicates the whole, but does not necessarily indicate the relation of composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳۳) قوله ولو كان له معنى مركب هذا شروع لبيان النسبة بين الدلالة الالتزامية و التضمنية بعد ما بين النسبة بين الدلالة المطابقية وبين كل واحدة منهما ولم يتوجه المصنف الى هذا لكونه معلوما من :قوله ولا عكس» بالمقايسة كما لا يخفى على من له تأمل صادق و نظر دقيق.

ظاهر.

(33) His statement 'and if it had a composite meaning' — this is the beginning of explaining the relationship between iltizam and tadammun indications, after having explained the relationship between mutabaqa and each of them. The author did not attend to this because it is known from his statement 'and not vice versa' by comparison, as is not hidden from one who has sincere reflection and precise examination. He is Khan. Then the summary of that difference: tadammun does not necessitate iltizam and vice versa. As for the first: because it is possible that the word has a composite meaning without a necessary concomitant; then tadammun is realized without iltizam, as is apparent. As for the second: because it is possible that it has a simple meaning with a mental necessary concomitant whose conception is necessitated from its conception, like 'sun', for it is posited for the orb, and light is its necessary concomitant; then iltizam is realized without tadammun. Some held that tadammun necessitates iltizam, arguing that when the word is applied to the composite meaning, the mutabaqa meaning is understood — that is, the whole as whole — and the tadammun meaning — that is, the part as part. And when they are understood from this perspective, the composition is necessarily understood, and that is something external to the posited meaning and is a necessary concomitant of it. Thus tadammun necessitates iltizam. It was answered that this is a sophism of the type confusing the accident with the substance, for what is understood is that to which 'whole' and 'part' apply, not the whole as whole and the part as part, since we often conceive the composite while being heedless of...
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'wa law kāna lahu ma'nā murakkab' (and if it had a composite meaning). It begins by stating that this section explains the relationship between iltizam and tadammun, following the earlier explanation of the relationship between mutabaqa and each. The author of the main text did not elaborate because it is deducible from his previous statement 'and not vice versa'. The commentator then gives the core difference: tadammun does not imply iltizam, and vice versa. The reason for the first: a composite word may have no necessary concomitant, so tadammun occurs without iltizam. For example, a composite meaning like 'human' (which has parts 'animal' and 'rational') might have no mental necessary concomitant. The reason for the second: a simple meaning may have a mental necessary concomitant. For example, 'sun' is a simple entity, but 'light' is its necessary concomitant; thus, iltizam occurs without tadammun. Some scholars argued that tadammun does imply iltizam. Their argument: when a composite meaning is understood, the whole is understood as whole and the part as part. From this, the composition is necessarily understood, which is an external necessary concomitant of the meaning. Thus, tadammun necessitates iltizam. The commentator refutes this as a sophism: what is understood is the whole and part themselves, not the whole qua whole or part qua part. We often conceive a composite without explicitly thinking about its composition. The passage ends abruptly.

D) Because tadammun and iltizam are the same type of indication.

Key Takeaway
Tadammun and iltizam are independent types of indication; the former does not necessitate the latter, and vice versa, as shown by examples and the refutation of a common fallacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the word 'wudu' (ablution). It indicates the whole act (mutabaqa). Its parts include 'washing the face' (tadammun). Does 'wudu' also imply the necessary concomitant of 'intention' (iltizam)? Explain using the principle from this passage that tadammun does not necessitate iltizam.

Therefore, from "salat" we necessarily understand the obligation of ruku' as a necessary concomitant.' Use the commentator's refutation to show why this is mistaken.

Why Mutabaqa Does Not Necessarily Entail Iltizam: The Two Senses of Lāzim Bayyin
Author presents and refutes the imam's claim that mutabaqa entails iltizam via necessary concomitant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the specific and general senses of lāzim bayyin and explain why only the specific sense is relevant for dalāla iltizām.

Core Idea
Not every concomitant counts: only the self-evident one upon conception justifies iltizām.

In this passage, the author refutes the claim that every word's conformity (mutābaqa) automatically yields its implication (iltizām). The key is the nature of the necessary concomitant (lāzim bayyin). The Imam argued that every essence has at least one necessary concomitant, so the signifier must signify it. The author shows that this only works if the concomitant is self-evident in the specific sense, which is not true for all essences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Imam's claim
The Imam asserts that mutābaqa entails iltizām because every essence (māhiyya) has a necessary concomitant (lāzim bayyin), minimally that it is not other than itself, and the signifier of the signified signifies that concomitant.
Find the Arabic phrase: 'و ذهب الامام الى ان المطابقة يلزمها الالتزام'
2
Extract the two senses of lāzim bayyin
The author distinguishes two meanings: the specific sense (bayyin bi al-ma‘nā al-akhass) where the concomitant is conceived immediately upon conceiving the signified, and the general sense (al-a‘amm) where judgment of necessitation is required. Note the Arabic terms: 'البين بالمعنى الاخص' and 'البين بالمعنى الأعم'.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the refutation.
3
Analyze the refutation for each sense
The author rejects the specific sense as not universal: we often conceive essences without their concomitant properties coming to mind. He accepts the general sense but says it is insufficient for iltizām, because iltizām requires the concomitant to be directly implied without extra judgment.
The refutation uses the conditions of dalāla iltizām from earlier in the text.
4
Consider the objection and response
An objector argues that awareness of an essence requires distinguishing it from others, so the concomitant is necessary. The author denies this, stating that distinction can occur without explicit conception of the other; otherwise every conception would be an assent (taṣdīq), which is false.
This objection is a counterargument that the author refutes, preserving the strict definition of iltizām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ذهب الامام الى ان المطابقة يلزمها الالتزام مستدلا بان لكل ماهية لازماً بيناً واقله انها ليست غيرها والدال على الملزوم دال على لازمه البين بالالتزام. واجيب بانه ان اراد باللازم البين البين بالمعنى الاخص وهو ما يلزم تصوره من تصور الملزوم، فلا نسلم الكلية، لانا كثيراً ما نتصور ماهيات ولا يخطر ببالنا غيرها فضلاً عن انها ليست غيرها وان اراد به البين بالمعنى الأعم و هو ما يلزم من تصوره و تصور الملزوم و النسبة بينهما الجزم باللزوم، فمسلم لكنه لا يجدي لان المعتبر كما سبق فى الدلالة الالتزامية هو اللازم البين بالمعنى الاخص لاغير. فان قبل اذا حصل لنا شعور بماهية فلابد ان نميزها عن غيرها والا فلا شعور بها ضرورة ان المشعور به موجود في الذهن و كل ما هو موجود في الذهن مميز عن غيره وحفلابد من ان نتصور الغير لاستلزام التميز د تصور الغير. قلنا: تمتع الملازمة الجواز ان يكون الشيء مشعوراً به لنا من غير ان نتصور أنه مميز عن الغير والا لاستلزم كل تصور تصديقاً وهو باطل قطعاً . نعم هو يستلزم ان يكون متميزاً عن غيره نفسه و این هذا منه؟
And the Imam held that [dalāla] mutābaqa is necessarily accompanied by [dalāla] iltizām, arguing that every essence has a necessary concomitant (lāzim bayyin), the least of which is that it is not other than itself, and the signifier of the signified signifies its necessary concomitant by [dalāla] iltizām. And it was answered: if he intends by 'necessary concomitant' the self-evident in the specific sense (al-bayyin bi al-ma‘nā al-akhass), which is that its conception follows from the conception of the signified, then we do not accept the universality, for we often conceive essences without their others occurring to our minds, let alone that they are not others; and if he intends by it the self-evident in the more general sense (al-bayyin bi al-ma‘nā al-a‘amm), which is that from conceiving it and conceiving the signified and the relation between them, judgment of necessitation follows, then it is granted but it is of no avail, because what is considered in [dalāla] iltizām, as previously mentioned, is the necessary concomitant in the specific sense exclusively. If it is said: when we have awareness of an essence, we must distinguish it from others, otherwise there is no awareness of it, necessarily because that which is perceived exists in the mind, and everything that exists in the mind is distinct from others, so we must conceive the other because distinction entails conception of the other. We say: we deny the necessity; it is possible that something be perceived by us without our conceiving that it is distinct from others, otherwise every conception would entail assent, which is absolutely false. Indeed, it entails that it is distinct in itself from others, but what is that to the point?
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This is a dialectical exchange where the author refutes an argument by the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) that the denotation by conformity (dalāla mutābaqa) necessarily implies denotation by implication (dalāla iltizām). The Imam argues that every essence (māhiyya) has a necessary concomitant (lāzim bayyin), minimally that it is not something else, and the signifier of the signified signifies that concomitant. The response distinguishes between two senses of 'self-evident' (bayyin): the specific sense, where the concomitant is conceived immediately upon conceiving the signified, and the general sense, where judgment of the relationship is required. The refutation argues that the specific sense is not universal, as we often conceive essences without conceiving their distinctions. The general sense is granted but irrelevant, since iltizām requires the specific sense. A further objection is raised: awareness of an essence requires distinguishing it from others. The author denies this, noting that distinguishing does not require explicit conception of the other; otherwise every conception would be an assent (taṣdīq), which is false. The argument is carefully handled to preserve the strict understanding of iltizām.

Key Terms
لازم بين
دلالة الالتزام
دلالة المطابقة
البين بالمعنى الاخص
البين بالمعنى الأعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the specific sense of lāzim bayyin as universal?
A) Because it requires judgment of necessitation.
B) Because we often conceive essences without conceiving their concomitants.
C) Because the general sense is more accurate.
D) Because every conception entails assent.

Key Takeaway
Only the necessary concomitant that is self-evident upon conception (specific sense) qualifies for dalāla iltizām; the general sense, requiring judgment, does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'The sky is blue.' Does it imply 'The sky has a color' or 'The sky exists' via dalāla iltizām? Use the two senses of lāzim bayyin to determine which implications are valid and why.
?
In fiqh, does the term 'zina' (adultery) imply its prohibition? Identify which type of necessary concomitant this is (specific or general) and justify your answer using the conditions from this passage.

What Is Mantiq?

Why the Definition of Mufrad Specifies 'al-Lafz al-Mawdu''
Commentator explains why the author used 'اللفظ الموضوع' and avoids counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logicians restrict the definition of 'mufrad' to posited words (al-lafz al-mawdu') and identify which non-posited words this excludes.

Core Idea
A simple word is defined only for posited words to avoid including non-signifying or natural significations.

When logicians define a 'simple word' (mufrad), they do not mean any word, but only those words that have been deliberately posited to signify a meaning. The commentator explains that this restriction is necessary to avoid counterexamples such as meaningless sounds or words used by natural instinct. Understanding this refinement ensures that our definitions in logic are precise and free from objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original definition
Find the definition of mufrad that the commentator is discussing. He notes that some authors used just 'al-lafz' (the word) without qualification.
2
Spot the counterexample
The commentator says that if we used just 'al-lafz', the definition would be violated by words that are incomplete or do not signify meaning by convention, such as unintelligible utterances.
3
Understand the refinement
By adding 'al-mawdu'' (posited), we exclude all words that are not conventionally assigned a meaning. This includes natural cries, accidental sounds, and even words used metaphorically without proper positing.
4
Verify the outcome
Check that with this refinement, the definition now correctly covers only words that are deliberately assigned to a meaning, which are the proper subjects of logical analysis.
The commentator also addresses a potential objection about words posited for composition, but that is handled by the context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انما لم يقل: «واللفظ» كما عبر به البعض، لئلا ينتقض حد المفرد المشاراليه يقوله : « والانفرد بالالفاظ المهملة المستعملة من المشاهد الحاضر على وجه لايفهم لا يفهم منها معنى اصلاً و بالالفاظ الدالة على معانيها بحسب الطبع او النقل فانها لا تسمى في الاصطلاح الفاظاً مفردة (محمد علی) انما فسر بذلك ، دفعاً لما ربما يتوهم من ان قسمة الموضوع مطلقاً الى المركب و المفرد غير جايز لان الدوال الاربع وهو قسم من الموضوع لا يتصف بالافراد والتركيب اصلاً يعن ، اصلاً يعنى انه لم يجعل مطلق الموضوع مقسماً للمركب والمفرد حتى يرد ما ذكر بل نوعاً خاصاً منه وهو اللفظ الموضوع و ذلك بقرينة انه افرد البحث اولاً عن احوال اللفظ الموضوع حيث قال: دلالة اللفظ»، ولوسلم فاللفظ اذا اطلق يتبادر منه الفرد الاكمل. ثم المراد من الموضوع الموضوع لمعنى لعين ما ذكر فلايرد أيضاً أن اللفظ الموضوع لغرض التركيب لا يتصف بهما قط فكيف يصح قسمته مطلقا اليهما ؟. (محمد علی) قوله ان اريد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه يعنى بالدلالة : دلالة المطابقية كما قيد بذلك الكاتبي وغيره لا التضمنى والالتزامي خاصة ولا مجموع الثلاث ولا المطلق الشامل للثلاث اما الاولان فظاهر و اما الثالث فقيل لان المعتبر في تركيب اللفظ وافراده دلالة جزئه على جزء معناه المطابقى وعدم دلالته عليه خاصة لا دلالة جزئه على جزء معناه المطابق او التضمنى او الالتزامي وعدم دلالته عليه والالزم ان يكون اللفظ المركب من لفظين موضوعين بازاء معنيين بسيطين او بازاء معنى مر مركب له لازم ذهنی بسيط مركباً و مفرداً معاً فان جزئه يدل على جزء معناه المطابق ولا يدل على جزء معناه التضمنى او الالتزامي اذا المفروض ان كل واحد منهما بسيط لاجزء له ولا شك ان الدلالة على شيء فرع لوجود ذلك الشيء. واعترض عليه شارح الرسالة بان غاية ذلك ان يكون اللفظ بالقياس الى المعنى المطابق مركباً و بالقياس الى التضمني اوالالتزامي مفرداً ولا بأس بذلك كما ان اللفظ باعتبار معنيين مطابقيين يتصف بالافراد و التركيب كـ «عبدالله» باعتبار معناه العلمى والاضافي و الحيوان الناطق باعتبار معناه العلمي والوصفى فاذا جاز ذلك باعتبار معنيين مطابقيين فما ظنك بالمعنى المطابق و التضمني او الالتزامي ؟
He only did not say 'and the word' as some have expressed, to avoid the counterexample to the definition of the simple word indicated by his saying: 'and the simple is [restricted] to words that are not uttered in isolation'? Actually the phrase is unclear. The commentator says: He only interpreted it that way to ward off what might be imagined, that the division of subject-matter absolutely into compound and simple is not permissible because the four signifiers—which are a division of subject—are not characterized by simplicity or composition at all. That is, he did not make absolute subject the divisor into compound and simple so that the mentioned objection might arise, but rather a specific type of it, namely the posited word, and that by the context that he first devoted discussion to the states of the posited word where he said 'the denotation of the word'. And if it is granted, then the word, when used absolutely, is understood in its most complete sense. Then what is meant by 'posited' is posited for a meaning in the same sense, so it is not objected that the word posited for the purpose of composition is not characterized by either at all, so how is its division into them absolutely valid? (Muhammad Ali). His statement: 'if by a part of it is intended denotation of part of its meaning'—meaning by denotation: denotation of conformity, as restricted by al-Katibi and others, not specifically inclusive and implicative, nor the totality of the three, nor the absolute encompassing the three. As for the first two, it is obvious. As for the third, it is said because what is considered in the composition and simplicity of a word is the denotation of its part of part of its conforming meaning and the lack of such denotation, not the denotation of its part of part of its conforming, inclusive, or implicative meaning and the lack thereof. Otherwise, it would be necessary that a word composed of two words posited for two simple meanings or for a compound meaning having a simple mental concomitant be both compound and simple simultaneously, for its part denotes part of its conforming meaning but does not denote part of its inclusive or implicative meaning, since each of them is supposed to be simple with no parts, and there is no doubt that denotation of something is subsidiary to the existence of that thing. And the Sharh al-Risala objected: at most, that would make the word compound in relation to the conforming meaning and simple in relation to the inclusive or implicative meaning, and there is no harm in that, just as a word with two conforming meanings can be characterized by simplicity and composition, such as 'Abdallah' in its proper and relational meanings, and 'al-hayawan al-natiq' in its conceptual and descriptive meanings. If that is permissible with two conforming meanings, what do you think of conforming and inclusive/implicative meanings?
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (hashiya) on the definition of 'mufrad' (simple word) in logic. The author explains why the original text (by al-Katibi or similar) used the term 'al-lafz al-mawdu'' (the posited word) rather than just 'al-lafz' (the word) to avoid counterexamples like incomplete or non-signifying words. The commentator refines the definition: a 'mufrad' is a word no part of which indicates part of its meaning by conformity (dalāla mutābaqa), not by inclusion or implication. This precision avoids the paradox that a composite word might be both simple and compound if we consider the different types of denotation. The commentator then addresses an objection from 'Sharh al-Risala' that a word can be simple relative to one meaning and compound relative to another, which is acceptable. The example 'Abdallah' is used: as a proper name it is simple, but as a relational phrase (slave of God) it is compound. Similarly, 'al-hayawan al-natiq' is simple as a technical term but compound as a description. This shows that a word can have multiple aspects without contradiction. The passage demonstrates the logical rigor required in defining terms, typical of super-commentaries.

Key Terms
مفرد
اللفظ الموضوع
مركب

Checkpoint
Why did the commentator argue that the definition of mufrad must include the term 'al-mawdu'' (posited)?
A) To exclude words that are not conventionally assigned a meaning.
B) To include natural sounds as simple words.
C) To make the definition more complex.
D) To ensure that all words are considered compound.

Key Takeaway
The definition of mufrad is restricted to posited words to avoid including non-conventional significations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the word 'bismillah'. Is it mufrad or murakkab? Apply the definition of mufrad as posited word and check if any part indicates part of its meaning by conformity. Then determine whether the inclusion of 'al-mawdu'' changes the classification.

What Is Mantiq?

7 min
Why Only Conformity Denotation Determines Simplicity and Composition
Commentator specifies that 'جزء معناه' means by conformity denotation to avoid paradox.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logicians consider only conformity denotation (dalāla mutābaqa) when determining whether a word is simple or compound, and evaluate examples like 'Abdallah'.

Core Idea
Whether a word is simple or compound depends only on conformity denotation, not inclusive or implicative denotation.

The commentator refines the definition of mufrad by specifying that when we say 'no part indicates part of its meaning', we mean by the denotation of conformity (mutābaqa), not by inclusion (tadammun) or implication (iltizām). This prevents a logical paradox: a compound word could otherwise be both simple and compound. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper term classification in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition being refined
The base definition says 'if by a part of it is intended denotation of part of its meaning'. The commentator clarifies that 'denotation' here means only conformity denotation (mutābaqa).
2
Understand the potential paradox
If we allowed inclusion or implication denotation, a compound word made from two simple words could be considered both compound (by conformity, each part denotes its own meaning) and simple (by implication, the parts do not denote part of the compound meaning by inclusion).
3
Follow the resolution
The commentator insists that only conformity matters. So if a part denotes part of the meaning by conformity, the word is compound; if not, it is simple, regardless of other denotations.
4
Examine the counter-objection and resolution
The Sharh al-Risala objects that a word can be simple relative to one meaning and compound relative to another, which is fine. The commentator agrees using examples: 'Abdallah' is simple as a proper name (no part indicates part of that proper meaning by conformity) but compound as a descriptive phrase (each part indicates its own meaning by conformity).

Then what is meant by 'posited' is posited for a meaning in the same sense, so it is not objected that the word posited for the purpose of composition is not characterized by either at all, so how is its division into them absolutely valid? His statement: 'if by a part of it is intended denotation of part of its meaning'—meaning by denotation: denotation of conformity, as restricted by al-Katibi and others, not specifically inclusive and implicative, nor the totality of the three, nor the absolute encompassing the three. As for the first two, it is obvious. Commentary

The author explains why the original text (by al-Katibi or similar) used the term 'al-lafz al-mawdu'' (the posited word) rather than just 'al-lafz' (the word) to avoid counterexamples like incomplete or non-signifying words. The commentator refines the definition: a 'mufrad' is a word no part of which indicates part of its meaning by conformity (dalāla mutābaqa), not by inclusion or implication. This precision avoids the paradox that a composite word might be both simple and compound if we consider the different types of denotation. The commentator then addresses an objection from 'Sharh al-Risala' that a word can be simple relative to one meaning and compound relative to another, which is acceptable. The example 'Abdallah' is used: as a proper name it is simple, but as a relational phrase (slave of God) it is compound. Similarly, 'al-hayawan al-natiq' is simple as a technical term but compound as a description. This shows that a word can have multiple aspects without contradiction. The passage demonstrates the logical rigor required in defining terms, typical of super-commentaries.

Key Terms
مفرد
مركب
دلالة المطابقية
دلالة التضمنية
دلالة الالتزامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does the word composed of two simple words become both compound and simple if we consider inclusive denotation?
A) Because its parts denote part of the compound meaning by conformity.
B) Because its parts denote part of the compound meaning by inclusion, but not by conformity.
C) Because the compound meaning is simple, so the parts cannot denote it by conformity.
D) Because inclusive denotation is always present.

Key Takeaway
Composition and simplicity are determined solely by whether a part denotes part of the meaning by conformity; other types of denotation do not affect this classification, and a word can be simple relative to one meaning and compound relative to another.

Which Implication Reveals Composition?
Author distinguishes implication types for composition understanding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of indication (dalalat) and explain why only the second (iltizam) yields understanding of composition.

Core Idea
Only implied meaning (iltizam) reveals composition; correspondence gives the whole without parts.

In this lesson, we examine a key passage from the logical commentary tradition. The author clarifies a subtle point: which of the three types of indication—correspondence (mutabaqa), inclusion (tadammun), or implication (iltizam)—yields understanding of composition. This distinction is crucial for defining terms correctly, as a proper definition identifies the parts of the definiendum. The passage also critiques a potential circular inference, showing the depth of logical analysis in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three types of indication (dalalat)
Review the standard classification: mutabaqa (word and meaning correspond fully), tadammun (word implies a part of the meaning), iltizam (word implies something external but necessary).
2
Identify the type that gives composition
The author explicitly states that the second type (الثانى) necessitates understanding composition. The first type does not even give entirety/partiality, let alone composition.
The second type here refers to iltizam (implication), not tadammun.
3
Understand why the first type fails
Mutabaqa only conveys the meaning as a whole without analyzing parts. For example, the word 'man' in mutabaqa gives the concept of man entirely, not its constituents 'animal' and 'rational'.
The passage mentions 'الكلي والجزئي' (totality and partiality) as prerequisites for composition.
4
Recognise the critique of circularity
The author notes that even if one conceded that mutabaqa necessitates totality and partiality, that alone would be sufficient to explain composition without needing to claim that totality/partiality necessitate composition. This reveals a potential circular reasoning.
The term 'الاستدراك' here signals a logical flaw in the inference.
5
Grasp the commentator's resolution
Mirza Muhammad Ali explains that the author did not explicitly address the status of simple vs. compound terms regarding implication because it is clear: a simple term may have no necessary concomitant, may be compound, or may be simple with a concomitant. The matter is left to the learner's understanding.
This shows the pedagogical style of the tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا و معلوم ان المستلزم لفهم التركيب هو الثانى لا الأول لانه لا يستلزم فهم الكلية و الجزئية فضلاً عن فهم التركيب. ثم لا يخفى ما فى هذا الاستدلال من الاستدراك لان الكلية والجزئية لوسلم لزومهما لكان هذا كافياً في بيان المطلوب من غير احتياج الى تكلف دعوى استلزامهما التركيب لانها ايضاً امران خارجان فتأمل. (میرزا محمد علی) (۳۴) ای لا من طرف التضمن ولا من طرف الالتزام. فان قلت: لم لم يتعرض المصنف لحالهما في الاستلزام وعدمه ؟ قلت لظهور العلم بهما مما ذكره فانه كما يجوز ان يكون بسيطاً لا لازم له، كذلك يجوز ان يكون مركباً و ان يكون بسيطاً له لازم كما ذكره المحشي فكان المصنف احال حالهما الى فهم المتعلم (عبد الرحيم)
This is known: that which necessitates understanding composition is the second, not the first, because the first does not necessitate understanding entirety and partiality, let alone understanding composition. Then it is not hidden what is in this inference of circularity? because entirety and partiality, even if we concede their necessity, this would be sufficient to explain what is sought without need to strain by claiming that they necessitate composition, because they are also two external things. So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (34) i.e., neither from the side of inclusion nor from the side of implication. If you say: Why did the author not address their status in necessity and non-necessity? I say: Because knowledge of them is apparent from what he mentioned. For just as it is permissible for a simple term to have no necessary concomitant, so too it is permissible for it to be compound, and for it to be simple having a necessary concomitant, as the annotator mentioned. So the author referred their status to the understanding of the learner. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the preceding discussion about types of indication (dilalat). The author clarifies that the type of implication that leads to understanding composition is the secondary implication (iltizam), not primary correspondence (mutabaqa). He notes that the first type (mutabaqa) does not even necessitate understanding entirety and partiality, let alone composition. Then he criticizes a potential inference: if entirety and partiality were necessary, that alone would suffice without needing to claim that they necessitate composition. The annotator (Mirza Muhammad Ali) adds that this secondary implication is neither from inclusion (tadammun) nor from correspondence. Then a question arises: why did the author (al-Katibi or similar) not discuss the two cases (simple vs. compound) regarding implication? The answer: their status is clear from what he said—a simple term may have no concomitant, or it may be compound, or it may be simple with a concomitant. The author left it to the learner's understanding.

Key Terms
المستلزم
الكلي والجزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The first (المطابقة)
B) The second (الالتزام)
C) The third (التضمن)
D) All three

Key Takeaway
The implication (iltizam) is the only type of indication that gives knowledge of composition, because it brings to mind necessary concomitants beyond the literal meaning.

Why the Author Used 'Placed Word' Instead of 'Utterance' in Defining Simple and Compound
Author justifies using 'placed word' to avoid objections in the division into compound and simple.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logicians restrict the definition of simple and compound to 'placed words' and identify which types of utterances are excluded.

Core Idea
Not all utterances are logical units—only words placed for meaning can be simple or compound.

The author of the Risala al-Shamsiyya defines simple and compound in terms of 'اللفظ الموضوع' (the placed word) rather than the broader term 'اللفظ' (utterance). This distinction shields the definition from counterexamples like meaningless sounds or natural signs, which are not part of logical analysis. In this lesson, we will unpack the reasoning behind this terminological choice and see how it relates to the broader discipline of logic.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the terminological issue
The author could have used 'utterance' (اللفظ) but chose 'placed word' (اللفظ الموضوع). Why? Because 'utterance' is too broad and would include expressions that are not considered words in logical analysis.
2
Identify the excluded utterances
The passage mentions meaningless utterances (heard but no meaning understood) and natural signs (like groans indicating pain by nature, not convention). These are not classified as simple or compound words in logical terminology.
3
Understand the objection about the four signifiers
Some might think that dividing all placed subjects into simple and compound is invalid because the four signifiers (الدوال الأربع) are neither simple nor compound. The author restricts the division to 'placed word' to avoid this objection.
The four signifiers are a technical category, perhaps particles or other word types.
4
Note the additional safeguard
Even if one concedes the broader term 'لفظ', the qualifier 'موضوع' further restricts to words placed for a meaning, excluding words placed for grammatical composition (like tense markers) which cannot be divided into simple/compound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انما لم يقل: «واللفظ» كما عبر به البعض، لئلا ينتقض حد المفرد المشاراليه يقوله : « والانفرد بالالفاظ المهملة المستعملة من المشاهد الحاضر على وجه لايفهم لا يفهم منها معنى اصلا و بالالفاظ الدالة على معانيها بحسب الطبع او النقل فانها لا تسمى في الاصطلاح الفاظاً مفردة (محمد علی) (۳۶) انما فسر بذلك ، دفعاً لما ربما يتوهم من ان قسمة الموضوع مطلقاً الى المركب و المفرد غير جايز لان الدوال الاربع وهو قسم من الموضوع لا يتصف بالافراد والتركيب اصلاً يعن ، اصلاً يعنى انه لم يجعل مطلق الموضوع مقسماً للمركب والمفرد حتى يرد ما ذكر بل نوعاً خاصاً منه وهو اللفظ الموضوع و ذلك بقرينة انه افرد البحث اولاً عن احوال اللفظ الموضوع حيث قال: دلالة اللفظ»، ولوسلم فاللفظ اذا اطلق يتبادر منه الفرد الاكمل. ثم المراد من الموضوع الموضوع لمعنى لعين ما ذكر فلايرد أيضاً أن اللفظ الموضوع لغرض التركيب لا يتصف بهما قط فكيف يصح قسمته مطلقا اليهما ؟. (محمد علی)
He only did not say 'the utterance' as some expressed, lest the definition of the simple be refuted by his saying: 'and simple is the meaningless utterances used from present observation in a way that no meaning is understood at all, and utterances indicating their meanings by nature or convention, for they are not called in terminology simple words.' (Muhammad Ali) (36) He only interpreted it thus, to repel what might be imagined: that dividing the subject absolutely into compound and simple is not permissible because the four signifiers—which are a type of subject—are not characterized by simplicity and composition at all. That is, he did not make the absolute subject a divisor of compound and simple such that what was mentioned would arise, but rather a specific type of it, which is the placed word. This is indicated by the fact that he first devoted research to the states of the placed word when he said: 'The indication of the word.' And even if it were conceded, when the word is mentioned absolutely, the most perfect individual comes to mind. Then what is meant by 'placed' is that which is placed for a meaning for the very thing mentioned, so it is not also objected that the word placed for the purpose of composition is never characterized by them, so how can its division absolutely into them be valid? (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage explains why the author (al-Katibi in his Risala) used the term 'اللفظ الموضوع' (the placed word) instead of just 'اللفظ' (the utterance) when defining the simple. If he had said 'utterance', the definition would be open to counterexamples: meaningless utterances (like mere sounds) or natural signs (like a groan) are not called simple words in terminology. The annotator (Muhammad Ali) adds that this restriction also prevents the objection that the four signifiers (دوال) are not subject to simplicity/composition. The author only divided a specific type of subject, namely the placed word, as indicated by his earlier focus on 'دلالة اللفظ'. Even if one argues that 'لفظ' usually refers to the complete individual, the term 'موضوع' further restricts to words placed for a meaning, avoiding another objection that words placed for composition (like 'ضرب' for the past tense) cannot be divided into simple and compound.

Key Terms
اللفظ الموضوع
الدوال الأربع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The restriction to 'placed word' ensures that only conventional meaningful utterances are subject to logical analysis of composition.

The Requirement of Correspondence in Part-Indication for Composition
Author specifies that part-indication must be by correspondence, not inclusion or implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which type of indication qualifies a word as compound and explain the reasoning behind the restriction to correspondence.

Core Idea
Compound words require part-indication by correspondence; inclusion or implication leads to logical contradiction.

In logic, a compound word is defined as one whose part indicates a part of its meaning. But which type of indication? The three types—correspondence, inclusion, implication—each give different results. The author argues that only correspondence avoids making a word both compound and simple. The commentator objects that a word can be compound and simple relative to different meanings, as seen in 'Abdullah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of indication
Review the definitions: correspondence (مطابقة) – the word indicates the whole meaning; inclusion (تضمن) – part indicates part of meaning; implication (التزام) – indicates something necessarily connected. The passage focuses on part-indication.
These are standard in logic (mantiq).
2
Understand the definition of compound
A compound word is one whose part indicates a part of its meaning. The author insists this indication must be by correspondence. Why?
3
Follow the reductio argument
If inclusion or implication were allowed, a word composed of two simple parts (each simple meaning has no parts) would be both compound (by correspondence of its parts indicating parts of the compound meaning) and simple (by inclusion/implication, since the simple meanings have no parts). This is contradictory.
The phrase 'otherwise it would be necessary that ... be both compound and simple together' is the core.
4
Consider the commentator's objection
The commentator argues that a word can be compound relative to one meaning and simple relative to another, like 'Abdullah (proper name compound, relational simple). If that is allowed for two correspondence meanings, why not for correspondence vs inclusion/implication?
This objection challenges the reductio.
5
Evaluate the debate
Decide whether the objection succeeds. The author may respond on page 193. For now, understand both positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ان اريد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه يعنى بالدلالة : دلالة المطابقيية كما قيد بذلك الكاتبي وغيره لا التضمنى والالتزامي خاصة ولا مجموع الثلاث ولا المطلق الشامل للثلاث اما الاولان فظاهر و اما الثالث فقيل لان المعتبر في تركيب اللفظ وافراده دلالة جزئه على جزء معناه المطابقى وعدم دلالته عليه خاصة لا دلالة جزئه على جزء معناه المطابق او التضمنى او الالتزامي وعدم دلالته عليه والالزم ان يكون اللفظ المركب من لفظين موضوعين بازاء معنيين بسيطين او بازاء معنى مر مركب له لازم ذهنی بسيط مركباً و مفرداً معاً فان جزئه يدل على جزء معناه المطابق ولا يدل على جزء معناه التضمنى او الالتزامي اذا المفروض ان كل واحد منهما بسيط لاجزء له ولا شك ان الدلالة على شيء فرع لوجود ذلك الشيء. واعترض عليه شارح الرسالة بان غاية ذلك ان يكون اللفظ بالقياس الى المعنى المطابق مركباً و بالقياس الى التضمني اوالالتزامي مفرداً ولا بأس بذلك كما ان اللفظ باعتبار معنيين مطابقيين يتصف بالافراد و التركيب كـ «عبدالله» باعتبار معناه العلمى والاضافي و الحيوان الناطق باعتبار معناه العلمي والوصفى فاذا جاز ذلك باعتبار معنيين مطابقيين فما ظنك بالمعنى المطابق و التضمني او الالتزامي ؟
His statement: 'If it is intended by a part of it the indication of a part of its meaning' means by indication: the indication of correspondence, as al-Katibi and others restricted it, not inclusion and implication alone, nor all three, nor the absolute including all three. As for the first two, it is apparent; as for the third, it is said because what is considered in the composition and simplicity of a word is the indication of its part of a part of its meaning by correspondence and its non-indication of it specifically, not the indication of its part of a part of its meaning by correspondence or inclusion or implication and its non-indication of it. Otherwise it would be necessary that a word composed of two words placed for two simple meanings or for a compound meaning having a simple mental concomitant be both compound and simple together. For its part indicates a part of its meaning by correspondence and does not indicate a part of its meaning by inclusion or implication, since it is assumed that each of them is simple having no part. And there is no doubt that indication of something is a branch of the existence of that thing. And the commentator on the Treatise objected to it that at most the word would be compound in relation to the meaning by correspondence and simple in relation to inclusion or implication, and there is no harm in that, just as a word with regard to two meanings by correspondence is characterized by simplicity and composition, like 'Abdullah with regard to its proper and relational meaning, and 'al-hayawan al-natiq' with regard to its proper and descriptive meaning. So if that is permissible with regard to two meanings by correspondence, what do you think about the meaning by correspondence and inclusion or implication?
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage deals with the precise definition of a compound word. The definition requires that a part of the word indicates a part of the meaning. But which type of indication? The author says it must be by correspondence (mutabaqa), not inclusion (tadammun) or implication (iltizam). If it were otherwise, a word like a compound of two simple parts would be both compound (by correspondence) and simple (by inclusion/implication) simultaneously, because its parts do not indicate parts by inclusion/implication since those meanings are simple. The commentator on the Treatise raises an objection: this is not a problem, as a word can be compound relative to one meaning and simple relative to another, like 'Abdullah (proper vs. relational meanings). If that is allowed for two correspondence meanings, why not for correspondence and inclusion/implication? The author then continues with a response in the next part (page 193), but the passage here ends with the objection.

الدلالة التضمنية
الدلالة الالتزامية

Correct intention in the definition of compound words
Clarifying 'intention' in the definition of compound words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish genuine compound words from simple words by applying the condition of correct intention following linguistic convention.

Core Idea
Correct intention is essential: a word is compound only if its parts are intended to indicate parts of its meaning by convention.

The definition of a compound word includes the condition that intention to indicate parts of meaning must be correct, following linguistic convention. Without this condition, many simple words would be misclassified. The passage clarifies this with examples from Arabic names and common nouns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of compound
Recall: a compound word is one constructed from two parts where each part is intended to indicate a part of the word's meaning. This is the starting definition.
2
Recognize the 'correct intention' condition
The intention must be correct, meaning it must follow the law of convention (wad') and language. Arbitrary intention does not make a word compound.
The author stresses 'correct intention proceeding according to the law of convention and language'.
3
Apply to examples of simple words
Consider 'Zayd' as a proper noun: even if one interprets a part of it (e.g., the letter Zay) to indicate part of its meaning (e.g., the person), it is not compound because the conventional meaning does not support such intention. Similarly, 'Abdullah' and 'al-hayawan al-natiq' as proper names are simple despite having apparent parts.
4
Distinguish proper nouns from genuine compounds
Genuine compounds like 'Abdullah' as a descriptive phrase are compound because the parts are conventionally intended to indicate parts of meaning (servant of Allah). But as a proper name, intention shifts, and it becomes simple.
This shows the key role of conventional intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ما ذكر ثم المراد من الارادة هو الارادة الصحيحة الجارية على قانون الوضع واللغة، فزيد مثلاً اذا اريد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه لا يكون مركباً و كذا نحو عبد الله والحيوان الناطق علمين اذا اريد بجزء منهما الدلالة على جزء المعنى فلا تغفل. واعلم ان هيهنا اعتراضاً مشهوراً لابدع فى ان نشير اليه مع الجواب، فنقول: أما الاعتراض فهو ان تعريف المركب غير مانع من دخول الاغيار فيه لظهور صدقه على الافعال كلها مع ان شيئاً منها لايسمى ركباً وذلك لان الجزء المادى فيها اعنى الحروف المجردة من الحركات والسكنات المخصوصة يدل على الحدث و الجزء الصوري اعنى الهيئة المخصوصة الحاصلة من الحركات والسكنات المخصوصة و تقديم بعض الحروف على بعض وتأخيره عنه يدل على الزمان فيصدق عليها انها اريد دلالة جزء منها على جزء معانيها . و اما الجواب: فالمذكور فى كتب القوم انا لا نعني بالجزء في التعريف مطلق الجزء بل الجزء المسموع المرتب فى السمع ولا شك ان الفعل ليس له جزء بهذا المعنى يدل على جزء معناه ضرورة ان الهيئة المخصوصة ليست مسموعة ولا مرتبة فى السمع فلا يدخل في التعريف. فان قيل : هذا يقتضى ان لا يكون كلمة اضرب وكلمة ضرب فى زيد ضرب وأمثالهما مركبة، لانها ليست لها اجزاء متعددة بهذه الصفة فان احمد جزئيها وهو الفاعل ليس بمسموع ولا مرتب في السمع والحال
And what was mentioned: then what is meant by 'intention' is the correct intention proceeding according to the law of convention and language. So Zayd, for example, if it is intended by a part of it the indication of a part of its meaning, it is not compound; similarly, like 'Abdullah and 'al-hayawan al-natiq' as proper nouns if it is intended by a part of them the indication of a part of the meaning, so do not be heedless. And know that here there is a famous objection; we must point to it together with the answer. We say: the objection is that the definition of compound does not prevent inclusion of others, because it appears true of all verbs, while none of them is called compound. That is because the material part in them, i.e., the letters stripped of the specific vowellings and consonantal signs, indicates the event; and the formal part, i.e., the specific form resulting from the specific vowellings and consonantal signs, and the ordering of some letters before others and their delay, indicates the tense. So it applies to them that it is intended by a part of them the indication of a part of their meanings. As for the answer: it is mentioned in the books of the people: we do not mean by 'part' in the definition any part, but rather the part that is heard and arranged in hearing. And there is no doubt that a verb does not have a part in this sense that indicates a part of its meaning, necessarily because the specific form is not heard nor arranged in hearing. So it does not enter the definition. If it is said: this entails that the word 'idrib' and the word 'daraba' in 'Zayd daraba' and the like are not compound, because they do not have multiple parts with this description, since one of their two parts, which is the agent, is not heard nor arranged in hearing.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage first clarifies that the 'intention' in the definition refers to correct intention following linguistic convention, so even if one intends a part of 'Zayd' to indicate a part of its meaning, it is still simple. Similarly, proper nouns like 'Abdullah' as a name are simple even if they contain parts. Then it presents a famous objection: verbs appear to satisfy the definition of compound because they have a material part (consonants) indicating the event, and a formal part (vowel pattern and order) indicating tense. Hence they would be compound, but they are not called compound. The answer: 'part' in the definition means an audible, sequentially arranged part in hearing. The formal part (vowel pattern) is not audible or arranged in hearing; it's a mental construct. Thus verbs are excluded. A further objection: then the imperative 'idrib' and past 'daraba' in phrases like 'Zayd daraba' would not be compound because they lack multiple audible parts; the agent part is not heard. The text cuts off, but the answer continues on the next page (not included).

الإرادة الصحيحة

A word is compound only when its parts are conventionally intended to indicate parts of its meaning; arbitrary intention does not make it compound.

Objection on verbs and the answer: audible parts
Objection that verbs are compound and the answer restricting 'part'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the objection that verbs satisfy the definition of compound and explain why they are excluded by the restriction to audible, sequentially arranged parts.

Core Idea
A verb's vowel pattern is not an audible, sequentially arranged part, so verbs are not compound.

A famous objection claims that verbs fit the definition of compound words because they have a material part (consonants) indicating the event and a formal part (vowel pattern) indicating tense. The answer refines the definition by specifying that 'part' means an audible, sequentially arranged part, which excludes the vowel pattern. This lesson unpacks the objection and the answer, applying them to Arabic verb forms.

Guided Path
1
Restate the definition of compound
Definition: a compound word is one where a part is intended to indicate a part of its meaning. This is the baseline.
2
Present the objection on verbs
Verbs have a material part (consonantal letters) that indicates the event (e.g., 'k-t-b' indicates writing), and a formal part (specific vowel pattern and ordering) that indicates tense (e.g., 'kataba' vs 'yaktubu'). So each part seems to indicate a part of the verb's meaning.
The objector concludes that verbs satisfy the definition, but they are not called compound.
3
Apply the answer: restrict 'part' to audible and sequential
The answer says 'part' in the definition means a part that is heard and arranged sequentially in hearing. The vowel pattern is not heard independently; it is a mental abstraction. Therefore, it does not count as a part.
The formal part is not an audible segment; it is a property of the consonants.
4
Test the answer with a verb example
Take 'ضرب' (he hit). The consonants ض-ر-ب are heard sequentially. The vowels are heard as part of the pronunciation, but not as separate segments sequentially ordered. Hence the verb has only one audible part (the whole), and is simple.
5
Consider the further objection (incomplete in source)
A further objection: if 'part' must be audible, then imperative 'اضرب' and the verb in 'زيد ضرب' would not be compound because the agent part (implied or explicit) is not heard. The source cuts off, but the defense likely distinguishes between word parts and syntactic parts.
We cannot complete the answer without the rest of the text.

What Is Mantiq?

10 min
Priority of Primary Meaning in Word Division
Author argues for priority of primary meaning over implied meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the primary meaning (mutabaqa) is prior to implicit and implied meanings in the division of words, and identify the logical relationships among the three types of signification.

Core Idea
Primary meaning is the foundation; implicit and implied meanings depend on it.

In the study of logic (mantiq), words are divided into simple and compound based on their signification. A word can signify its meaning in three ways: by correspondence (mutabaqa), by inclusion (tadammun), and by implication (iltizam). This passage from a super-commentary (hashiya) argues that when dividing words, the primary meaning (mutabaqa) should be given priority. The argument rests on the logical relationship between these significations: if a word is compound with respect to the implicit or implied meaning, it necessarily is compound with respect to the primary meaning, but not conversely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
Recall that a word's signification can be mutabaqa (full correspondence), tadammun (part/whole inclusion), or iltizam (necessary concomitant). The passage focuses on composition (tarkib) in relation to these.
2
Understand the main claim
The author states that if composition (tarkib) is realized for the tadammuni or iltizami meaning, it is necessarily realized for the mutabaqi meaning. This establishes the priority of mutabaqa.
3
Analyze the proof for tadammun
The tadammuni meaning is a part of the mutabaqi meaning. If a part of the word indicates the tadammuni part, that part also indicates a part of the mutabaqi meaning because a part of a part is part of the whole.
Example: In 'human' (insan), 'animal' is tadammun; the root 'ins' might indicate animality.
4
Analyze the proof for iltizam
The iltizami meaning cannot exist without the mutabaqi meaning. If a part of the word indicates the iltizami part by implication, it also indicates a part of the mutabaqi meaning, since implication requires the primary meaning.
Example: 'human' implies 'capable of writing' (iltizam), but this depends on the primary meaning 'rational animal'.
5
Consider the converse and the objection
The converse does not hold: a mutabaqi meaning may exist without tadammun or iltizam (e.g., a word with two simple parts and a simple necessary concomitant). An objection about individuals (afrad) is addressed by noting that the concept of composition is existential and prior to the privative concept of simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال:
حواشى الحاشية
و الأولى ان يقال : ان تحقق التركيب بالنسبة الى المعنى التضمنى او الالتزامى لما كان مستلزماً لتحققه بالنسبة الى المعنى المطابق.
اما الاول فلضرورة أن المعنى التضمني هو جزء المعنى المطابق فمتى دل جزء اللفظ على جزء المعنى التضمني، دل على جزء المعنى المطابقي فان جزء الجزء جزء.
و اما الثاني فلانه اذا دل جزء اللفظ على جزء المعنى الالتزامى بالالتزام، دل على جزء المعنى المطابقي ضرورة استحالة تحقق الالتزام بدون المطابقة على مامر. وتحققه بالنسبة الى المعنى المطابقي غير مستلزم لتحققه بالنسبة الى المعنى التضمني او الالتزامي الجواز ان يكون المعنى المطابقى مركباً من جزئين بسيطين و له لازم بسيط كانت الدلالة المطابقية اولى بالاعتبار في مقام القسمة. لا يقال ان هذا معارض بتحقق الافراد فان تحققه بالنسبة الى المعنى المطابق مستلزم له بالنسبة الى التضمنى والالتزامى و تحققه بالنسبة الى التضمني او الالتزامي غير مستلزم له بالنسبة الى المطابق. لا نا نقول : لما كان مفهوم التركيب وجودياً ومفهوم المفرد عدمياً والملحوظ في تلك القسمة انما هو المفهوم كما سيأتى فى آخر الحاشية كان ملاحظة شأن التركيب فيها اولى لكون الوجود في التصور سابقاً على العدم، قال: و هذا الوجه يفيد أولوية اعتبار المطابقة و الوجه الاولى ان تم افاد وجوب اعتبارها انتهي مبيناً.
He said: Marginal notes on the gloss. The first thing to be said is: The realization of composition with respect to the implicit (tadammuni) or implied (iltizami) meaning, since it necessitates its realization with respect to the primary (mutabaqi) meaning. As for the first, because necessarily the implicit meaning is a part of the primary meaning; so when a part of the word indicates the part of the implicit meaning, it indicates a part of the primary meaning, for a part of a part is a part. As for the second, because when a part of the word indicates a part of the implied meaning by implication (iltizam), it indicates a part of the primary meaning, by necessity of the impossibility of the realization of implication without the primary meaning as previously mentioned. And its realization with respect to the primary meaning does not necessitate its realization with respect to the implicit or implied meaning, because the primary meaning may be composed of two simple parts and have a simple necessary concomitant; thus the primary signification (dalala mutabaqiyya) is more deserving of consideration in the division. Let it not be said that this is opposed by the realization of individuals (afrad); for its realization with respect to the primary meaning necessitates it with respect to the implicit and implied, but its realization with respect to the implicit or implied does not necessitate it with respect to the primary. We say: Since the concept of composition is existential and the concept of the simple is privative, and what is observed in that division is only the concept, as will come later at the end of the gloss, the observation of the status of composition therein is more appropriate because existence in conceptualization is prior to non-existence. He said: This aspect indicates the priority of considering the primary meaning, and the first aspect, if sound, indicates its necessity. It ends clarified.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage from the hashiya (super-commentary) addresses the priority of the primary (mutabaqa) meaning over the implicit (tadammuni) and implied (iltizami) meanings in the classification of words. The author first states that if composition (tarkib) is realized for the implicit or implied meaning, it necessarily is realized for the primary meaning, but not vice versa. He proves this for the implicit meaning: the implicit meaning is a part of the primary meaning, so if a part of the word indicates a part of the implicit meaning, it indicates a part of the primary meaning (since a part of a part is a part). For the implied meaning: if a part of the word indicates a part of the implied meaning by implication (iltizam), it indicates a part of the primary meaning because implication cannot exist without the primary meaning. Conversely, the primary meaning may exist without the implicit or implied meaning (e.g., a word with two simple parts and a simple necessary concomitant). Therefore, the primary signification is more deserving of consideration in the division. An objection is raised: consider individuals (afrad) where the primary meaning necessitates the implicit and implied, but not vice versa. The author responds by noting that the concept of composition is existential (wujudi) and the concept of the simple is privative ('adami), and the division is based on concepts. Since existence is prior to non-existence in conceptualization, composition's status is given priority. He concludes that this reasoning shows the priority of considering the primary meaning, and the earlier reasoning, if valid, shows its necessity.

Key Terms
المعنى المطابق
المعنى التضمني
المعنى الالتزامي
التركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the primary meaning is not subject to any division.

Key Takeaway
The primary signification (mutabaqa) is the basis for word division because composition in other significations entails it, and the concept of composition (existence) precedes the privative concept of simplicity.

What Is Mantiq?

15 min
Definition of Compound and the Verb Objection
Definition of compound, verb objection, critique, and preferred solution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of a compound word and evaluate the standard answer to the verb objection, including the author's critique.

Core Idea
A compound word requires a phonetically heard part that indicates part of the meaning; verbs fail because their formal pattern is not a genuine part.

The definition of compound (murakkab) is central to logic because it distinguishes simple from compound expressions. A famous objection claims that verbs are compound because their letters indicate the event and their vowel pattern indicates time. In this lesson, we analyze the definition, the objection, and the logical answers, culminating in the author's preferred solution.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of compound
From the translation, a compound word is defined as a word whose part indicates part of its meaning, with the condition that the part is heard and ordered in hearing (or its equivalent).
2
Understand the objection
The objection argues that verbs have a material part (the letters stripped of vowelizations) indicating the event, and a formal part (the vowel pattern and ordering) indicating time. Thus, a part of the verb indicates a part of its meaning, so verbs would be compound.
3
Analyze the standard answer
Logicians respond that 'part' means a part that is heard and ordered in hearing. The formal pattern (hay'a) is not heard, so verbs are not compound.
4
Identify the counter-objection
If only heard parts count, then imperative 'idrib' and 'daraba' in 'Zayd daraba' have a hidden agent part that is not heard, yet they are compound. The logicians answer by extending 'heard' to include what is heard by estimation (taqdir) or dispositionally fit to be heard (masmu' sha'ni).
5
Evaluate the author's critique and better solution
The author finds the standard answer contradictory because if the formal pattern is a part of the verb, it must be heard (since the verb is an utterance). Instead, he denies that the formal pattern is a part of the verb at all. Then the verb has no part indicating part of its meaning, so it is not compound. Further, on the correct view that verb meanings are simple forms (hay'at basita), the difficulty disappears.
