والاصوات ضرورة انها لا تكون لفظا و ما لا يكون جزئه لفظاً لا يكون كله لفظاً لاستلزام انتفاء الجزء انتفاء الكل. فالاولى في الجواب ان يمنع كون الهيئة جزء من الفعل و السند ما اشير اليه فحينئذ لا يرد ايضاً ما يتوهم من انا لو سلمنا ان المراد من الجزء الجزء المسموع المرتب فى السمع وان الهيئة الصورية ليست بذلك الصفة نقول :ايضاً ان الجزء المادى من الفعل يدل على جزء المعنى يقيناً فيصدق تعريف المركب على الفعل بهذه الحيثية كما هو ظاهر و ذلك لانه بعد ما ثبت ان الهيئة ليست بجزء من الفعل فلا يكون للفظ الفعل جزء حتى يدل على جزء معناه أو لا يدل فتأمل فان هذا المقام يستصعبه اقوام. ثم لا يخفى ان جميع ما ذكر الى هنا مبنى على القول بان معاني الافعال مركبة من الحدث والزمان والنسبة الى فاعل معين او فاعل ما على خلاف فى ذلك واما على القول بانها هي الهيئات البسيطة المنتزعة عن هذه الثلاثة لا المجموع المركب منها كما هو الحق، فنقول : انا لا نسلم ان الهيئة تدل على الزمان والمادة على الحدث حتى يرد ما ذكر ويحتاج في التفصى عنه الى ما سطر بل نقول : المجموع المركب من المادة و الصورة يدل على و الصورة يدل على الهيئة البسيطة المنتزعة عن الامور المذكورة الثلاثة، فلا محذور حتى يتفصى عنه وليكن هذا على ذكر منك وسيأتى لهذا زيادة تحقيق انشاء الله تعالى.
And one may apologize for the objection by showing the difference between the two contexts: composition and simplicity in that which is measured (maqis 'alayh) are according to multiple positions and different significations, unlike them in that which is measured (maqis), for although they are considered according to two different significations, they are according to one position and one state. There is no doubt that the confusion of divisions here is not like it is there, so the possibility does not entail the possibility. Then the intended meaning of 'intention' (irada) is the correct intention that runs according to the law of position (wad') and language. So Zayd, for example, if a part of it is intended to indicate part of its meaning, it is not compound; similarly, 'Abdullah and 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq' as proper names, if a part of each is intended to indicate part of the meaning — so do not be heedless. And know that here there is a famous objection — I must mention it with the answer. We say: As for the objection, it is that the definition of compound does not prevent the entry of others, because it is apparently true of all verbs, while none of them is called compound. This is because their material part — namely the letters stripped of specific vowellessness (sukuns) and vowelizations — indicates the event, and the formal part — namely the specific form (hay'a) resulting from the specific vowelizations and vowellesses and the precedence of some letters over others and their postponement — indicates the time. So it is true of them that a part of their word is intended to indicate part of their meaning. As for the answer: it is mentioned in the books of the [logician] people that we do not mean by 'part' in the definition absolute part, but rather the part that is heard (masmu'a) and ordered in hearing. There is no doubt that the verb has no part in this sense that indicates part of its meaning, because the specific form is not heard nor ordered in hearing, so it does not enter the definition. If it is said: This would necessitate that the word 'idrib' (imperative 'strike!') and the word 'daraba' in 'Zayd daraba' and their likes are not compound, because they do not have multiple parts with this description; for one of their two parts — the agent — is not heard nor ordered in hearing, yet they are compound. We say: They [the logicians] meant by 'heard' what is common between being in reality, as in 'ramiy al-hijara' (the thrower of stones), and being by estimation, as in 'idrib', for the agent in it, although not actually heard and ordered in hearing, is so by estimation. Or we say: The intended meaning of 'heard' is the dispositional heard (al-masmu' al-sha'ni), i.e., what is fit to be heard and ordered in hearing, even if not actually so. There is no doubt that the word 'anta' (you) in 'idrib' and the word 'huwa' (he) in 'daraba' in 'Zayd daraba' and their likes are a part heard in one of these two senses. This is the answer. It is not hidden what contradiction (tahafut) is in this answer, for if the form (hay'a) is conceded to be a part of the verb, which is a type of utterance, it cannot be said that it is not heard; otherwise, the verb would have to not be counted as a type of utterances and sounds, because if it is not an utterance and that whose part is not an utterance cannot have its whole be an utterance, due to the implication of the non-existence of the part for the non-existence of the whole. So the preferable answer is to deny that the form (hay'a) is a part of the verb, and the evidence is what was pointed to. Then neither does what is imagined also follow: if we concede that the intended meaning of 'part' is the heard part ordered in hearing, and that the formal part is not of that description, we also say that the material part of the verb certainly indicates part of its meaning, so the definition of compound would be true of the verb in this respect, as is clear. That is because after it is established that the form is not a part of the verb, the verb-word has no part so as to indicate part of its meaning or not. So reflect, for this station is difficult for some people. Then it is not hidden that all that has been mentioned until here is based on the statement that the meanings of verbs are composite of event, time, and relation to a specific agent or an agent, there being disagreement on that. But based on the statement that they are the simple forms (hay'at basita) abstracted from these three — not the composite sum — as is the truth, we say: We do not concede that the form indicates time and the material indicates the event, such that what was mentioned occurs and one needs to extricate oneself from it through what was written. Rather, we say: The composite sum of the material and form indicates the simple form abstracted from the aforementioned three things. So there is no difficulty from which to extricate oneself. Let this be in your remembrance, and there will come for this further verification, God willing.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage from the hashiya discusses the definition of a compound word (murakkab) and defends it against a famous objection. The author first mentions an apologetic distinction between two contexts (maqamayn) regarding composition and simplicity, then clarifies that 'intention' (irada) in the definition means correct intention according to linguistic convention. The famous objection states that verbs like 'daraba' would be compound because their material part (letters) indicates the event and their formal part (vowel pattern) indicates time, so a part of the word indicates part of the meaning. The standard answer from logicians is that 'part' in the definition means a part that is heard (masmu') and ordered in hearing, and the verb's formal part is not heard. But an objection arises: imperative 'idrib' and 'daraba' in 'Zayd daraba' have a hidden agent part that is not heard, yet they are considered compound. The logicians answer that 'heard' includes estimation (taqdir) or dispositional fitness (sha'ni). The author criticizes this answer as contradictory, because if the form is a part of the verb, it must be heard, otherwise the verb would not be considered an utterance. Instead, the author proposes a better answer: deny that the formal part (hay'a) is a part of the verb at all. Then the material part alone does not indicate a part because it is not a part in the required sense. Finally, the author notes that the entire discussion presupposes that verb meanings are composite of event, time, and relation; but on the correct view that verbs are simple forms (hay'at basita), the difficulty disappears entirely. The author promises further verification later.

Key Terms
المركب
الجزء المسموع
الهيئة الصورية
المسموع الشأني

The definition of compound requires that a part be phonetically heard and ordered; since a verb's formal pattern is not a genuine part, verbs are not compound.

Why Compound Precedes Simple in Logical Division
Author explains why compound is presented before simple in the classification of words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why compound words are presented before simple words in logical classification, using the concepts of privation-possession opposition and existential priority.

Core Idea
In logical divisions, conceptual nobility can override natural priority.

In the logical classification of words, one might expect simple words to be introduced first since they are naturally prior. However, logicians place compound words first. This lesson explores the reasons behind this choice, grounded in the nature of privation and possession and the nobility of existential concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
Recognize that simple words are naturally prior to compound words, yet logicians place compound first. This seems to go against the principle that natural order should be followed in classification.
The author states 'what is prior by nature is also given priority in the position' but here compound is placed first despite being posterior by nature.
2
Examine the first reason: opposition of privation and possession
The opposition between compound and simple is of the type 'privation and possession' (taqabul al-'adam wa al-milkah). In such oppositions, the privative term (simple, which lacks composition) is known only after knowing the possessive term (compound). Therefore, compound must be introduced first epistemologically.
Example: blindness is known only after knowing sight.
3
Examine the second reason: existential vs. privative concept
The concept of compound is existential (wujudi) — it positively indicates composition. The concept of simple is privative ('adami) — it indicates lack of composition. Existence is more noble (ashraf) than non-existence, so compound is given priority in a classification based on concepts.
Nobility here means conceptual precedence, not temporal priority.
4
Understand the overall principle
The division of words is made with regard to the concept (bi-hasab al-mafhum). Since the concept of compound is existential and thus more noble, it is given priority in position, even though it is posterior in nature. This shows that conceptual considerations can override natural order in logical organization.
This principle is not unique to logic; it appears in other sciences when defining terms.
5
Clarify the footnote on 'part'
The author adds that 'part' in the definition of compound means the part that is heard and ordered in hearing, either actually or by estimation. This excludes verbs, whose parts (like the form of the verb) are not heard as separate parts, thus preserving the definition of compound.
This addresses an objection that verbs would then be compound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم انهما قدم المركب على المفرد والحال ان المفرد مقدم بالطبع عليه وقد تقدم أن ما هو مقدم بالطبع يقدم في الوضع ايضاً، ليوافق الوضع الطبع. لان التقابل بينها تقابل العدم والملكة ولا تعرف الاعدام الابعد معرفة ملكاتها، وأيضاً فان مفهوم المركب وجودى و مفهوم المفرد عدمى والوجود اشرف من العدم فهو بهذا الاعتبار مقدم عليه و ان كان مؤخراً عنه من حيث الطبع ولما كان القسمة هنا بالنظر الى المفهوم اعتبر في الوضع حاله فقدم ما مفهومه وجودى على الذي مفهومه عدمی . (میرزا محمد علی) (۳۸) المراد من الجزء، هو الجزء المسموع المرتب فى السمع سواء كان تحقيقاً او تقديراً فيندفع ما قيل من ان ما ذكرتموه فى تعريف المركب يقتضى ان يكون الافعال مركبة فان الجزء المادى فيها بدل على الحدث و الجزء الصورى اعنى الهيئة الحاصلة من الحركات و السكنات وتقديم بعض الحروف و تأخيرها يدل على الزمان فيصدق عليها ان جزء لفظها يدل على جزء معناها دلالة مقصودة فيلزم ان يكون
Then they [the logicians] gave priority to the compound over the simple, even though the simple is prior by nature. And it has been mentioned that what is prior by nature is also given priority in the position (wad'), so that position matches nature. Because the opposition between them is the opposition of privation and possession, and privations are not known except after knowing their possessions. Also, the concept of compound is existential and the concept of simple is privative, and existence is more noble than non-existence; thus, in this respect, it is given priority over it, even if it is posterior to it in terms of nature. And since the division here is with regard to the concept, the situation in the position is considered, so what has an existential concept is given priority over what has a privative concept. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (38) The intended meaning of 'part' is the part that is heard and ordered in hearing, whether in reality or by estimation. And this repels what was said: that what you mentioned in the definition of compound necessitates that verbs be compound, for their material part indicates the event and the formal part — namely the form resulting from the vowelizations and vowellesses and the precedence of some letters and postponement — indicates time, so it is true of them that a part of their word indicates part of their meaning by intended signification, and thus they would be compound.

Commentary
This passage explains why compound (murakkab) is placed before simple (mufrad) in the division of words, even though simple is prior by nature. The author gives two reasons. First, the opposition between compound and simple is one of privation and possession (taqabul al-'adam wa al-milkah). In such oppositions, the privative term (simple, which lacks composition) is known only after knowing the possessive term (compound). Therefore, compound must be introduced first. Second, the concept of compound is existential (wujudi), while the concept of simple is privative ('adami). Since existence is more noble than non-existence, compound is given priority in the division, which is based on concepts. The author adds a footnote clarifying the definition of 'part' as the part heard and ordered in hearing, whether actually or by estimation. This clarifies that the definition of compound does not apply to verbs because their formal part is not a heard part in this sense. The footnote reiterates the earlier argument against the objection that verbs would be compound.

The Debate on Indication and Intention in Linguistic Logic
Author presents the debate between earlier and later logicians on whether indication depends on intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the early and later logicians' positions on whether linguistic indication requires speaker's intention, and explain the verbal nature of the dispute.

Core Idea
The dispute over indication and intention is merely verbal, depending on how 'indication' is defined.

This passage from a hashia on logic examines a key epistemological question: does a word indicate its meaning only when the speaker intends that meaning, or does it indicate by convention regardless of intention? The author presents the positions of Ibn Sina and al-Tusi, who tie indication to intention, and the later logicians, who argue for indication by convention. Understanding this debate sharpens your grasp of how definitions and terms function in Islamic intellectual discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
The passage begins by referencing a condition that 'the indication is intended.' The author immediately notes that this implies indication is more general than intention, because a listener can conceive a meaning from a word even without the speaker's intention.
2
Understand Ibn Sina's position
Ibn Sina in al-Shifa argues that a word does not indicate by itself; its indication follows the speaker's intention. He uses the example of 'al-ayn', which can mean spring or coin depending on intention. Without intention, the word indicates nothing and is not even a word.
This is a key claim: indication is contingent on speaker's will.
3
Analyze al-Tusi's elaboration
Al-Tusi in Sharh al-Isharat states that since indication is conventional, it is attached to the speaker's intention. Only the intended meaning is considered indicated; other possible meanings are not indicated even if the word could bear them.
Al-Tusi reinforces Ibn Sina's view with a more technical formulation.
4
Examine the later scholars' objection
The commentator of al-Mutali' objects: we know by necessity that whoever knows the convention of a word for a meaning will conceive that meaning upon hearing the word, whether the speaker intends it or not. Homonyms (mushtarak) show that all meanings come to mind, though context determines which one the speaker meant.
This counters the idea that indication depends on intention; instead, it is based on convention.
5
Grasp the author's resolution
The author reconciles by pointing out that the dispute is verbal, rooted in how 'indication' is defined. If indication means 'transition to meaning as intended', then it depends on intention. If it means 'absolute transition', then it is more general. Both sides agree on the psychological facts; they only differ in terminology.
This shows the importance of precise definitions in resolving debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله الرابع ان يكون هذه الدلالة مرادة فيه اشارة الى ان الدلالة اعم من الارادة لظهور ان من كان عالماً بوضع اللفظ للمعنى وكان ذلك المعنى حاضراً عنده كلما تصور اللفظ يتصور معناه ايضاً وان لم يكن مراداً للمتكلم و لذا قالوا ان من سمع لفظاً مشتركاً بين معان متعددة و كان عالماً بوضعه لها هذه المعاني و ان لم يرد المتكلم الا واحداً منها وليس هذا الاندلالته عليها . يتصور جميع ومنهم من جعلها تابعة للارادة وموقوفة عليها قال الشيخ فى الشفاء: ان اللفظ لايدل بنفسه والا لكان لكل لفظ حق من المعانى لا يجاوزه بل دلالته تابعة لارادة اللافظ فاذا اريد بلفظ العين مثلاً الينبوع دل عليه و اذا اريد الدينار دل عليه ولوخلا عن الارادة لم يكن دالا على شيء بل لا يكون لفظاً عند كثير من اهل النظر فان الحرف والصوت فيما اظن لا يكون بحسب التعارف عند كثير من المنطقيين لفظاً ما لم يشتمل على دلالة.
و عن المحقق الطوسي (ره) انه قال في شرح الاشارات: دلالة اللفظ لما كانت وضعية كانت متعلقة بارادة المتلفظ فيا يتلفظ به ویراد به معنى و ما فهم منه ذلك المعنى يقال : انه دال على ذلك المعني وما سوى ذلك المعنى مما لا يتعلق به ارادة المتكلم وان كان ذلك اللفظ او جزء منه بحسب تلك اللغة او لغة اخرى او بارادة يصلح لان يدل عليه فلا يقال انه دال عليه. والمتأخرون شددوا النكير عليهم ، قال شارح المطالع : ان دلالة اللفظ ليست بنفسه لكن لا يلزم منه ان تكون تابعة للارادة بل بحسب الوضع فانا نعلم بالضرورة ان من علم وضع لفظ لمعنى و كان صورة ذلك اللفظ محفوظة له فى الخيال وصورة المعنى مرتسمة فى البال فكلما تخيل ذلك اللفظ تعقل معناه سواء كان مراداً اولا و اما المشترك فلا شك ان العالم بوضعه المعانيه يتعقلها عند اطلاقه. نعم تعيين ارادة اللافظ موقوف على القرينة لكن بين ارادة المعنى ودلالة اللفظ عليه بون بعيد انتهى . والحق ان النزاع لفظى مبنى على الاختلاف في تفسير الدلالة بانها الانتقال من اللفظ الى المعنى من حيث انه مراد او الانتقال منه اليه مطلقا فمن فسرها بالاول جعلها تابعة للارادة و من فسرها بالثانى جعلها اعم منها. فالشيخ ومن تبعه لا ينكرون تصور المعانى عند تصور الالفاظ بالمعنى الذي ذكره المتأخرون بل تصورها من حيث انها مرادة للمتكلم والمتأخرون لا يدعون انها تتصور بهذه الحيثية عند تصور الالفاظ سواء ارادها المتكلم أم لا فان كل واحد من هذين مما لا ينبغى ان يصدر عن ذى مسكة، فالنزاع في الحقيقة في تفسير الدلالة هكذا ظهر و ان شئت فوازن كلماتهم وتصفح مكتوباتهم. (میرزا محمد علی)
His statement: 'And the fourth is that this indication is intended in it.' It is a reference to the fact that indication is more general than intention, because it is obvious that whoever knows the convention of a word for a meaning and that meaning is present with him, whenever he imagines the word he imagines its meaning as well, even if it is not intended by the speaker. That is why they said: whoever hears a word that is shared among multiple meanings and knows its convention for those meanings, even if the speaker intends only one of them – and it is not that this indication [is limited] to it – he imagines all of them. And among them is one who made it dependent on intention and contingent upon it. Al-Shaykh (Ibn Sina) said in al-Shifa: 'The word does not indicate by itself, otherwise each word would have a right to [indicate] meanings that it does not exceed. Rather, its indication is dependent on the intention of the speaker. So if by the word 'al-'ayn' for example the spring is intended, it indicates it; and if the dinar is intended, it indicates it. And if it is devoid of intention, it indicates nothing. Indeed, it is not a word according to many scholars, for the letter and sound in my opinion are not, according to the common usage of many logicians, a word unless it includes indication.' And from al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi (may God have mercy on him) that he said in Sharh al-Isharat: 'Since the indication of a word is conventional (wad'iyya), it is attached to the intention of the speaker. So what he utters and intends by it a meaning, and what is understood from it of that meaning is said: it indicates that meaning. And other than that meaning, which the speaker's intention does not attach to, even if that word or part of it according to that language or another language or by an intention is capable of indicating it, it is not said that it indicates it.' And the later scholars strongly denied them. The commentator of al-Mutali' said: 'The indication of a word is not by itself, but it does not necessitate that it be dependent on intention; rather, it is by convention. For we know by necessity that whoever knows the convention of a word for a meaning, and the form of that word is preserved in his imagination and the form of the meaning is depicted in his mind, then whenever he imagines that word, he comprehends its meaning, whether it is intended or not. As for the shared (homonym), there is no doubt that the one who knows its convention for its meanings comprehends them when it is uttered. Yes, the determination of the speaker's intention is dependent on context, but between the intention of the meaning and the indication of the word there is a great distance.' End. The truth is that the dispute is verbal, based on the difference in interpreting 'indication' as the transition from word to meaning insofar as it is intended, or as the transition from it to it absolutely. So whoever interprets it as the first makes it dependent on intention; whoever interprets it as the second makes it more general than intention. So al-Shaykh and his followers do not deny the conception of meanings when conceiving words in the sense that the later scholars mentioned, but rather [they say] they are conceived insofar as they are intended by the speaker. And the later scholars do not claim that they are conceived in this respect when conceiving words, whether the speaker intended them or not. For each of these two is not something that should be issued by a person of discernment. So the dispute in reality is about the interpretation of 'indication'. Thus it appeared. And if you wish, balance their statements and scrutinize their writings. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the nature of linguistic indication (دلالة لفظية). It begins as a commentary on the fourth condition for 'compound' (that the indication be intended). The author explains that indication is more general than intention (إرادة), because a listener may understand a meaning from a word even if the speaker did not intend that meaning – for example, with homonyms where all meanings come to mind. However, some logicians, particularly Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) and al-Tusi, argued that the indication of a word depends on the speaker's intention. Ibn Sina in al-Shifa says a word does not indicate by itself; its indication follows the speaker's intention. For example, 'al-ayn' can indicate a spring or a coin depending on intention. Without intention, the word is not even a word. Al-Tusi in Sharh al-Isharat echoes this: conventional indication is attached to the speaker's intention; only the intended meaning is considered indicated. Later scholars (متأخرون) strongly opposed this. The commentator of al-Mutali' (probably Qutb al-Din al-Razi) argues that indication is by convention, not by intention: a person who knows the convention will understand the meaning upon hearing the word, whether or not the speaker intends it. With homonyms, all meanings come to mind, though context (قرينة) determines which specific meaning the speaker intended. The author then reconciles: the dispute is verbal, boiling down to how 'indication' is defined. If 'indication' means transition to meaning qua intended, then it depends on intention; if it means absolute transition, then it is more general. Ibn Sina and his followers do not deny that meanings are conceived; they say they are conceived as intended by the speaker. The later scholars do not claim they are conceived as intended without intention. So the real difference is in the interpretation of the term 'indication'. The author invites the reader to compare their statements. This passage is a classic example of resolving a dispute by clarifying definitions.

Key Terms
دلالة
إرادة
وضع
مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It indicates nothing and is not even considered a word by many scholars.

D) It indicates the meaning determined by context.

Key Takeaway
The debate over whether indication depends on intention is resolved by recognizing that it hinges on the definition of 'indication' itself.

The Definition of Khabar: Possibility of Truth and Falsity
Author addresses an objection to the definition of khabar as admitting both truth and falsity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the definition of khabar against objections using the concept of considering the statement in itself.

Core Idea
A declarative sentence is defined by its inherent possibility of truth/falsity, not by actual fact.

The logicians define khabar as a statement that possibly admits truth and falsity. But what about statements that are necessarily true, like 'The whole is greater than the part'? Do they cease to be khabar? The objector thinks so, but the answer lies in distinguishing the linguistic form from external reality. This lesson will walk you through that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objector's challenge
The objector says some statements are absolutely true or false, so they cannot possibly admit the opposite. For example, 'The whole is greater than the part' is always true, so it cannot be false.
This objection aims to show the definition is not universal.
2
Identify the core of the definition
The definition considers the statement in itself, disregarding external facts. The author says: 'The meaning is that it possibly admits them by merely considering its concept without observing the particularity of the informant and the informed.'
The key is to abstract from reality.
3
Apply the distinction to an example
Take 'The sky is above us'. When considered purely as a linguistic form, it could be false if the sky were not above us. Thus, even a necessary truth in reality still has the possibility in its form.
This preserves the definition for all khabar.
4
Reformulate the definition
Realize that the definition is about the inherent possibility of the linguistic form, not about actual truth value. So all declarative sentences, even necessarily true ones, are khabar.
The definition is convertible (universal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ان احتمل الصدق والكذب: لايقال: ان قولنا « الكل اعظم من الجزء» صادق قطعا لا يحتمل الكذب وقوله : الجزء اعظم من الكل كاذب قطعاً لا يحتمل الصدق وكذا نظائر هما مما يكون صدقه او كذبه قطعياً كقولنا السماء «فوقنا و الارض تحتنا» او بلعكس فيلزم عدم انعكاس التعريف . لانا نقول : المراد انه يحتملها بمجرد النظر إلى مفهومه من غير ملاحظة خصوصية الخبرا والمخبر، فقولنا : السماء فوقنا من حيث هو هو يحتمل الصدق والكذب وكذا قولنا : «السماء تحتنا» يعنى انك اذا جردت النظر عن الوقوع الخارجى او اللا وقوع ولاحظت هذا الكلام من حيث هو هو، يحتمل عند العقل للصدق والكذب. محمد على
His statement: 'That it possibly admits truth and falsehood' — it should not be said: 'Our statement "The whole is greater than the part" is absolutely true, not possibly false; and our statement "The part is greater than the whole" is absolutely false, not possibly true; and similarly their analogues which are absolutely true or false, like our statement "The sky is above us" and "The earth is beneath us" or the reverse, so the definition would not be convertible.' For we say: What is meant is that it possibly admits them by merely considering its concept without observing the particularity of the informant and the informed. So our statement 'The sky is above us' in itself possibly admits truth and falsehood; similarly our statement 'The sky is beneath us' — meaning if you strip the consideration of external occurrence or non-occurrence and observe this speech in itself, it is possible for the intellect to admit truth and falsehood.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the definition of khabar (declarative sentence) as that which possibly admits truth and falsehood. The objector argues that some statements are necessarily true (like 'The whole is greater than the part') or necessarily false (like 'The part is greater than the whole'), so they would not fall under the definition, making it non-universal. The answer explains that the definition considers the statement in itself, abstracting from external reality. When we consider the linguistic form alone, even a self-evident truth like 'The sky is above us' could in principle be false if the external situation differed; the possibility of truth/falsity is inherent to the form, not to the actual state of affairs. Thus, the definition is preserved.

Key Takeaway
A declarative sentence is defined by the inherent possibility of truth and falsity in its linguistic form, not by its actual truth value.

Discrepancy Between Logical and Grammatical Definitions of 'Fi'l'
Author highlights differences between what logicians and grammarians call a verb (fi'l).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the two major discrepancies between the logical and grammatical definitions of 'fi'l' using the examples of incomplete verbs and action names.

Core Idea
The term 'fi'l' splits between logic and grammar—each discipline classifies words differently based on their criteria.

In this passage, the author reveals that the categories of 'verb' in grammar and 'kalima' in logic do not perfectly overlap. This is a critical refinement for students who might assume perfect correspondence. You will examine two specific cases where the classifications diverge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two definitions
Recall that in grammar, 'fi'l' is defined by morphological criteria (inflection for tense). In logic, 'fi'l' (or 'kalima') is defined by meaning: a word that indicates an event in time. Note the difference in criteria.
Keep these definitions in mind as you examine the examples.
2
Identify the first discrepancy: incomplete verbs
Incomplete verbs like 'kana' are considered verbs by grammarians. However, logicians classify them as 'ada' (tools) because they do not indicate an independent event. Ask yourself: Why does the logical definition exclude them?
The distinction hinges on whether the word independently signifies a time-bound event.
3
Identify the second discrepancy: action names
Action names like 'haya' (come!) are classified by grammarians as nouns (because they lack inflection). But logicians classify them as verbs because they indicate a command with a time reference. Compare this to the first example.
Notice the reversal: what is a noun in grammar is a verb in logic.
4
Synthesize why these differences matter
The passage shows that one cannot assume a straightforward mapping between grammatical and logical categories. This awareness is crucial when reading texts that mix disciplines, such as works of usul al-fiqh or kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وفى عرف النحاة فعل : قيل : ان ظاهره يدل على ان كلما يقال له في عرف النحاة «فعل» فهو «كلمة» في اصطلاح المنطقيين وبالعكس. ولاشيء منها بمطرد. اما الاول: فلان الافعال الناقصة فعل افعال خ ل عند النحاة اتفاقاً مع انها ليست بكلمة عند المنطقيين بل «اداة» كما سيأتي. و اما الثاني فلان اسماء الافعال مثلاً كلمة عند المنطقيين على ما صرح به المحقق الشريف وليست يفعل عند النحويين بل اسم . فان قلت ان سلمنا كون الافعال الناقصة اداة عند المنطقيين - بناء على ما اشتهر بينهم من قسمة
His statement: 'And in the terminology of grammarians, fi'l (verb)': It is said that its apparent meaning indicates that whatever is called 'fi'l' in the terminology of grammarians is 'kalima' in the terminology of logicians, and vice versa. But none of them is universal. As for the first: because the incomplete verbs (af'al naqisa) are verbs by consensus among grammarians, while they are not 'kalima' in logic, but rather 'ada' (tool), as will come. And as for the second: because the action names (asma' al-af'al) for example are 'kalima' in logic, according to what al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif explicitly stated, while they are not 'fi'l' in grammar but rather 'ism' (noun).
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage examines the discrepancy between the logical and grammatical definitions of 'fi'l' (verb). The author notes that the apparent equivalence is not consistent. First, incomplete verbs like 'kana' and its sisters are considered verbs by grammarians, but logicians classify them as 'ada' (particles/tools) because they do not indicate an event in time in the same way as full verbs. Second, 'asma' al-af'al' (nouns that function like verbs, e.g., 'haya' for 'come!') are considered 'kalima' (verbs) by logicians because they indicate a command or event with time reference, but grammarians consider them nouns. This shows that the two disciplines have different criteria: grammar focuses on inflectional morphology, while logic focuses on meaning and the indication of time. The passage is a critical refinement, pointing out a confusion if one assumes perfect correspondence.

Key Terms
فعل
أفعال ناقصة
أسماء الأفعال

D) Because it is a proper name.

Key Takeaway
The classification of words as 'fi'l' differs between logic and grammar: incomplete verbs are verbs in grammar but tools in logic, while action names are nouns in grammar but verbs in logic.

The Logical vs Grammatical Verb: A Critical Refinement
Author critiques the claimed correspondence between logical kalima and grammatical fi'l.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the mismatches between the logical term 'kalima' and the grammatical term 'fi'l' and explain why deficient verbs and verbal nouns challenge the assumed equivalence.

Core Idea
Logical 'kalima' and grammatical 'fi'l' are not synonyms—each discipline carves language differently.

This passage from Mirza Muhammad Ali's hashia on the commentary of Quṭb al-Dīn al-Rāzī deals with a common assumption: that the grammatical verb (fi'l) matches the logical word (kalima). The author shows this correspondence fails in two directions. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying logical categories to linguistic material, especially in theology and jurisprudence where definitions matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed equivalence
The passage starts by noting that some claim that everything called 'fi'l' in grammar is 'kalima' in logic, and vice versa. The author says neither direction is universally true. Look for the phrase 'وبالعكس'.
2
Recognize the first counterexample: deficient verbs
Deficient verbs (like kāna) are verbs in grammar but are considered particles (adawāt) in logic because they indicate a temporal copula, not a complete temporal event. This refutes the claim that all grammatical verbs are logical words.
3
Recognize the second counterexample: verbal nouns
Verbal nouns like 'hayahāt' are considered kalima in logic because they can function as predicates, but grammarians classify them as nouns, not verbs. This refutes the reverse claim.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The author entertains an objection that verbal nouns do not indicate time, so they should not be kalima. He responds by citing an alternative definition of kalima (what can be a predicate alone) and argues that verbal nouns do indicate time through their forms. However, he concludes that according to the base author's definition, verbal nouns are not kalima.
5
Note the one-directionality of the original statement
The author points out that the original text only claims that kalima implies fi'l, not the reverse. Thus the first objection based on deficient verbs is invalid because it assumes the reverse direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وفى عرف النحاة فعل : قيل : ان ظاهره يدل على ان كلما يقال له في عرف النحاة «فعل» فهو «كلمة» في اصطلاح المنطقيين وبالعكس. ولاشيء منها بمطرد. اما الاول: فلان الافعال الناقصة فعل عند النحاة اتفاقاً مع انها ليست بكلمة عند المنطقيين بل «اداة» كما سيأتي. و اما الثاني فلان اسماء الافعال مثلاً كلمة عند المنطقيين على ما صرح به المحقق الشريف وليست يفعل عند النحويين بل اسم . فان قلت ان سلمنا كون الافعال الناقصة اداة عند المنطقيين - بناء على ما اشتهر بينهم من قسمة الرابطة الى الزمانية وغير الزمانية وجعل الافعال الناقصة من الزمانية كما سيأتى فلانسلم كون اسماء افعال كلمة لظهور انها لا تدل بهيئاتها على احد الازمنة الاترى ان «سعلاة» و «قوقاة» و «ضراب» و «علام» لا تدل على الزمان وهى على هيئة «هیهات» و «شتان». قلت ان كون اسماء الافعال كلمة ظاهرا على قول من عرفها بانها ما يصلح لان يخبر به وحده ولا يصلح لأن يخبر عنه ويمكن ذلك على تعريف المصنف ايضاً فانها لما دلت على معانى تدل هي على الزمان بهيئاتها فكانها دلت بهيئاتها عليه هذا. والتحقيق ان يقال انها ليست بكلمة عند المصنف كما هو ظاهر تعريفها لها والمحشى جرى فى هذا الحكم على مذهبه ويمكن ان يدفع الايراد الاول ايضاً فان كلامه (ره) انما يدل على ان كل ماهو كلمة عند المنطقيين فهو فعل عند التحويين و اما ان كل ما هو فعل عند النحويين فهو كلمة عند المنطقيين فليس في كلامه ما يدل عليه حتى يرد ان الافعال الناقصة فعل عند النحويين وليست بكلمة عندهم فتأمل. (میرزا محمد علی)
His statement: 'and in the terminology of grammarians it is a verb (fi'l)'. It is said: Its apparent meaning indicates that everything called 'verb' in grammatical terminology is a 'word (kalima)' in the terminology of logicians, and vice versa. But neither is universal. As for the first: because the deficient verbs (af'al naqisa) are verbs according to grammarians by consensus, yet they are not 'kalima' according to logicians but rather 'particle (adaat)', as will come. As for the second: because verbal nouns (asma' al-af'al) for example are 'kalima' according to logicians, as the noble sharif declared, yet they are not verbs according to grammarians but rather nouns. So if you say: we concede that deficient verbs are particles for logicians – based on what is well-known among them of dividing copulae (rabita) into temporal and non-temporal, and making deficient verbs temporal as will come – but we do not concede that verbal nouns are 'kalima' because it is apparent that their forms (hay'at) do not indicate any of the tenses; do you not see that 'sa'lata', 'qawqata', 'darab', 'allam' do not indicate time, and they are on the same form as 'hayhat' and 'shattan'? I say: That verbal nouns are 'kalima' is apparent according to the one who defines them as 'what is fit to be used as a predicate alone and not fit to be predicated upon', and that can also be according to the author's definition, for since they indicate meanings, they indicate time through their forms, so as if they indicate through their forms. The investigation is to say: They are not 'kalima' according to the author, as is clear from his definition of them, and the commentator (muhashi) judged in this ruling according to his own school. And then the first objection can also be refuted: for his speech (r.a.) only indicates that everything that is 'kalima' for logicians is a verb for grammarians; but that everything that is a verb for grammarians is 'kalima' for logicians – his speech does not indicate that, so the objection that deficient verbs are verbs for grammarians but not 'kalima' for them does not apply. So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion about the correspondence between grammatical terms and logical terms. The author (the super-commentator) critiques the claim that the logical 'kalima' and the grammatical 'fi'l' are coextensive. He gives two counterexamples: (1) Deficient verbs (like 'kana') are verbs in grammar but particles (adaat) in logic, as they do not indicate time independently. (2) Verbal nouns (like 'sifr' or 'sah') that lack tense morphology are considered 'kalima' in logic because they can function as predicates, but they are not verbs in grammar. The passage then considers an objection: even if we accept that deficient verbs are particles, verbal nouns are not 'kalima' because their forms do not indicate time. The author responds by citing an alternative definition of 'kalima' (something that can be a predicate alone) and argues that verbal nouns do indicate time through their forms. However, the author concludes that according to the base author's definition, verbal nouns are not 'kalima'. He also points out that the original statement only claims one direction (kalima → fi'l), not the reverse, so the first objection about deficient verbs is invalid because it assumes the reverse direction.

Defining 'Aḍaat': Particles and Existential Verbs
Author defines 'adaat' as needing something else to indicate meaning, and includes deficient verbs like 'kana' as particles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the logical criterion of independent signification to classify words as particles (aḍaat) or not, including existential verbs like kāna.

Core Idea
A particle is a word that cannot stand alone in meaning—even verbs like 'kana' must lean on others.

This passage from the logical tradition defines 'aḍaat' (particle) as a word that does not independently indicate its meaning but requires other words to complete it. The author then extends this definition to include existential verbs (al-kalimāt al-wujūdiyya) such as the deficient verb 'kāna', which behave like particles in logic because they only indicate a relation (nisba) between two concepts. By understanding this criterion, you will be able to identify particles not only as prepositions but also as dependent verbs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
The passage states that 'aḍaat' is a word that 'does not independently indicate' (lam yastaqill fi al-dalāla). This means the word requires an additional element (ḍammīma) to convey its intended meaning.
2
Apply the test to common particles
Consider 'min' and 'ilā': by themselves they do not give a complete meaning. Only in a phrase like 'min al-Baṣra ilā al-Kūfa' do they indicate inception and termination. This confirms they are particles.
The passage uses this example from travel.
3
Examine existential verbs
Verbs like 'kāna' (was) indicate a relation (nisba) that cannot be understood without the two ends (the subject and predicate). For example, 'kāna Zaydun qāʾiman' (Zayd was standing) – 'kāna' alone does not give a complete thought.
In grammar, these are called 'afʿāl nāqiṣa' (deficient verbs).
4
Conclude inclusion
Because existential verbs also lack independent signification, the author logically includes them in the definition of 'aḍaat'. They share the property of dependence, even though they indicate time (which distinguishes them from prepositions).
5
Distinguish from grammatical classification
In grammar, 'kāna' is a verb, not a particle. But in logic, the classification is based on semantic dependence. This shows how logical categories can cross grammatical boundaries.
The passage notes the grammatical term 'ḥarf' meaning 'edge' or 'extremity'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله و ان لم يستقل فى الدلالة اى يحتاج فيها الى ضم ضميمة فان الأداة كـ «من» و «إلى» مثلا لا تدل بمجردها على معنى بخلاف ما لوضم اليهما الفاظ اخر فانك اذا قلت: سرت من البصرة الى الكوفة، يفهم من «من» معنى الابتدائية و من «الى» معنى الانتهائية فعلى هذا تدخل الكلمات الوجودية كـ «كان» الناقصة واخواتها في تعريف الاداة فان الكلمات الوجودية ايضاً لا تدل بانفرادها على معنى مقصود بل انما تدل على نسبة لا تتعقل الابعد تعقل الطرفين فالكلمات الوجودية مشتركة مع الاداة فى عدم الدلالة بالانفراد و تمتاز عنها بالدلالة على الزمان. هذا القـ و انما سمى القسم بالاداة ، لانها في اللغة الآلة و هذا القسم آلة في تركيب الالفاظ بعضها على بعض. و اما تسميته حرفاً كما هو اصطلاح النحاة، فلانه على حرف اى طرف من الكلام من حيث انه لا يدل على معنى في نفسه او لانه لا يقع عمدة في الكلام قال صاحب القاموس: «الحرف من كل شيء طرفه». (عبد الرحيم)
His statement: 'and if it does not independently indicate' i.e., it needs in indication the addition of an accessory (damma), for the particle (adaat) like 'min' and 'ila' for example does not by itself indicate a meaning, unlike if other words are added to them, for if you say: 'I traveled from Basra to Kufa', the meaning of inception is understood from 'min' and termination from 'ila'. So on this basis, existential words (kalimat wujudiyya) like the deficient 'kana' and its sisters enter into the definition of particle, for existential words also do not by themselves indicate an intended meaning but only indicate a relation (nisba) that cannot be conceptualized except after conceptualizing the two ends. So existential words share with the particle in not indicating independently, and are distinguished from it by indicating time. This is... And this category is called 'adaat' because it means in the language 'tool', and this category is a tool in the composition of words upon one another. As for calling it 'harf' as is the grammatical terminology, it is because it is on a 'harf' i.e., an extremity of speech in that it does not indicate a meaning in itself, or because it does not occur as a principal (umda) in speech. The author of al-Qamus said: 'The haraf of everything is its extremity.' (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage provides a definition and expansion of the term 'adaat' (particle) in logic. The commentator explains that a particle is a word that does not independently indicate its meaning; rather, it requires the addition of other words to complete the signification. He gives examples of 'min' (from) and 'ila' (to) which only convey inception or termination when placed in context like 'min al-Basra ila al-Kufa'. Then he argues that existential verbs (al-kalimat al-wujudiyya) such as the deficient verb 'kana' (was) and its sisters (like 'sara', 'laysa') also fit this definition because they only indicate a relational meaning that cannot be understood without the subject and predicate (the two ends). Thus, existential verbs are included in the category of particle. However, they are distinguished from other particles because they also indicate time (e.g., past tense). The passage then gives the reason for the name 'adaat' (tool) – these words are tools for linking other words. It also mentions the grammatical term 'harf' (letter/particle) and provides an etymology: 'harf' means edge or extremity, because such words do not indicate a complete meaning in themselves or are not principal parts of speech.

Key Terms
أداة
كلمات وجودية
نسبة
حرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider 'kana' (was) as an 'adaat'?
A) Because it is a verb in grammar.
B) Because it does not indicate a complete meaning by itself and requires a subject and predicate.
C) Because it indicates time.
D) Because it is a linking word like 'wa'.

Key Takeaway
The logical classification of a word as a particle depends on its dependence on other words for complete meaning, not on its grammatical category as a noun, verb, or particle.

On Asmaa' Af'aal Not Being Kalimah
Author argues that asmaa' af'aal are not kalimah according to the musannif's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the logician's definition of kalimah to classify asmaa' af'aal and incomplete verbs, distinguishing them from grammatical fi'l.

Core Idea
Not every grammatical verb is a logical kalimah; the logician's definition is narrower.

This passage refines the classification of asmaa' af'aal (verbal nouns) by testing them against the logician's definition of kalimah. The key is to understand that logicians define kalimah as a word that can serve as a complete predicate and must inherently indicate time. Many grammatical verbs, especially asmaa' af'aal and incomplete verbs, fail this test. We will dissect the definition and see how the commentator defends the author's position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definition of kalimah according to the logicians. Two criteria emerge: (1) it is fit to be a predicate by itself, and (2) it indicates time by its form (or by virtue of its form indicating time). Note: the author mentions both definitions.
The passage contrasts with the grammatical definition of fi'l, which is broader.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
Genus: a word that indicates a meaning. Differentia: it is fit to predicate by itself (independently) and indicates temporal relation. The commentator notes that asmaa' af'aal like 'hayhat' and 'shattan' do not indicate tense by their form, thus fail the differentia.
Incomplete verbs like 'kaana' also fail because they need a predicate to complete the meaning.
3
Test asmaa' af'aal against the definition
The objection claims asmaa' af'aal (e.g., 'hayhat', 'shattan', 'sa'lah') do not indicate time by their form. The counter-response says that they do indicate time implicitly, but the verification (tahqiq) sides with the objector: they are not kalimah according to the author.
The commentator follows his own doctrine, clarifying that the musannif's definition excludes them.
4
Understand the one-way implication
The author's statement only implies that every logical kalimah is a grammatical fi'l, not the reverse. Therefore, incomplete verbs being grammatical fi'l does not make them logical kalimah, so no contradiction exists.
This resolves the potential objection about 'kaana' and its sisters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Verbs (al-af'aal) are glossed in the marginalia: the copula (al-rabita) is divided into temporal and non-temporal, and the incomplete verbs (al-af'al al-naqisa) are included in the temporal as will come. So we do not accept that the verbal nouns (asma' al-af'al) are kalimah (verb) because it is apparent that they do not indicate any of the tenses by their forms. Do you not see that 'sa'lah', 'quq'ah', 'dirab', and 'allam' do not indicate time, and they are on the pattern of 'hayhat' and 'shattan'? I say: That the verbal nouns are kalimah is apparent according to the one who defines it as 'that which is fit to predicate by itself and not fit to be predicated of', and that is possible also according to the author's definition, for since they indicate meanings, they indicate time by their forms, so it is as if they indicate time by their forms. The verification is that they are not kalimah according to the author, as is apparent from his definition of them, and the commentator (al-muhashi) followed his own doctrine in this ruling. And it is possible to repel the first objection as well, for his statement (may God have mercy on him) only indicates that everything that is a kalimah according to the logicians is a fi'l (verb) according to the grammarians. But that everything that is a fi'l according to the grammarians is a kalimah according to the logicians is not indicated by his statement, so that it might be objected that the incomplete verbs are fi'l according to the grammarians but are not kalimah according to them. So consider.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage discusses whether asmaa' af'aal (verbal nouns like 'hayhat' and 'shattan') qualify as kalimah (word/phrase) in the logical sense. The objection is raised that they do not indicate time by their form, unlike typical verbs. The response clarifies that the author (musannif) defines kalimah as capable of serving as a predicate independently, and that asmaa' af'aal can indeed indicate time through their forms, but the verification (tahqiq) is that they are not kalimah for the author. The author's statement only equates logical kalimah with grammatical fi'l, not the reverse. Thus, incomplete verbs like 'kaana' and its sisters are grammatical fi'l but not logical kalimah, so no contradiction arises. The gloss is a critical refinement typical of hashiah works, defending the musannif's classification.

Key Takeaway
The logical definition of kalimah is more restrictive than the grammatical fi'l; asmaa' af'aal and incomplete verbs are fi'l but not kalimah, consistent with the musannif's classification.

Logical Definition of Adah and Inclusion of Existential Verbs
Author defines 'adat' and includes existential verbs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical criteria for classifying a word as an 'adah' and distinguish it from the grammatical category of harf.

Core Idea
In logic, any word that depends on others for meaning is an adah, even if grammarians call it a verb.

When logicians classify words, they look beyond grammar to independence of meaning. In this passage, the author expands the category of 'adah' to include existential verbs like 'kaana' because they too require complements. This expansion reveals a key difference between logical and grammatical classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: 'adah' (أداة) - a word that functions as a tool in composition.
In logic, 'adah' is a technical term broader than the grammatical 'harf'.
2
Extract the defining condition
The condition is 'lack of independent indication' (عدم الاستقلال في الدلالة). Such a word does not convey a meaning by itself but requires attachment to other words.
Examples given: 'min' and 'ila' only indicate meaning in a sentence like 'I traveled from Basra to Kufa'.
3
Apply the condition to examples
Test the definition: 'min' and 'ila' fit because they need nouns to convey starting/endpoint. Without attachment, they are meaningless.
The definition correctly includes these particles.
4
Extend to existential verbs
Existential verbs like 'kaana' also do not independently indicate a meaning; they require a subject and predicate to express a proposition (e.g., 'Kaana Zaidun qaaiman'). Thus they meet the condition.
They are distinguished from particles by indicating time, but this does not affect the core criterion.
5
Contrast with grammatical classification
Grammarians restrict 'harf' to a specific set of particles, excluding nouns and verbs. Logicians include nouns like separating pronouns and verbs like 'kaana' under 'adah' due to functional dependency.
The passage notes this discrepancy: 'damair al-fasl' are nouns in grammar but adawat in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۵۸) قوله و ان لم يستقل فى الدلالة اى يحتاج فيها الى ضم ضميمة فان الأداة كـ «من» و «إلى» مثلا لا تدل بمجردها على معنى بخلاف ما لوضم اليهما الفاظ اخر فانك اذا قلت: سرت من البصرة الى الكوفة، يفهم من «من» معنى الابتدائية و من «الى» معنى الانتهائية فعلى هذا تدخل الكلمات الوجودية كـ «كان» الناقصة واخواتها في تعريف الاداة فان الكلمات الوجودية ايضاً لا تدل بانفرادها على معنى مقصود بل انما تدل على نسبة لا تتعقل الابعد تعقل الطرفين فالكلمات الوجودية مشتركة مع الاداة فى عدم الدلالة بالانفراد و تمتاز عنها بالدلالة على الزمان. هذا القـ و انما سمى القسم بالاداة ، لانها في اللغة الآلة و هذا القسم آلة في تركيب الالفاظ بعضها على بعض. و اما تسميته حرفاً كما هو اصطلاح النحاة، فلانه على حرف اى طرف من الكلام من حيث انه لا يدل على معنى في نفسه او لانه لا يقع عمدة في الكلام قال صاحب القاموس: «الحرف من كل شيء طرفه». (عبد الرحيم) (۵۹) يرد عليه ان ضماير الفصل والافعال الناقصة ادوات عند المنطقيين وليست بحروف عند النحويين بل الاولى اسماء و الثانية افعال عندهم فتأمل. (محمد على)
(58) His statement: 'and if it does not independently indicate' means it needs to have something attached to it. For the particle (al-adah), like 'min' and 'ila' for example, does not by itself indicate a meaning, unlike if other words are attached to them. For if you say: 'I traveled from Basra to Kufa,' from 'min' the meaning of starting point is understood, and from 'ila' the meaning of endpoint. On this basis, the existential words (al-kalimat al-wujudiyya) like the incomplete 'kaana' and its sisters enter into the definition of the particle (adah). For the existential words also do not by themselves indicate an intended meaning, but rather indicate a relation that is not conceivable except after conceiving the two extremes. So the existential words share with the particle in lacking independent indication, and are distinguished from it by indicating time. This is the section called 'adah' because in language it means 'tool', and this section is a tool in the composition of words with each other. As for its being called 'harf' as is the terminology of the grammarians, it is because it is on a side (harf) meaning an extremity of speech, in that it does not indicate a meaning in itself, or because it does not occur as a main part (umda) in speech. The author of al-Qamus said: 'The harf of everything is its side.' (59) It is objected that the separating pronouns (damair al-fasl) and the incomplete verbs are particles (adawat) according to logicians, but they are not harf according to grammarians. Rather, the first are nouns and the second are verbs according to them. So consider.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage defines the category of 'adah' (particle/tool) in logic. It explains that particles like 'min' and 'ila' do not convey a full meaning by themselves but only in combination with other words. The definition is expanded to include existential verbs like 'kaana' because they also require complements to express a complete proposition. However, verbs are distinguished by indicating time. Then there is a discussion of terminology: why this class is called 'adah' (tool) and 'harf' (letter/particle) in grammar. The objection at the end points out that the logical category of adawat includes some items (like the separating pronoun and incomplete verbs) that grammarians classify differently, creating a discrepancy. This is a typical expansion probe in the hashiah, where the commentator clarifies the logical definition and notes a critical difference from grammatical classification.

Key Takeaway
In logic, the category of 'adah' is defined by functional dependence, not by grammatical part of speech, so existential verbs are included.

Defining Mushtarak Lafzi and Distinguishing It from Manqul
Author defines mushtarak lafzi and distinguishes it from manqul and other types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the true homonym (mushtarak lafzi) based on primary placement and distinguish it from the transferred word (manqul) by analyzing the nature of secondary placement.

Core Idea
A true homonym is placed for each meaning by a separate primary placement; a transferred word gains a second meaning through usage, not placement.

In this passage, the commentator refines the definition of the homonym (mushtarak lafzi) by excluding words that acquire new meanings through customary usage rather than independent placement. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise logical analysis of ambiguous terms in Islamic texts, especially in usul al-fiqh where the meaning of a word can determine legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'mushtarak lafzi' (homonym). Recognize that the passage offers two possible definitions: one strict and one broad.
The strict definition requires separate primary placements for each meaning.
2
Extract the key condition
The core condition is that a homonym must be placed for each meaning by a separate placement. Distinguish this from cases where a word gains a second meaning through fame and connection (secondary placement).
This condition excludes manqul from the strict definition.
3
Understand the exclusion of manqul
The transferred word (manqul) is excluded from the first definition because it lacks primary placement for the new meaning. However, it is included in the second definition (word brought by usage to the result).
Note the nuance: manqul becomes like a homonym in outcome but not in origin.
4
Apply the distinction to examples
Consider the example of 'nabidh' being placed for one meaning in Arabic and another in Turkish – this is a true homonym because each meaning has its own placement event. Contrast with a word like 'salat' that shifted meaning via religious usage.
The example shows that homonyms can arise from different languages or from the same language.
5
Analyze further categories
The passage discusses the improvised (murtajal) and the indeterminate words (mubhamat). See how they are included or excluded from the homonym category based on the nature of placement.
Murtajal is debated: some say it is a homonym because its second placement is independent; others say it is co-equal because it depends on the first by way of contrast.

(عبد الرحيم) (٨٣) قوله و على الثانى اى على ان لا يكون موضوعاً لكل واحد من تلك المعانى ابتداء بوضع عليحدة فحينئذ أما ان لا يكون موضوعاً لشيء منها اصلا لا بوضع ابتدائي ولا ثانوى و اما ان يكون موضوعاً جميعها بوضع واحد اجمالي وضعاً ثانوياً و اما ان يكون موضوعاً الجميعها وضعاً ثانوياً أيضاً لكن باوضاع متعددة و اما ان يكون موضوعاً الجميعها بوضع ابتدائى لكن لا باوضاع متعددة بل بوضع واحد اجمالى واما ان يكون موضوعاً لبعضها بوضع ابتدائى ولبعضها بوضع ثانوى فان انتفاء المركب قد يكون بانتفاء جميع الاجزاء و قد يكون بانتفاء بعضها ، لكن القسم الاول منها باطل لأنه ليس لنا لفظ مستعمل في معانى متعددة لا يكون موضوعاً لواحد منها أصلاً لا بوضع اولى ولا ثانوى فان المراد من الوضع ما يشمل الوضع الشرعي ايضاً. و اما الاقسام الاخر فمثال القسم الاول منها المبهمات عند القدماء من اهل العربية فانها عندهم موضوعة للجزئيات المخصوصة التي هي مستعملاتها بوضع ثانوى واحد اجمالي. فان قلت: وضعها للماهية الكلية عندهم يوضع ابتدائى لا محالة فكيف يكون مثالاً لما يكون موضوعاً لجميع معانيها بوضع ثانوى؟ :قلت: قد تقدم ان المراد من المعنى هو المعنى المستعمل فيه اللفظ لا المطلق والماهية الكلية وان كانت
On significations. 211. Then it is placed for it according to usage, thus the transferred (manqul) is excluded from the definition, and its placement is not primary but by virtue of fame. (Abd al-Rahim) (And the teacher, the investigator, Sheikh Muhammad al-Karmi, may Allah preserve him, said in this regard, his very words:) His statement: 'for each of those meanings initially' — this is a restriction against what if a word placed for a meaning becomes famous in another meaning due to a connection, until it becomes a proper name (science) in this second meaning, derived from it without a context (qarina), and it is called in the language of the usuliyyun 'al-wad' al-ta'ayyuni'. For when the placere places the word for a meaning and others use it in another meaning, and the usage of the word in them becomes equal such that each is derived from it without a context, the word is transferred from the first to the second. So this is not called a homonym, if it is necessary to say in the definition of the homonym: it is the placement of the word for each of the multiple meanings by a separate placement. For the use of a word for a meaning not by placement in it but by a connection between the meaning used in and the meaning placed for, until the usage becomes customary in this used meaning due to a connection, such that the appropriate meaning is derived from the word without a context, it is not called a placement or by placement necessarily, even if it provides the benefit of the meaning for which the word was placed. But such a meaning cannot be expelled from the sphere of homonyms, for it is like them in the outcome and result. So the truth is to say in the definition of the homonym: it is a word placed for each of the multiple meanings by a separate placement, or a word brought by usage to this result. And the transferred (manqul) is excluded from the first definition, which says it is a word placed for each one, for it is not placed for the two meanings, the transferred-from and the transferred-to, but rather it is placed for the transferred-from primarily. As for the second definition that we mentioned, it includes it, because the word, with respect to what it is used for of the meaning of the transferred-to, has become famous until it indicates it without a context and provides the benefit of placement. Just as the truth, following the apparent meanings of the language, is that the homonym is only said of a word that the hand of placement has handled in each of the meanings derived from it, and accordingly the transferred is excluded because the hand of placement has not handled the meaning used in by connection to the placed-for meaning, definitely. And that may be the difference between homonyms and transferred words. (Taqrib, p. 2423) (79) That it be placed for each of those meanings by another placement, i.e., not placed by a single summary placement like names of reference (asma' al-ishara) according to the later scholars. Then know that the placement of those meanings is general, whether by one placement or multiple, whether the placer is from a single language or not. The summary: if a word has many meanings, it is not called a homonym whether it is from one placement or multiple, from one language or not, such that a placer placed it in a language for one of those meanings, then another placer placed it in another language for another meaning, like 'nabidh', for it is placed in Arabic for a meaning and in Turkish for another meaning. (Sheikh Abd al-Rahim) (80) His statement 'or not like that', i.e., it is not placed for each of those multiple meanings, and this necessitates that it indicates the negation of generality, not the generality of negation, such that the word is not placed for any of them at all, because this division is for the single word (al-mufrad), and the single word is a part of the placed word, as the matn said: 'And the placed word, if a part of it is intended for signification...', so the subject of all these divisions is the placed word, so it is not allowed to claim regarding a word that it is not placed for any meaning at all, because that would be exiting from the research, so ponder. (Taqrib, p. 24) (p. 212) Hashiya al-Hashiya. (81) i.e., a homonym with respect to all, but with respect to each one it is called a 'mujmal' (ambiguous). For the fact that the word is placed for this alone and for that alone is known, so it is a homonym from this respect due to its sharing among those meanings, and the fact that the intended meaning when it is uttered, this and that, is not known, so it is mujmal from this respect due to the lack of clarity of its signification. (Abd al-Rahim) And Sheikh Muhammad Ali (may Allah have mercy on him) said in verifying this research: His statement 'and the first is called a homonym': the homonym is what is placed for multiple meanings by multiple placements, primarily. So by 'the first' is excluded the proper noun (al-'alam), the univocal (al-mutawati), the equivocal (al-mushakkik), the indefinite (al-nakira), the real (al-haqiqa), and the metaphorical (al-majaz). And by our saying 'by multiple placements' is excluded what is placed for multiple meanings by a single summary placement, like names of reference, relative pronouns, and other indeterminate words (al-mubhamat), according to the later scholars, because at the time of placement the universal meaning was considered and a single unit of the particulars was placed by one placement. So the indeterminate words are outside the limit of the homonym according to both schools. And by our saying 'primarily' is excluded the transferred (manqul), for although it is placed for multiple meanings by multiple placements, it is not by primary placement; it is apparent that its placement with respect to the meaning transferred-to is only by secondary placement. As for the improvised (al-murtajal), some held that it is included within it, because since no connection to the first meaning was considered in its placement for the second meaning, its placement for it is primary. For it is apparent that the meaning of 'primary' is not that all placements occur in the same rank such that some do not precede others, but rather the meaning is that in its placement for the last meaning, the state of the first meaning and its connection to it are not considered, and its placement for it is an independent placement. Others held that it is a co-equal (qasim) to it, considering that the meaning of primary placement is what does not consider the state of the first meaning absolutely, even if from the aspect of non-connection, and the improvised considers its state from the aspect of non-connection, so a kind of dependence and secondariness occurs in it, and it exits from independence. (82) His statement 'like al-'ayn for the seeing': this is the first meaning that Muhammad ibn Ya'qub mentioned among the meanings of 'ayn, where he said: 'al-'ayn al-basira, feminine, its plural is a'yan, a'yun, 'uyun, and it is broken, and the plural of the plural is a'yinat.' (Abd al-Rahim) (83) His statement 'and on the second', i.e., on the basis that it is not placed for each of those meanings initially by a separate placement, then either it is not placed for any of them at all, neither by primary nor secondary placement, or it is placed for all of them by a single summary placement, secondarily, or it is placed for all of them secondarily as well but by multiple placements, or it is placed for all of them primarily but not by multiple placements, rather by a single summary placement, or it is placed for some of them primarily and for some secondarily. For the negation of a composite may be by the negation of all parts or by the negation of some. But the first of these divisions is false, because we have no word used in multiple meanings that is not placed for any of them at all, neither by primary nor secondary placement, for the intended meaning of 'wad'' includes religious placement as well. As for the other divisions, an example of the first of them is the indeterminate words (al-mubhamat) according to the early Arab grammarians, for they, according to them, are placed for the particular specifics that are their usages by a single secondary summary placement. If you say: their placement for the universal essence according to them is necessarily primary, so how can it be an example of what is placed for all its meanings by secondary placement? I say: It has preceded that the intended meaning of 'meaning' is the meaning in which the word is used, not the absolute and the universal essence, even if they were ...

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definitional and classificatory discussion of the homonym (al-mushtarak al-lafzi) from a commentary (hashiya) on the logical text. The author, likely drawing from earlier sources like al-Taqrib, aims to refine the definition of mushtarak lafzi and clearly distinguish it from other categories like the transferred (manqul) and the improvised (murtajal). The commentary proceeds in several steps. First, it takes a phrase from the matn—'for each of those meanings initially'—and explains it as a restriction against cases where a word becomes famous for another meaning due to a connection, leading to a secondary placement (al-wad' al-ta'ayyuni). Such a word is not a true homonym because the second meaning arises from usage and association, not from an independent primary placement. Next, the author presents two definitions of homonym: one as a word placed for multiple meanings by separate placements, and another as a word that usage has brought to that result. The transferred is excluded from the first definition because it lacks primary placement for the second meaning, but it is included in the second definition because of its fame. The text then notes that the homonym is properly only a word that the 'hand of placement' has handled for each meaning; the transferred is excluded because the hand of placement did not handle the second meaning directly. The commentary then discusses various subdivisions: homonyms can arise from one placer or multiple placers, from one language or multiple languages (giving the example of 'nabidh' in Arabic and Turkish). It then considers the case where a word is not placed for each of those meanings (i.e., not a homonym), which necessitates either being unplaced for all meanings (impossible), or being placed for all by a single summary placement (like indeterminate words), or by multiple secondary placements, etc. A further refinement is given regarding the first of these subcases: it is impossible for a word to be used for multiple meanings without being placed for any of them, since 'wad'' includes religious placement. The passage closes by addressing a potential objection about indeterminate words: why are they considered placed by secondary placement rather than primary? The answer is that the relevant 'meaning' here is the specific usage, not the universal essence. Throughout, the commentary is careful to exclude various categories: proper nouns, univocal terms, equivocal terms, indefinite nouns, real and metaphorical usage, and transferred words. The discussion also touches on the murtajal (improvised), where scholars differ on whether it is a type of homonym or a co-equal category. The overall purpose is to achieve a precise, exhaustive definition of the homonym that accounts for all possible cases and excludes all others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between a homonym (mushtarak lafzi) and a transferred word (manqul)?
A) A homonym has meanings from different languages, while a transferred word has meanings from the same language.
B) A homonym is placed for each meaning by a separate primary placement, while a transferred word gains a new meaning through customary usage without a primary placement.
C) A homonym cannot have more than two meanings, while a transferred word can have many.
D) The two terms are synonymous and interchangeable.

Key Takeaway
The true homonym (mushtarak lafzi) is defined by having each meaning originate from a separate primary placement, which distinguishes it from the transferred word (manqul) that gains a second meaning through usage without a new primary placement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the Arabic word 'zakah' – does it meet the definition of a homonym according to this passage? Consider its uses in legal contexts (obligatory charity) versus its linguistic meaning (purity, growth). Determine whether each meaning comes from a separate primary placement or from transferred usage.
?
Identify a word in the Quran that you suspect is a homonym (e.g., 'ummah' or 'yaum'). Using the passage's criteria, explain whether it satisfies the condition of being placed for each meaning by a separate placement, or if it is a transferred word. Provide reasoning based on the commentator's definitions.

What Is Mantiq?

Conditions of Majaz: A Refined Definition
Definition of majaz with its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish majaz from haqiqah, kinayah, and error by applying the conditions of usage, preventing context, and correct usage.

Core Idea
Majaz requires a preventing context that blocks the literal meaning, distinguishing it from kinayah and error.

In this lesson, we unpack the precise definition of majaz (metaphorical usage) as refined by later scholars. The passage adds crucial conditions to avoid confusion with haqiqah (literal), kinayah (allusion), and simple mistakes. You will learn to identify each element and apply them to examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its original meaning
Determine what the word is conventionally placed for (wad'). This is the haqiqah meaning. For example, 'asadd' originally means 'lion'.
2
Check if used in a different meaning
See if the word is being used for something else, e.g., 'asadd' used for a brave man. This is necessary but not sufficient for majaz.
3
Look for a preventing context (qarina mani'a)
Is there a contextual indicator that prevents the original meaning from being intended? If yes, it is majaz. If no (and the literal meaning is still possible), it is kinayah. If a mistake, it is neither.
The author explicitly excludes kinayah because in kinayah the literal meaning could also be intended.
4
Verify the usage is correct
Some scholars add that usage must be correct (sahih) to exclude errors like calling a book 'horse'. This condition is only needed if 'usage' includes incorrect speech.
The author notes this condition is added by some but not always necessary.
5
Classify the example
Combine the checks to classify an expression: haqiqah (literal, no preventing context), majaz (non-literal with preventing context), kinayah (non-literal without preventing context), or error (incorrect use with preventing context).
