(محمد علی) (۹۰) عطف تفسير للعرف الخاص (محمد علی) (۹۱) ان كان مع ياء النسبة كما في بعض النسخ فهو تمثيل لاهل العرف الخاص وان كان مجرداً عنها كما في أكثر النسخ فتمثيل للعرف الخاص (محمد على)
In semantics (with kasra of the mim) with a preventing context (qarina mani'a) from intending it. So by the condition of usage, what is not used in a meaning at all is excluded, for it is not called majaz just as it is not called haqiqah, and it has passed that it is in other than what it was originally placed for regarding haqiqah. And by our saying: 'in the terminology of the user' enters the majaz that is used in what it was placed for, but not in the terminology of the user rather in another terminology, as passed in the previous hashya. And by our saying: 'with a preventing context from intending what it was placed for' is excluded kinayah, for it is used in other than what it was placed for while its intention is permissible. And some added another condition: that the usage be correct, to exclude error used in other than what it was placed for with a preventing context, like your saying: 'Take this horse' while pointing to a book. And it is not hidden that this also is only needed if usage is general including correct and other. This. And the author (musannif) and the commentator (muhashshi) did not observe the conditions of haqiqah and majaz, because they are not in the position of defining them; rather the purpose is to divide them in a way that indicates their definition in summary. Then 'majaz' is a verbal noun (masdar mimi) in the sense of the active participle from 'jaza al-makan yajuzuhu' when he traversed it; it was transferred to the word used in other than what it was placed for because it is traversing, moving beyond its original place which is what it was placed for. And it is permissible that it be in the sense of the passive participle from 'jaza bihi al-makan' with transitive to the object, because they made it traverse its original place, so it is 'majuz bihi'. But the first is more appropriate because the second requires the estimation of the word 'bihi' with it without the first. And it is said: it is from their saying 'ja'altu kadha majazan ila hajati' i.e. a path, that 'majaz' is a path to understanding its meaning. In it there is that the correspondence between it and haqiqah is lost, due to what passed that its name derives from its being fixed or established in its original place, not traversing it. As for what is said that it would necessitate that haqiqah also be called majaz because it is a path to imagining its meaning, it is refuted by the apparent difference between considering appropriateness in naming a thing with a name and considering the meaning in describing a thing with something. For the second must be universal [applied to all cases], unlike the first, for it is for preferring the name over others at the time of its placement for the meaning and showing that it is more appropriate and fitting for this meaning than others, not for the correctness of the expression. And it does not necessitate from that that everything in which that appropriateness is found should be called by this name, as it would necessitate from the second that this description be applied to everything in which that meaning is found. Hence the condition of remaining of the meaning is stipulated in descriptive expression but not in naming. So for example 'al-harith' if a man is described with it meaning he ploughs, then when ploughing ceases from him it is not correct to describe him with it, unlike if a man is named with it, for it is permissible to apply it to him even if the appropriateness considered at the time of naming has ceased. So memorize this, for it is beneficial for you in many places in what will come, God willing. Then it is not hidden that haqiqah and majaz in verbs and particles are only by considering their objects and by following them, as in 'nataqat al-hal' (the state spoke) and His statement: 'So the family of Pharaoh picked him up to be for them an enemy and grief.' Some researchers of the usuliyyun said: This is according to the materials; as for the form (hay'a), the verb may be described as haqiqah, majaz, mushtarak, and manqul, like the past tense for informative and creative, the present tense for present and future, and the command for obligation and recommendation. (Muhammad 'Ali) (90) Explanatory conjunctive for 'al-'urf al-khass' (Muhammad 'Ali) (91) If it is with the letter of attribution (ya' al-nisba) as in some copies, it is an example for the people of the specific convention; and if it is without it as in most copies, it is an example for the specific convention itself.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a refined definition of the term 'majaz' (metaphorical usage) in Arabic semantics, typical of a hashiya that critiques and expands upon earlier definitions. The author begins by stating the key condition: a preventing context (qarina mani'a) that blocks the intended literal meaning. This immediately disambiguates majaz from simple error or nonsense. The author then refines the definition by adding 'in the terminology of the user' to cover cases where a word is used in its original meaning but according to a different technical convention (e.g., legal vs. everyday usage). Next, the condition of 'a preventing context' excludes kinayah (allusion), where the metaphorical meaning is intended but the literal meaning is also possible. Some scholars add a further condition: the usage must be correct (sahih) to exclude mistakes like calling a book a 'horse' while pointing to it. The author notes that this condition is only needed if 'usage' includes both correct and incorrect. The author then explains why the original author (musannif) and the first commentator (muhashshi) did not spell out these conditions: they were not defining majaz but merely dividing it in a way that hints at its definition. The author provides the etymology of 'majaz': either as an active participle meaning 'that which traverses' (moving from its original place) or as a passive participle 'that which is made to traverse'. The first is preferred for linguistic economy. Another proposed etymology is that 'majaz' means 'path' (tariq), as a path to understanding the meaning. However, this would make haqiqah also a 'majaz' because it too is a path to meaning. The author refutes this by distinguishing between the basis for naming (appropriateness) and the basis for describing (meaning). Naming is not universal; it only indicates that the name is appropriate, not that every appropriate thing must be named thus. Describing, on the other hand, must apply universally as long as the meaning persists. The example of 'al-harith' illustrates: if you describe a man as 'harith' (ploughman), you must stop when he stops ploughing; if you name him 'al-Harith', the name remains even if he no longer ploughs. Finally, the author extends the discussion to verbs and particles, noting that haqiqah and majaz apply to them through their dependents, giving the Quranic example 'So the family of Pharaoh picked him up...' and quoting an usuli scholar who distinguishes between material (maddah) and form (hay'ah) in verbs.

Key Terms
مجاز
قرينة مانعة
كناية
حقيقة
غلط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author exclude kinayah from majaz?
A) Because kinayah is used in its literal meaning only.
B) Because kinayah allows the literal meaning to still be intended.
C) Because kinayah always has a preventing context.
D) Because kinayah is not a form of metaphorical usage.

Key Takeaway
Majaz is defined as correct usage in a non-literal meaning with a preventing context that eliminates the possibility of the original meaning, thereby excluding both kinayah and error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

(b) 'And the sky We built with hands' (51:47) – is this majaz? Provide reasons.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
6 min
Naming vs Describing: The Logical Distinction
Logical distinction between naming and describing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why naming (tasmiyah) does not require the continued existence of the quality that motivated the name, while describing (wasf) does, and apply this to examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Naming is a one-time convention based on appropriateness, not a universal predicate; describing requires the quality to persist.

In this lesson, we examine a subtle logical point made by the author: the difference between naming (tasmiyah) and describing (wasf). The example of 'al-harith' (the ploughman) illustrates that a name can endure after the reason for it disappears, while a description changes when the quality changes. This distinction has important implications for how we understand names in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the example of 'al-harith'
The word 'al-harith' means 'the ploughman'. If you describe a man as 'harith' (ploughman), the description is only true while he ploughs. If you name him 'al-Harith', the name remains even after he stops ploughing.
2
Identify the logical principle
Naming is based on appropriateness (munasaba) at the moment of naming, not on a universal condition. Describing is based on meaning (ma'na) that must be present for the description to hold universally.
The author says: 'The second must be universal [applied to all cases], unlike the first.'
3
Apply to the case of haqiqah and majaz
Some argued that if 'majaz' means 'path', then haqiqah should also be called 'majaz' because it is a path to meaning. The author refutes this by distinguishing naming from describing: 'majaz' is a name, not a description, so it does not need to apply universally.
The author's point: 'What is said that it would necessitate that haqiqah also be called majaz... is refuted by the apparent difference between considering appropriateness in naming... and considering the meaning in describing.'
4
Generalize the rule
Any name given based on a quality does not automatically apply to everything with that quality. Descriptions, however, must apply to everything that has the described quality. This is crucial in understanding the use of names in revelation.

Commentary
This passage is a refined definition of the term 'majaz' (metaphorical usage) in Arabic semantics, typical of a hashiya that critiques and expands upon earlier definitions. Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the name 'al-Harith' remain correct for a man even after he stops ploughing?
A) Because the meaning of 'harith' is always true for that man.
B) Because naming is based on a one-time appropriateness, not a permanent quality.
C) Because he might start ploughing again.
D) Because 'al-Harith' is a description, not a name.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between naming and describing resolves the apparent conflict that a term's etymology might suggest universal applicability; naming is a conventional act, not a logical universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Explain whether the title 'Sayyid' (master) for a descendant of the Prophet ﷺ is a name or a description. If it is a name, can it be used for someone who does not act like a sayyid? Use the author's distinction to support your answer.
